

{0}------------------------------------------------

# <span id="page-0-0"></span>Zero-Knowledge IOPPs for Constrained Interleaved Codes

Alessandro Chiesa alessandro.chiesa@epfl.ch EPFL

Giacomo Fenzi giacomo.fenzi@epfl.ch EPFL

Guy Weissenberg guy.weissenberg@epfl.ch EPFL

February 25, 2026

#### **Abstract**

Succinct arguments based on interactive oracle proofs (IOPs) have achieved remarkable efficiency improvements and are now widely adopted in applications. State-of-the-art IOPs involve protocols for testing proximity to constrained interleaved linear codes, and enjoy essentially optimal parameters. However, recent IOP constructions provide no privacy guarantees, which remain a must for many applications.

We present an IOP of proximity for testing constrained interleaved linear codes that achieves (honest-verifier) zero-knowledge, while incurring a negligible overhead compared to the (nonzero-knowledge) state of the art. In line with recent constructions, our construction satisfies round-by-round knowledge soundness with a straightline extractor and negligible error.

We propose a definition of (honest-verifier) zero-knowledge for interactive oracle reductions (IORs) that we prove is compatible with composition, and then obtain our result by constructing and modularly composing several lightweight zero-knowledge IORs. Our key technical contributions are a zero-knowledge sumcheck IOR and a zero-knowledge code-switching IOR that fit the strict efficiency requirements of our setting; these contributions and other technical complications entailed overcoming several challenges with new notions and protocols. Finally, along the way, we highlight the efficiency benefits of high-distance codes obtained from dispersers, which may be of independent interest.

**Keywords**: interactive oracle reductions; zero-knowledge; linear codes; dispersers

{1}------------------------------------------------

{2}------------------------------------------------

# <span id="page-2-0"></span>1 Introduction

Succinct non-interactive arguments (SNARGs) are short cryptographic proofs that admit a fast-verification procedure. They are widely deployed across applications in decentralized systems and cloud computing. We study hash-based SNARGs, a class of constructions notable for their transparent setup (the choice of hash function), post-quantum security (in the quantum random oracle model), fast provers (they avoid public-key cryptography), and more. Such SNARGs are, e.g., used for scaling distributed systems (e.g. [Sta; Pol; Ris; Suc]) and for anonymous credentials [Fs24].

Hash-based SNARGs are constructed via the BCS transformation [BCS16] from an interactive oracle proof (IOP), and a beautiful line of research investigates efficient IOP constructions. In this paper we investigate efficient constructions of (honest-verifier) zero-knowledge IOPs with the goal of obtaining efficient constructions of (malicious-verifier) zero-knowledge hash-based SNARGs.

The efficiency of code-agnostic IOPs. Many constructions of efficient IOPs rely on polynomial codes [AHIV17; BBHR19; BCRSVW19; COS20; ACFY24; HK24; Dia25; MZ25]. The limitations of constructions based on polynomial codes (e.g., superlinear encoding time) have motivated the study of IOPs based only on combinatorial transformations of general linear codes (e.g., tensoring, interleaving), which has achieved "code-agnostic" IOPs [BCGGHJ17; BCG20; RR20; RR22; NA25; BMMS25a; DMR25; DL25; ARR25; BFRW25; BMMS25b]. This is reminiscent of similar efforts for PCPs (the non-interactive counterparts of IOPs) where researchers have sought to decouple PCPs from the properties of the underlying codes in order to understand the minimal structure that enables PCPs and related tools [GS00; DR04; Din07; IKW09; DM11; Mei13; Mei12].

Recent works have shown that general linear codes and their interleaving are sufficient to construct state-of-the-art IOPs in terms of efficiency [BCGGHJ17; BCFW25; ACFY25; BCFRRZ25; NA25; BMMS25a], even outperforming constructions tailored to an error-correcting code (such as e.g. [ACFY24]). This has provided a class of highly configurable and efficient IOP constructions.

The cost of zero-knowledge. Zero-knowledge for hash-based SNARGs is increasingly in demand [Fs24; Mid; Goo; Lig], which requires the underlying IOP to satisfy (honest-verifier) zero-knowledge. Such an IOP is usually obtained by modifying an underlying (non-private) IOP. A key goal is minimizing the "zero-knowledge overhead", i.e., the cost incurred by the prover and verifier in using the zero-knowledge version of the protocol compared to its non-private equivalent.

The zero-knowledge overhead of concretely efficient IOPs is  $\geq 2 \times$  (in prover and verifier times), and they are specific to Reed–Solomon codes [RS60] (or other polynomial codes). This overhead deters developers and engineers from using the zero-knowledge version. Moreover, none of the state-of-the-art code-agnostic IOPs in the literature provide zero-knowledge.

Two notable works study zero-knowledge IOPs based on any "zero-knowledge" code (we define these shortly). [BCL22] obtains a tensor-code-based IOP with  $\geq 2\times$  overhead; the IOP is not concretely efficient partly due to heavy PCP machinery. [RW24] obtains a tensor-code-based IOP that reduces the overhead to 1 + o(1) relative to witness size, however only targets constant soundness. Both works achieve malicious-verifier zero-knowledge, while honest-verifier zero-knowledge suffices for achieving (malicious-verifier) zero-knowledge via the Fiat-Shamir transformation.

**Tensoring vs. interleaving.** There are two fundamental operations on codes that are used to create a modicum of structure for designing "code-agnostic" protocols.

• Code tensoring. The tensor product of codes  $C_1 \subseteq \Sigma^{m_1}$  and  $C_2 \subseteq \Sigma^{m_2}$  is the code  $C_1 \otimes C_2 \subseteq \Sigma^{m_1 \times m_2}$  of matrices  $u \in \Sigma^{m_1 \times m_2}$  such that each column of u is in  $C_1$  and each row of u is in  $C_2$ . The code  $C_1 \otimes C_2$  is a "nice" code if  $C_1$  and  $C_2$  both have (even general forms of) linear structure.

{3}------------------------------------------------

• Code interleaving. The K-interleaving of a code  $C \subseteq \Sigma^m$  is the code  $C^{\equiv K} \subseteq (\Sigma^K)^m$  obtained by "stacking" K codewords from C, that is, a word  $u \in (\Sigma^K)^m$  is in  $C^{\equiv K}$  if and only if there exist words  $v_1, \ldots, v_K \in \Sigma^m$  in C such that  $u(z) = (v_1(z), \ldots, v_K(z)) \in \Sigma^K$  for every  $z \in [m]$ .

Both operations lead to linear-time IOP provers by way of linear-time encodable codes, but they offer different tradeoffs. Tensoring creates key structures for local testability and tensor sumchecks [Mei13; RR20; BCG20; RR22; BCL22; RW24; BFRW25; BMMS25b], enabling for example query-optimal IOPs; this comes with the cost of worse rate and distance compared to the original code as well as a less efficient encoding. Interleaving also enables key structures for alternative sumchecks and code-switching [AHIV17; BBHV22; ACFY25; NA25; BMMS25a], while preserving rate and distance at the cost of increasing the alphabet size (which precludes query-optimal IOPs); on the other hand, interleaving leads to better encoding efficiency (including with small space [BBHV22]) and also works for every code, enabling the most freedom in code choice for a protocol.

#### <span id="page-3-0"></span>1.1 Our results

We present a concretely-efficient HVZK IOPP for constrained interleaved codes, as well as a corresponding HVZK IOP for R1CS. We match the efficiency of state-of-the-art (non-private) IOPs up to low-order factors, obtaining a zero-knowledge overhead of 1 + o(1); moreover, we achieve round-by-round straightline extraction for every code by relying on mutual correlated agreement.

We elaborate on our results below. We begin by recalling the notions of zero-knowledge encodings and constrained interleaved codes, which we need to state our results.

**ZK** encodings. We use zero-knowledge encodings for codes, also known as zero-knowledge codes [ISVW13; BCGV16; BBHR19; BCL22; RW24]. For a finite field  $\mathbb{F}$ , we say that a code  $\mathcal{C} \subseteq \Sigma^m$  is  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive if the alphabet  $\Sigma$  is an  $\mathbb{F}$ -linear vector space (e.g.,  $\Sigma = \mathbb{F}^{\iota}$  for some  $\iota \in \mathbb{N}$ ); when  $\Sigma = \mathbb{F}$  this becomes an  $\mathbb{F}$ -linear code in the common sense. For message length  $\ell$  and randomness length r, we say that  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}} : \mathbb{F}^{\ell} \times \mathbb{F}^{r} \to \Sigma^{m}$  is a t-query zero-knowledge encoding for  $\mathcal{C}$  if: (i) the image of  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}$  is in  $\mathcal{C}$  (i.e.,  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(\mathbb{F}^{\ell}, \mathbb{F}^{r}) \subseteq \mathcal{C}$ ); and (ii) for every message  $\mathsf{msg} \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell}$  and index subset  $S \subseteq [m]$  with  $|S| \leq t$ , the distribution  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(\mathsf{msg}, r \leftarrow \mathbb{F}^{r})[S]$  can be simulated efficiently, up to some error  $\zeta$ , given only S (but not  $\mathsf{msg}$ ). Intuitively, a randomized encoding of a message yields multiple possible encodings and the view of a certain number of locations is independent of the message.

Our target: constrained interleaved codes with ZK encodings. We seek to construct concretely-efficient HVZK IOPPs for relations that involve interleaved codes whose underlying messages are subject to succinct linear constraints. This is in line with state-of-the-art protocols that consider proximity testing to codes with linear constraints [ACFY25; BMNW25; BCFW25], which enables significantly more efficient (and simpler) reductions from NP compared to merely relying on proximity tests to unconstrained codes. Informally, given a zero-knowledge encoding  $Enc_{\mathcal{C}}$  for a code  $\mathcal{C}$  we consider the relation of linear constraints defined as follows:

<span id="page-3-1"></span>
$$\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},T}^{\mathsf{lin}} \coloneqq \left\{ \begin{pmatrix} \mathbb{X} = (\mu, \langle \boldsymbol{v} \rangle) \in \mathbb{F} \times \mathbb{F}^{\log \ell}, \\ \mathbb{Y} = f \in \Sigma^{m}, \\ \mathbb{W} = (\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{r}) \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell} \times \mathbb{F}^{r} \end{pmatrix} \middle| \begin{array}{c} f = \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{r}) \\ \wedge \langle \boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{v} \rangle = \mu \end{array} \right\}.$$
(1)

Above  $\langle \boldsymbol{v} \rangle$  is some "succinct" description of the vector  $\boldsymbol{v} \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell}$  from which  $\boldsymbol{v}$  can be computed in time T and "k-folds of  $\boldsymbol{v}$ " (which we define later) can be computed in time  $T^{(k)}$ . (In fact  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},T}^{\mathsf{lin}}$  is a simplification of the relation we use; in Section 2.4 we discuss and motivate a refined variant of it.)

{4}------------------------------------------------

**HVZK IOPP for constrained interleaved codes.** Our first result is an honest-verifier zero-knowledge IOP of proximity for the linear constraint relation  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^k},T}^{\mathsf{lin}}$  (Equation 1) for a given interleaving parameter k. This IOPP is in the "sublinear regime" and is the result of a single reduction step of a more general HVZK code-switching technique that we discuss further below.

<span id="page-4-1"></span>**Theorem 1** (informal). Let  $C \subseteq \Sigma^{m_C}$  be an  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive code with a  $t_C$ -query zero-knowledge encoding  $\operatorname{Enc}_C \colon \mathbb{F}^{\ell_C} \times \mathbb{F}^{r_C} \to \Sigma^{m_C}$  with error  $\zeta_C$  and encoding time  $\tau_C$ . Fix an interleaving parameter  $k \in \mathbb{N}$ , security parameter  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , and proximity parameter  $\delta \in (0,1)$ ; let  $t := O(\frac{\lambda}{-\log(1-\delta)})$ . We construct a O(k)-round IOPP for  $\mathcal{R}_{C^{\equiv 2^k},T}^{\text{lin}}$  with the following features.

- The prover sends  $O(m_C) + \widetilde{O}(k \cdot \lambda)$  alphabet symbols, and the verifier makes t queries over the alphabet  $\Sigma^{2^k}$ .
- The prover time is  $O(T+2^k \cdot \ell_{\mathcal{C}} + \tau_{\mathcal{C}}) + \widetilde{O}(k \cdot \lambda)$  and verifier time is  $O(T^{(k)} + 2^k \cdot t + \tau_{\mathcal{C}}) + \widetilde{O}(k \cdot \lambda)$ .
- The protocol has round-by-round knowledge soundness error at most  $2^{-\lambda}$  assuming  $\mathbb{F}$  is large enough with respect to  $\mathcal{C}$ 's mutual correlated agreement radius at distance  $\delta$ .
- If  $t_{\mathcal{C}} \geq t$  the protocol is honest-verifier zero-knowledge with error  $\zeta_{\mathcal{C}}$ . The notation  $\widetilde{O}(\cdot)$  hides poly(log  $\lambda$ ) factors (here and elsewhere in this introduction).

The prover sends  $O(m_{\mathcal{C}}) + \widetilde{O}(k \cdot \lambda)$  alphabet symbols, while the block length of  $\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^k}$  is  $N = 2^k \cdot m_{\mathcal{C}}$  alphabet symbols. For common settings of the interleaving parameter k (say  $\frac{\log N}{2}$  or  $\log \log N$ ), the prover sends  $O(\frac{m_{\mathcal{C}}}{N}) \cdot N + \widetilde{O}(\frac{k \cdot \lambda}{N}) \cdot N = o(1) \cdot N$  alphabet symbols. As this includes the costs arising from the addition of HVZK, the zero-knowledge overhead is o(1). A notable setting of parameters for Theorem 1 is the *sublinear regime*, achieved when  $2^k \approx m_{\mathcal{C}}$ . In this setting, the proof length is  $\approx O(\sqrt{N})$  alphabet symbols, and verifier time is sublinear.

Theorem 1 gives the first IOPP for constrained interleaved general codes with list-decoding soundness and 1+o(1) zero-knowledge overhead in the *sublinear verifier* regime. Prior works either show HVZK IOPPs for interleaved Reed–Solomon codes in the unique decoding regime [AHIV17], or give IOPPs for interleaved general codes in the unique decoding regime [BCG20; GLSTW23] without providing zero-knowledge. None of these prior works consider constrained code testing relations. Theorem 1 improves upon these works, simultaneously achieving the desirable properties.

**HVZK code switching.** We obtain honest-verifier zero-knowledge IOPPs beyond the sublinear-verifier regime by providing an *HVZK code-switching* IOR from an interleaved code to another code (that, in particular, can itself be interleaved). Code-switching loosely refers to techniques for reducing a testing problem for a code to a testing problem for another *smaller* code, first introduced in the setting of tensor codes [RR20; RR22].

<span id="page-4-0"></span>**Theorem 2** (informal, code switching with HVZK). For  $b \in \{1, 2\}$ , let  $C_b \subseteq \Sigma^{m_{C_b}}$  be an  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive code that has a  $t_{C_b}$ -query zero-knowledge encoding  $\mathsf{Enc}_{C_b} \colon \mathbb{F}^{\ell_{C_b}} \times \mathbb{F}^{r_{C_b}} \to \Sigma^{m_{C_b}}$  with error  $\zeta_{C_b}$  and encoding time  $\tau_{C_b}$ . Fix an interleaving parameter  $k \in \mathbb{N}$ , security parameter  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , and proximity parameter  $\delta \in (0,1)$ ; let  $t := O(-\frac{\lambda}{\log(1-\delta)})$ . We construct a O(k)-round IOR of proximity from  $\mathcal{R}^{\mathsf{lin}}_{C_1^{=2^k},T_1}$  to  $\mathcal{R}^{\mathsf{lin}}_{C_2,T_2}$ , where  $T_2 = O(T_1 + T_{C_2})$  and  $T_{C_2}$  is a quantity determined by  $C_2$ 's generator matrix, with the following features.

- The prover sends  $O(m_{\mathcal{C}_2}) + \widetilde{O}(k \cdot \lambda)$  alphabet symbols, and the verifier makes t queries over alphabet  $\Sigma^{2^k}$ .
- The prover time is  $O(2^k \cdot \ell_{\mathcal{C}_1} + \tau_{\mathcal{C}_2}) + \widetilde{O}(k \cdot \lambda)$  and the verifier time is  $O(2^k \cdot t) + \widetilde{O}(k \cdot \lambda)$ .

{5}------------------------------------------------

- The protocol has round-by-round knowledge soundness error at most  $2^{-\lambda}$  assuming  $\mathbb{F}$  is large enough with respect to  $\mathcal{C}_1$ 's and  $\mathcal{C}_2$ 's mutual correlated agreement errors at distance  $\delta$ .
- The protocol is honest-verifier zero-knowledge with error  $\zeta_{C_2}$  against distinguishers that perform at most  $t_{C_2}$  queries.

We consider a stronger notion of HVZK for IORs in Theorem 1, as we require an HVZK notion that is compatible with composition of IOR (accounting for later queries by algorithms we refer to as distinguishers on the output implicit instance). We elaborate on the "correct" HVZK notion for IORs in Section 2.3. Additionally, the fixed quantity  $T_{c_2}$  refers to the code-switching friendliness of the code, and we elaborate on this property in Section 2.6. Finally, similarly to Theorem 1, for common settings of the interleaving parameter k the zero-knowledge overhead is o(1).

Polylogarithmic HVZK IOPP for constrained interleaved codes. Theorem 2 and the building blocks of Theorem 1 directly yield HVZK variants of WHIR [ACFY25] and Ligerito [NA25], as follows. Use the parameter settings in [NA25] (implicit in [ACFY25]) to apply Theorem 2 in sequence and conclude with Theorem 1. Moreover, Theorem 2 also improves upon [BCL22], who achieve HVZK IOPP for general linear codes but with O(1) overhead and unique decoding soundness, while our result achieves o(1) overhead and list decoding soundness.

In particular, instantiating this recipe with Reed-Solomon code yields the following corollary.

<span id="page-5-0"></span>Corollary 3. Let  $C^{\equiv 2^k}$  be an interleaved Reed-Solomon code with message length  $\ell$ , evaluation domain size m, and with an  $O(\lambda)$ -query zero-knowledge encoding, where  $k = O(\log \log \ell)$ . There exists an IOPP for  $\mathcal{R}^{\text{lin}}_{C\equiv 2^k,T}$  with the following features.

- The prover sends  $O(m) + \widetilde{O}(\lambda)$  alphabet symbols, and the verifier makes  $O(\lambda)$  queries over alphabet  $\mathbb{F}^{\log \ell}$ .
- The prover time is  $O(T + m \cdot \log m + \frac{\lambda \cdot \ell}{\log \ell}) + \widetilde{O}(\lambda)$  and the verifier time is  $O(T^{(\log \ell)} + \lambda \cdot \log \ell) + \widetilde{O}(\lambda)$ .
- The protocol has round-by-round knowledge soundness error at most  $2^{-\lambda}$ .
- The protocol is perfect honest-verifier zero-knowledge.

HVZK IOR from NP to constrained code testing. As an additional contribution, we provide a reduction from the NP-complete Rank-1-Constraint-Satisfaction (R1CS) problem to proximity testing of  $\mathcal{R}^{\text{lin}}_{\mathcal{C}\equiv^{2^k},T}$ . The reduction incurs o(1) overhead over sending a single (interleaved) codeword, illustrating that the efficiency characteristics of the proximity test are also achievable in the reduction from an NP problem. The reduction is based on the same zero-knowledge sumcheck (see Section 2.5), which is an essential ingredient in the proofs of Theorem 1 and Theorem 2, and adapts the  $\Sigma$ -IOP for R1CS in [ACFY25]. In addition, we use a sublinear masking technique (similar to the one we use in our zero-knowledge sumcheck) to mask the R1CS matrix-vector product. Prior reductions from R1CS [BCL22] incur O(1) overhead by sending full masking oracles.

Our reduction can, in particular, be instantiated with the interleaving of a linear-time-encodable code (with zero-knowledge encodings, as shown in [BCL22]) and, together with our proximity test, leads to an HVZK IOR from R1CS to testing proximity to a code, with a linear-time prover, list-decoding soundness, and o(1) overhead. We note that our size comparison is against non-zero-knowledge protocols that use this style of reduction ("reduce R1CS to constraint code-testing"), e.g. [ACFY24; ACFY25; MZ25]. This improves upon the state-of-the-art for linear-time provers from [BCL22], which only achieves unique decoding soundness and constant overhead.

Composing the IOR from NP with Theorem 1 (or its polylogarithmic-verifier version that we provide in Theorem 4 in Section 2.7) achieves an HVZK IOP for NP with desirable efficiency

{6}------------------------------------------------

properties. We consider an example application of such efficiency improvements: [\[Fs24\]](#page-79-0) develop an anonymous credential solution using the sublinear hash-based zkSNARK in [\[AHIV17\]](#page-76-2), which is based on zero-knowledge IOPs with zero-knowledge overhead ≥ 2× (and the corresponding nonprivate IOPs are no longer state of the art); our protocols can be used as a drop-in replacement to avoid this overhead. The effect is further amplified by the fact that we measure our overhead against *state-of-the-art* non-private IOPs.

**Memory efficiency.** While not an explicit focus of our work, [Theorem 1](#page-4-1) is plausibly amenable to space efficient streaming proving. The memory efficiency of the prover is concretely dominated by the space-efficiency of the encoding of the interleaved code and by the sumcheck. Crucially, an encoding of an interleaved code C <sup>≡</sup>*<sup>K</sup>* can be performed in space equivalent to that required for the encoding of a message via the *base code* C, which can be significantly smaller. This fact was already leveraged in [\[BBHV22\]](#page-76-7) to achieve efficient streaming provers, for the specific case when C is a Reed–Solomon code. The sumcheck protocol can also be implemented in a time and space efficient manner (see e.g. [\[CTY11;](#page-78-8) [VSBW13;](#page-80-14) [BCFFMMZ25;](#page-76-8) [NTZ25\]](#page-80-15)), and plausibly the techniques can be adapted to achieve memory efficiency for our protocol.

**On the choice of codes.** Any F-additive code with an appropriate zero-knowledge encoding can be used in [Theorem 1](#page-4-1) and [Theorem 2.](#page-4-0) These theorems use auxiliary *internal* smaller codes with zero-knowledge encoding, distinct from the *main* code being tested (exposed in the informal theorem statements). When setting the parameters for these theorems, we set the internal codes to be Reed–Solomon codes. Yet, the generality of our constructions (and full theorem statements) allows us to consider *other* codes for the codes being tested and for the internal codes.

We identify a class of codes that represents a midpoint between two popular choices of codes: (i) Reed–Solomon codes (an MDS code that is not linear-time encodable), and (ii) practical lineartime encodable codes. Specifically, we describe a class of codes obtained via *distance amplification based on dispersers*, and we argue that these codes achieve encoding time that approaches that of linear-time encodable MDS codes, but over a larger alphabet. We elaborate on this in [Section 2.8.](#page-23-0)

{7}------------------------------------------------

# <span id="page-7-0"></span>2 Technical overview

We summarize the main ideas behind our results via an extended technical overview; the technical sections are long as we invested much effort to provide formal statements, constructions, and proofs.

- In Section 2.1 we review basic notions used in the technical overview: zero-knowledge codes, interactive oracle reductions, and round-by-round knowledge soundness.
- In Section 2.2 we describe a *non-succinct* HVZK IOPP for linear constraints, serving as a warmup to the techniques we use and as a baseline for our constructions (details are in Section 7).
- In Section 2.3 we present a new definition of HVZK for IORs that is compatible with sequential composition of IORs (details are in Section 4).
- In Section 2.4 we discuss a new *committed sumcheck relation*, which allows us to ensure zero-knowledge and efficient verifiers in our protocols.
- In Section 2.5 we present an **HVZK sumcheck reduction** from testing the interleaving of a base code to testing the base code itself (details are in Section 6), expressed via the aforementioned committed sumcheck relation. This is a key technical ingredient of our paper.
- In Section 2.6 we present an **HVZK code switching protocol** (details are in Section 10). This is the other key technical ingredient of our paper.
- In Section 2.7 we sketch how we prove our main theorems, by combining the basic HVZK IOPP, the sumcheck reduction, and code switching (via our HVZK IOR composition theorem).
- In Section 2.8 we propose the use of high-distance codes constructed via distance amplification for improved efficiency, in encoding time and in argument size. These codes are compatible with our constructions, and we deem them of independent interest for other probabilistic proofs.

#### <span id="page-7-1"></span>2.1 Basic notions

We review basic notions used in this extended technical overview; full definitions appear in Section 3. Below  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive codes refers to codes  $\mathcal{C} \subseteq \Sigma^m$  whose alphabet  $\Sigma$  is an  $\mathbb{F}$ -linear vector space (e.g.,  $\Sigma = \mathbb{F}^{\iota}$  for some  $\iota \in \mathbb{N}$ ); when the alphabet  $\Sigma = \mathbb{F}$  this becomes a linear code in the usual sense.

**ZK codes.** A code  $C \subseteq \Sigma^m$  has a t-query zero-knowledge encoding  $\mathsf{Enc}_C \colon \mathbb{F}^\ell \times \mathbb{F}^r \to \Sigma^m$  if the image of  $\mathsf{Enc}_C$  is in C (i.e.,  $\mathsf{Enc}_C(\mathbb{F}^\ell, \mathbb{F}^r) \subseteq C$ ) and, for every message  $\mathsf{msg} \in \mathbb{F}^\ell$  and index subset  $S \subseteq [m]$  with  $|S| \leq t$ , the distribution  $\mathsf{Enc}_C(\mathsf{msg}, r \leftarrow \mathbb{F}^r)[S]$  can be simulated efficiently, up to some error, given only S. Zero-knowledge codes are key ingredients in prior work on zero-knowledge IOPs (e.g. [ISVW13; BCL22; RW24]), and all codes in this work have zero-knowledge encodings. A concrete example one can keep in mind is the Reed-Solomon code, where encoding a message extended with randomness in  $r \in \mathbb{F}^t$  achieves perfect t-query zero-knowledge (see Proposition 3.19). Many other zero-knowledge codes are known, including some with linear-time encoding algorithms [BCL22].

IORs. Recent constructions of hash-based succinct arguments and accumulation schemes are obtained via a modular approach based on *interactive oracle reductions* (IORs) [BCGGRS19; BMNW25]. Informally, an IOR is an interactive oracle protocol in which the verifier, rather than outputting a decision bit, either rejects or outputs an instance for a target relation; in addition, instances are split into two parts, an *explicit instance* part that the verifier receives as input and an *implicit instance* part that the verifier receives as an oracle to query.

In more detail, an IOR (of proximity) from relation  $\mathcal{R}$  to  $\mathcal{R}'$  works as follows. The prover **P** receives as input  $(x, y, w) \in \mathcal{R}$ , whereas the verifier **V** receives as (explicit) input x and query access to y. After interacting, **V** either rejects or outputs a new explicit instance x' and implicit instance

{8}------------------------------------------------

y', and (in the honest case) **P** outputs a new witness w' such that  $(z', y', w') \in \mathcal{R}'$ . We say that IOR has (perfect) completeness if for every  $(z, y, w) \in \mathcal{R}$  it always holds that  $(z', y', w') \in \mathcal{R}'$ .

RBR security. We target the strong notion of round-by-round security [CCHLRR18; CMS19; CY24; BMNW25; BCFW25] to ensure that our protocols are suitable to construct non-interactive succinct arguments (in the random oracle model). Informally, this amounts to establishing, for each round, a round error that upper bounds the probability of transitioning, in that round, from a bad state for the attacker ("the statement is false") into a good state ("the next statement is true"). Specifically, we target the straightline RBR knowledge soundness notion for IORs in [BCFW25], which is suitable for code-agnostic protocols such as ours. The notion essentially states that an IOR from  $\mathcal{R}$  to  $\mathcal{R}'$  can be viewed as a sequence of one-round reductions each satisfying straightline knowledge soundness, which requires providing for each round a knowledge extractor and bounding its extraction error. Finally, since both  $\mathcal{R}$  and  $\mathcal{R}'$  involve implicit instances (V receives query access to an implicit instance y and outputs an implicit instance y') the extraction errors are a function of proximity parameters  $\delta$  for  $\mathcal{R}$  ("y is  $\delta$ -far from  $\mathcal{R}$ ") and  $\delta'$  for  $\mathcal{R}'$  ("y' is  $\delta'$ -close to  $\mathcal{R}'$ ").

List decoding and mutual correlated agreement. The RBR knowledge soundness errors that we achieve for our protocols depend, as in prior work, on several properties of the given code C: distance, list-decoding size, and mutual correlated agreement error. We review the latter two.

- We denote the worst-case list size of a code  $\mathcal{C}$  at distance  $\delta \in (0,1)$  as  $|\Lambda(\mathcal{C},\delta)| := \max_f |\Lambda(\mathcal{C},f,\delta)|$  where  $\Lambda(\mathcal{C},f,\delta) := \{u \in \mathcal{C} : \Delta(u,f) \leq \delta\}$  is the set of codewords in  $\mathcal{C}$  at relative distance  $\leq \delta$  from f. Hence  $\mathcal{C}$ 's list decoding size captures the maximum number of codewords in the Hamming ball of a certain radius centered at any codeword in  $\mathcal{C}$ .
- Mutual correlated agreement (MCA) [ACFY25] is a strong notion of distance preservation. Informally, it bounds the probability that the random combination  $f_1 + \gamma \cdot f_2$  is close to  $\mathcal{C}$  when  $f_1, f_2$  are not individually close to  $\mathcal{C}$ . We let  $\epsilon_{mca}(\mathcal{C}, \delta)$  be the mutual correlated agreement error of  $\mathcal{C}$  at distance  $\delta \in (0, 1)$  (see Definition 3.14). In this overview we also consider the random combination  $(1 \gamma) \cdot f_1 + \gamma \cdot f_2$ , and use the same notation because the error is the same.

## <span id="page-8-0"></span>2.2 Warmup: a simple zero-knowledge IOPP for constrained codes

We describe a simple honest-verifier zero-knowledge IOPP for linear constraints that works for any given zero-knowledge  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive code. The protocol illustrates IOP-native ideas commonly used to achieve zero-knowledge, incurring a  $2\times$  overhead. This serves as a simple concrete example to understand before we describe more efficient protocols; moreover, variants of this protocol will serve as a "base case" on small instances in our protocols.

An initial relation. Let  $\mathcal{C} \subseteq \Sigma^m$  be an  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive code with a zero-knowledge encoding  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}} \colon \mathbb{F}^\ell \times \mathbb{F}^r \to \Sigma^m$ . We define a linear constraint relation  $\bar{\mathcal{R}}_{\mathcal{C}}$ : (a) the explicit instance  $\mathbb{X}$  contains a target  $\mu \in \mathbb{F}$  and a coefficient vector  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}^\ell$ , (b) the implicit instance  $\mathbb{X}$  is an oracle  $f \in \Sigma^m$ , and (c) the witness is a message  $\mathbf{f} \in \mathbb{F}^\ell$  and randomness  $\mathbf{r} \in \mathbb{F}^r$  such that  $f = \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(\mathbf{f}, \mathbf{r})$  and  $\langle \mathbf{f}, \mathbf{v} \rangle = \mu$ . Namely:

<span id="page-8-1"></span>
$$\bar{\mathcal{R}}_{\mathcal{C}} \coloneqq \left\{ \begin{pmatrix} \mathbf{x} = (\mu, \mathbf{v}) \in \mathbb{F} \times \mathbb{F}^{\ell}, \\ \mathbf{y} = f \in \Sigma^{m}, \\ \mathbf{w} = (\mathbf{f}, \mathbf{r}) \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell} \times \mathbb{F}^{r} \end{pmatrix} \middle| \begin{array}{c} f = \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(\mathbf{f}, \mathbf{r}) \\ \wedge \langle \mathbf{f}, \mathbf{v} \rangle = \mu \end{array} \right\}.$$
 (2)

As explained in Section 2.4,  $\bar{\mathcal{R}}_{\mathcal{C}}$  is a simplification of the linear constraints relation that we use.

**Recall: HVZK for IOPPs.** We recall the standard definition of HVZK for interactive oracle proofs of proximity [BCFGRS17], which are for ternary relations  $\mathcal{R}$  consisting of tuples (x, y, w)

{9}------------------------------------------------

where **x** is the explicit instance, **y** is the implicit instance, and **w** is the witness. The key feature of the definition is that the simulator has oracle access to the implicit instance **y** since the honest verifier also does, and we account for the query complexity of the simulator to **y**.

<span id="page-9-1"></span>**Definition 2.1** (informal)**.** *Let* IOP = (**P***,* **V**) *be an IOPP for a ternary relation* R*. The* **view** *of* (**P***,* **V**) *on* (**x***,* **y***,***w**) ∈ R *is the random variable* View(**P***,* **V***,* **x***,* **y***,* **w**) *that consists of* **V***'s randomness and the answers to* **V***'s queries in an interaction between* **P**(**x***,* **y***,* **w**) *and* **V<sup>y</sup>** (**x**)*.* IOP *has* **honest-verifier zero-knowledge with error** *ζ* **and query complexity** b *if there exists a polynomial-time simulator* **S** *such that the random variables* View(**P***,* **V***,* **x***,* **y***,***w**) *and* **S y** (**x**) *are ζ-close (in statistical distance) and* **S y** (**x**) *makes at most* b *queries to* **y***.*

**A simple protocol for linear constraints.** We sketch a *non-succinct* HVZK IOPP for R¯ C.

<span id="page-9-0"></span>**Construction 2.2.** The verifier receives as explicit input **x** = (*µ, v*) ∈ F×F *<sup>ℓ</sup>* and as implicit input **y** = *f* ∈ Σ *<sup>m</sup>*. The prover receives as input a message *f* ∈ F *<sup>ℓ</sup>* and randomness *r* ∈ F *r* such that *f* = EncC(*f, r*) and ⟨*f, v*⟩ = *µ*.

- 1. The prover samples *g* ∈ F *<sup>ℓ</sup>* and *r* ′ ∈ F *r* , and sends *g* := EncC(*g, r* ′ ) ∈ Σ *<sup>m</sup>* and *µ*˜ := ⟨*g, v*⟩ ∈ F.
- 2. The verifier samples and sends *ε* ← F.
- 3. The prover sends *h* := *g* + *ε* · *f* ∈ F *<sup>ℓ</sup>* and *r* ∗ := *r* ′ + *ε* · *r* ∈ F *r* .
- 4. The verifier samples *x*1*, . . . , x<sup>t</sup>* ← [*m*], computes *h* := EncC(*h, r* ∗ ) ∈ Σ *<sup>m</sup>*, and checks that:
  - ⟨*h, v*⟩ = ˜*µ* + *ε* · *µ*.
  - for *i* ∈ [*t*], *h*(*xi*) = *g*(*xi*) + *ε* · *f*(*xi*) (by querying *f* and *g* at *x*1*, . . . , xt*). Recall that *h*(*xi*)*, g*(*xi*)*, f*(*xi*) ∈ Σ and they are compared as alphabet symbols.

Completeness of [Construction 2.2](#page-9-0) follows by linearity of EncC, and its (straightline) round-byround knowledge soundness follows via an analysis similar to prior work (e.g., [\[BCFW25\]](#page-77-6)).

**Lemma 2.3.** *For every δ* ∈ (0*,* 1)*, [Construction 7.2](#page-42-1) has round-by-round knowledge soundness with errors* (*ϵ*mca(C*, δ*) + <sup>|</sup>Λ(C≡<sup>2</sup> *,δ*)| |F| *,* (1 − *δ*) *t* )*.*

We wish to highlight the privacy properties of [Construction 2.2,](#page-9-0) per the following lemma.

**Lemma 2.4.** *If* Enc<sup>C</sup> *is a t-query zero-knowledge encoding for* C *with error ζ, then [Construction 2.2](#page-9-0) is an honest-verifier zero-knowledge IOPP for* R¯ <sup>C</sup> *with error ζ and query complexity* b = *t.*

The intuition for the lemma is that if the encoding Enc<sup>C</sup> is *t*-query zero-knowledge then the queries of the verifier can be simulated efficiently, so they do not leak any information about the underlying message. The remaining information sent by the prover can also be efficiently simulated.

**Takeaway.** Informally, the overhead to achieve HVZK in [Construction 2.2](#page-9-0) is 2× (the prover sends *g* of the same size as *f*), and we will see how to achieve IOPPs for the same task with less overhead. Even so, [Construction 2.2](#page-9-0) will play the role of a "base case" on small instances in our protocols, so we describe and analyze (a generalization of) it in [Section 7.](#page-42-0) Note also that [Construction 2.2](#page-9-0) is a "minimal example" of an IOP-native HVZK protocol, as it relies on both interaction and the verifier's ability to query oracles; more restricted models such as IPs and PCPs typically require other techniques to achieve HVZK (or have significant limitations in their HVZK capabilities).

{10}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-10-0"></span>2.3 Composition for honest-verifier zero-knowledge IORs

We explain how prior (honest-verifier) zero-knowledge definitions of IOPs (and IOPPs), when naively adapted to IORs, do not preserve zero knowledge, precluding a modular treatment of zero knowledge. We address this via a **new definition (and composition theorem) for IORs**. This enables constructing zero-knowledge IOPs by modularly composing simple zero-knowledge IORs.

**Definition 2.1 is insufficient for IORs.** The notion of HVZK for IOPPs in Definition 2.1 can be straightforwardly adapted to IORs. However, this notion is insufficient for composing IORs, as we now explain via an (artificial) example. Let  $\mathcal{R}$  be a binary relation of instance-witness pairs  $(\mathfrak{x}, \mathfrak{w})$ , and let  $\mathcal{R}'$  be the ternary relation  $\{(\mathfrak{x}', \mathfrak{y}', \mathfrak{w}' = \bot) : (\mathfrak{x}', \mathfrak{y}') \in \mathcal{R}\}$ . Consider two IORs.

- In  $IOR_1$ , the prover sends w as an oracle to the verifier; the verifier makes no queries and then outputs the new instance (x', y') := (x, w) for  $\mathcal{R}'$ . This IOR from  $\mathcal{R}$  to  $\mathcal{R}'$  has perfect completeness and soundness, and it is honest verifier zero-knowledge: the view of the verifier is empty (the verifier performs no queries), so it can be efficiently (and perfectly) simulated.
- In  $IOR_2$ , the prover does nothing; the verifier queries the implicit instance y' entirely and then checks that  $(x', y') \in \mathcal{R}$ . This is an IOPP for  $\mathcal{R}'$  with perfect completeness and soundness, and it is honest verifier zero-knowledge: the simulator in Definition 2.1 can query the entire implicit instance, so it can simulate the view of the honest verifier.

The composition of  $IOR_1$  and  $IOR_2$  yields a trivial IOP for  $\mathcal{R}$ . Note that  $IOR_1$  and  $IOR_2$  each satisfy Definition 2.1, yet the prover in their composition sends the witness to the verifier, which is not honest-verifier zero-knowledge (unless  $\mathcal{R}$  is trivial). We conclude that Definition 2.1 is not strong enough to capture the notion of HVZK necessary for composing IORs (while preserving HVZK).

Variants of  $IOR_1$  where the prover sends w encoded with a zero-knowledge code will be central to our constructions. The problem with Definition 2.1 is that it does not account for the way in which the implicit instance y' output by  $IOR_1$  is queried in  $IOR_2$ .

**Defining HVZK for IORs.** We strengthen Definition 2.1 by requiring the simulator to additionally be responsible for simulating all future queries to the implicit instance y' output by the verifier. We model these queries via an oracle algorithm  $\mathcal{D}$  that we call a *distinguisher*, because  $\mathcal{D}$  captures the additional distinguishing opportunities arising from querying y'.

<span id="page-10-1"></span>**Definition 2.5** (informal). Let IOR = (**P**, **V**) be an IOR from  $\mathcal{R}$  to  $\mathcal{R}'$ . The **view** of IOR on  $(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y}, \mathbf{w}) \in \mathcal{R}$  with respect to an oracle algorithm  $\mathcal{D}$  is the random variable View(**P**, **V**,  $\mathcal{D}, \mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y}, \mathbf{w}$ ) that consists of **V**'s and  $\mathcal{D}$ 's randomnesses, the answers to **V**'s and  $\mathcal{D}$ 's queries, and  $\mathcal{D}$ 's output out in this experiment:  $\mathbf{P}(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y}, \mathbf{w})$  and  $\mathbf{V}^{\mathbf{y}}(\mathbf{x})$  interact yielding output  $(\mathbf{x}', \mathbf{y}', \mathbf{w}')$ , and then  $\mathcal{D}^{\mathbf{y}'}(\mathbf{x}')$  is run to produce out. IOR has **honest-verifier zero-knowledge with error**  $\zeta$  **and query complexity** b **with respect to**  $\mathcal{D}$  if there exists a polynomial time simulator **S** such that the random variables  $\mathbf{View}(\mathbf{P}, \mathbf{V}, \mathcal{D}, \mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y}, \mathbf{w})$  and  $\mathbf{S}^{\mathbf{y}, \mathcal{D}}(\mathbf{x})$  are  $\zeta$ -close (in statistical distance) and  $\mathbf{S}^{\mathbf{y}, \mathcal{D}}(\mathbf{x})$  makes at most b queries to  $\mathbf{y}$ .

Above, the simulator **S** has black-box access to  $\mathcal{D}$  so, in particular, can run  $\mathcal{D}(\mathbf{z}')$  to deduce simulated answers for its queries to  $\mathbf{y}'$ . Note that Definition 2.5 collapses to Definition 2.1 when  $\mathcal{D}$  makes no queries to  $\mathbf{y}'$  (i.e.,  $\mathcal{D}$  is trivial).

IOR<sub>1</sub> is honest-verifier zero-knowledge with respect to the trivial distinguisher  $\mathcal{D}^{y'}(z') = \bot$ , but does not satisfy Definition 2.5 for any non-trivial distinguisher  $\mathcal{D}^{y'}(z')$  that queries its implicit

{11}------------------------------------------------

instance y', and in particular does not satisfy honest-verifier zero-knowledge with respect to the simulator for  $IOR_2$  (unless the relation  $\mathcal{R}$  is trivial).

The oracle algorithm  $\mathcal{D}$  enables us to formulate a composition theorem, which we discuss next. **HVZK composition theorem.** We informally state a composition theorem for HVZK IORs.

<span id="page-11-1"></span>**Lemma 2.6** (informal). Let  $IOR_1$  be an IOR from  $\mathcal{R}_1$  to  $\mathcal{R}_2$  and  $IOR_2$  be an IOR from  $\mathcal{R}_2$  to  $\mathcal{R}_3$ , and let  $IOR := IOR_1 \circ IOR_2$  be the IOR from  $\mathcal{R}_1$  to  $\mathcal{R}_3$  obtained by composing these. Suppose that:

- IOR<sub>2</sub> is HVZK with error  $\zeta_2$  and query complexity  $b_2$  with respect to  $\mathcal{D}_2$ , with simulator  $\mathbf{S}_2$ ;
- IOR<sub>1</sub> is HVZK with error  $\zeta_1$  and query complexity  $b_1$  with respect to  $\mathcal{D}_1^{y}(z) := \mathbf{S}_2^{y,\mathcal{D}_2}(z)$ . Then IOR is HVZK with error  $\zeta_1 + \zeta_2$  and query complexity  $b_1$  with respect to  $\mathcal{D}_2$ .

Proof sketch. Let  $\mathbf{S}_1$  be the simulator for  $\mathsf{IOR}_1$  and define  $\mathbf{S}^{y_1}(\mathbf{x}_1) \coloneqq \mathbf{S}_1^{y_1,\mathcal{D}_1}(\mathbf{x}_1)$ . By the honest-verifier zero-knowledge of  $\mathsf{IOR}_1$  with respect to  $\mathcal{D}_1$ , the distribution of  $\mathbf{S}^{y_1}(\mathbf{x}_1)$  has statistical distance at most  $\zeta_1$  from the experiment in which  $(\mathbf{P}_1, \mathbf{V}_1)$  interact on  $(\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{y}_1, \mathbf{w}_1)$ , outputting  $(\mathbf{x}_2, \mathbf{y}_2, \mathbf{w}_2)$ , and then  $\mathcal{D}_1^{y_2}(\mathbf{x}_2)$  is run. Since  $\mathcal{D}_1^{y_2}(\mathbf{x}_2) = \mathbf{S}_2^{y_2,\mathcal{D}_2}(\mathbf{x}_2)$ , by honest-verifier zero-knowledge of  $\mathsf{IOR}_2$  with respect to  $\mathcal{D}_2$ , this latter part has statistical distance at most  $\zeta_2$  from running  $(\mathbf{P}_2, \mathbf{V}_2)$  on  $(\mathbf{x}_2, \mathbf{y}_2, \mathbf{w}_2)$  to obtain  $(\mathbf{x}_3, \mathbf{y}_3, \mathbf{w}_3)$  and then running  $\mathcal{D}_2^{y_3}(\mathbf{x}_3)$ . Thus, the distribution of  $\mathbf{S}^{y_1}(\mathbf{x}_1)$  has statistical distance at most  $\zeta_1 + \zeta_2$  from an honest execution of  $\mathsf{IOR}$  followed by  $\mathcal{D}_2$ .

Distinguisher classes in our constructions. Lemma 2.6 spells out the minimal condition for HVZK composition by relying on Definition 2.5 with  $\mathcal{D}$  set to a specific algorithm. More generally we consider an extension of Definition 2.5 that requires the simulator to work for every algorithm  $\mathcal{D}$  in a given class  $\mathfrak{D}$ . This strengthening enables preserving HVZK in a more flexible way: for HVZK composition, it suffices that the algorithm  $\mathcal{D}$  arising from the second IOR's simulator belongs to the class  $\mathfrak{D}$ . We typically construct IORs that satisfy this notion for a specific class of algorithms that is both general and restricted enough for our purposes: the class of t-restricted distinguishers, denoted  $\mathfrak{D}^{\leq t}$ , consisting of algorithms that make at most t queries to the implicit instance.

See Section 4 for the formal definition of HVZK for IORs and the associated composition theorem.

# <span id="page-11-0"></span>2.4 A "committed" relation for zero-knowledge

We describe and motivate the main relation that we use in this work. The relation is similar to constrained relations in prior work [ACFY25; BCFW25] but with differences that we motivate below: (1) succinct linear constraints to allow for a polylogarithmic verifier, and (2) a "committed" version of the relation to allow for zero-knowledge.

The relation in Equation 2 is insufficient. The linear constraint relation  $\bar{\mathcal{R}}_{\mathcal{C}}$  in Equation 2 has two limitations from the perspective of our work. First, linear constraints are expressed via an *explicit* coefficient vector  $\mathbf{v} \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell}$  in the explicit instance  $\mathbf{z}$ , which rules out verifiers that run in time  $o(\ell)$ ; instead, we seek a *succinct* representation of linear constraints. Second, the target  $\mu \in \mathbb{F}$  in the explicit instance  $\mathbf{z}$  contains information about the secret message  $\mathbf{f}$  (even if  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}$  is a zero-knowledge encoding), whereas we seek to avoid revealing even a single linear constraint about the message  $\mathbf{f}$ . We modify  $\bar{\mathcal{R}}_{\mathcal{C}}$  to address these two limitations.

Modification 1: succinct linear constraints. We replace v in x with a succinct state st that implicitly represents such a vector. We hardcode in the relation a *succinct linear form* sl that, given as input a "small" state st (from some space determined by sl), outputs an explicit vector  $v \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell}$ . A simple example to keep in mind is the succinct constraint sl mapping  $\mathsf{st} = \alpha \in \mathbb{F}$  to the

{12}------------------------------------------------

vector  $\mathsf{sl}(\mathsf{st}) \coloneqq (\alpha^{i-1})_{i \in [\ell]}$ . These succinct linear forms are similar to sumcheck-based constraints in [ACFY25]. While the two constraint classes are formally incomparable, both would suffice for our protocols; we opt to use succinct linear constraints as they are a better notational fit in this paper.

Modification 2: masks in the constraint. The target  $\mu \in \mathbb{F}$  leaks the linear constraint  $\langle \boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{v} \rangle$  about the secret message  $\boldsymbol{f}$ . We further modify the relation to incorporate sumcheck-friendly masks intended to eliminate the leakage in  $\mu$ . We introduce an additional linear code  $C_{\mathsf{zk}} \subseteq \Sigma_{\mathsf{zk}}^{m_{\mathsf{zk}}}$  with zero-knowledge encoding  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}} \colon \mathbb{F}^{\ell_{\mathsf{zk}}} \times \mathbb{F}^{r_{\mathsf{zk}}} \to \Sigma_{\mathsf{zk}}^{m_{\mathsf{zk}}}$  that we use to encode small masks  $\boldsymbol{\xi}_1, \dots, \boldsymbol{\xi}_n \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell_{\mathsf{zk}}}$  (as many masks as there are variables). We associate linear constraints  $\boldsymbol{u}_1, \dots, \boldsymbol{u}_n \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell_{\mathsf{zk}}}$  to these masks, which need not be succinct (as the masks are in fact small).

Committed sumcheck relation. The above considerations lead to the relation  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}}$  where: (a) the explicit instance  $\mathbf{x} = ((\mu,\mathsf{st},(\boldsymbol{u}_i)_{i\in[n]}))$  contains the target, succinct linear constraint, and explicit linear constraints; (b) the implicit instance  $\mathbf{y} = (f,(\xi_i)_{i\in[n]})$  contains the main encoding and the encoded masks; and (c) the witness  $\mathbf{w} = (f,r,(\xi_i,r_i)_{i\in[n]})$  contains the underlying secret messages and secret masks and their corresponding secret randomnesses. Namely:

<span id="page-12-1"></span>
$$\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}} := \left\{ \begin{pmatrix} \mathbb{X} = ((\mu,\mathsf{st},(\boldsymbol{u}_i)_{i\in[n]})), \\ \mathbb{Y} = (f,(\xi_i)_{i\in[n]}), \\ \mathbb{W} = (\boldsymbol{f},\boldsymbol{r},(\boldsymbol{\xi}_i,\boldsymbol{r}_i)_{i\in[n]}) \end{pmatrix} \middle| \begin{array}{l} f = \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(\boldsymbol{f},\boldsymbol{r}) \\ \wedge \ \forall \ i \in [n] \colon \ \xi_i = \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}}(\boldsymbol{\xi}_i,\boldsymbol{r}_i) \\ \wedge \ \langle \boldsymbol{f},\mathsf{sl}(\mathsf{st}) \rangle + \sum_{i\in[n]} \langle \boldsymbol{\xi}_i,\boldsymbol{u}_i \rangle = \mu \end{array} \right\}. \tag{3}$$

We call this a **committed sumcheck relation**: the implicit instance  $y = (f, (\xi_i)_{i \in [n]})$  can informally be viewed as a commitment to the secret message f and masks  $(\xi_i)_{i \in [n]}$ , which are jointly constrained via a sumcheck-friendly linear constraint. The encoding f is the "main" oracle, while the encodings  $(\xi_i)_{i \in [n]}$  are much smaller encoded "mask" oracles: they appear in the joint constraint to "hide" the value of a linear constraint on f. We refer to the last constraint as the joint constraint, which combines the masks as well as the main oracle.

Verifier succinctness in our protocols will come from the succinct state st: in our sumcheck reduction, the verifier will only need to evaluate a "fold" of st, which is in polylogarithmic time for the succinct linear forms that we consider.

## <span id="page-12-0"></span>2.5 An honest-verifier zero-knowledge sumcheck reduction

We present an HVZK sumcheck reduction from testing the (constrained) interleaving of a code to testing the (constrained) code itself plus several small testing problems for another code (with a zero-knowledge encoding); the reduction is based on an extension to zero-knowledge codes of the notion of interleaved folding in [ACFY25; NA25; BCFW25]. Later in Section 2.7 we discuss how we use this HVZK sumcheck reduction multiple times to obtain our results: we use it to establish Theorem 1 and Theorem 2, and we use it for an HVZK variant of [ACFY25]'s sumcheck-query IOP reduction from R1CS to testing interleaved codes.

Code interleaving and its folding. Recall that the K-interleaving of a code  $\mathcal{C} \subseteq \Sigma^m$  is the code  $\mathcal{C}^{\equiv K} \subseteq (\Sigma^K)^m$  consisting of words  $u \in (\Sigma^K)^m$  such that there exist words  $v_1, \ldots, v_K \in \Sigma^m$  in  $\mathcal{C}$  with  $u(z) = (v_1(z), \ldots, v_K(z)) \in \Sigma^K$  for every  $z \in [m]$ . In this paper we consider the interleaving operation for  $K = 2^k$  and we define interleaving directly in terms of a zero-knowledge encoding  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^k}}$  that separately encodes  $2^k$  pieces of the given message, chosen in a special way based on multilinear polynomials, via the given zero-knowledge encoding  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}$  for  $\mathcal{C}$ . Below eq is the

{13}------------------------------------------------

k-variable equality polynomial

<span id="page-13-0"></span>
$$\mathsf{eq}(\mathtt{X}_1,\ldots,\mathtt{X}_k,\mathtt{Y}_1,\ldots,\mathtt{Y}_k)\coloneqq\prod_{i=1}^k(\mathtt{X}_i\cdot\mathtt{Y}_i+(1-\mathtt{X}_i)\cdot(1-\mathtt{Y}_i))\,.$$

**Definition 2.7.** Let  $C \subseteq \Sigma^m$  be a code with a zero-knowledge encoding  $\operatorname{Enc}_C \colon \mathbb{F}^\ell \times \mathbb{F}^r \to \Sigma^m$ . For  $k \in \mathbb{N}$ , the  $2^k$ -interleaving of C is the code  $C^{\equiv 2^k} \subseteq (\Sigma^{2^k})^m$  obtained via the zero-knowledge encoding  $\operatorname{Enc}_{C^{\equiv 2^k}} \colon \mathbb{F}^{2^k\ell} \times \mathbb{F}^{2^kr} \to (\Sigma^{2^k})^m$  defined as follows. We split a message  $\mathbf{f} \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k\ell}$  and randomness  $\mathbf{r} \in \mathbb{F}^{2^kr}$  into  $2^k$  pieces  $(\mathbf{f_b})_{\mathbf{b} \in \{0,1\}^k}$  and  $(\mathbf{r_b})_{\mathbf{b} \in \{0,1\}^k}$  by viewing both as multilinear polynomials  $\hat{\mathbf{f}} \in \mathbb{F}^{<2}[X_1, \dots, X_{k+\log \ell}]$  and  $\hat{\mathbf{r}} \in \mathbb{F}^{<2}[X_1, \dots, X_{k+\log r}]$  and writing

$$\hat{\boldsymbol{f}}(\mathbf{X}_1, \dots, \mathbf{X}_{k+\log \ell}) = \sum_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^k} \operatorname{eq}(\boldsymbol{b}, (\mathbf{X}_1, \dots, \mathbf{X}_k)) \cdot \boldsymbol{f}_{\boldsymbol{b}}(\mathbf{X}_{k+1}, \dots, \mathbf{X}_{k+\log \ell}),$$
(4)

$$\hat{\boldsymbol{r}}(\mathbf{X}_1, \dots, \mathbf{X}_{k+\log r}) = \sum_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^k} \operatorname{eq}(\boldsymbol{b}, (\mathbf{X}_1, \dots, \mathbf{X}_k)) \cdot \boldsymbol{r}_{\boldsymbol{b}}(\mathbf{X}_{k+1}, \dots, \mathbf{X}_{k+\log r}).$$
 (5)

Then we define

$$\forall \, z \in [m], \ \operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^k}} \left( \boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{r} \right)(z) \coloneqq \left( \operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}} (\boldsymbol{f_b}, \boldsymbol{r_b})(z) \right)_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^k} \in \Sigma^{2^k} \, .$$

Next, we define the notion of *interleaved folding*.

<span id="page-13-1"></span>**Definition 2.8.** The interleaved fold of a function  $f: [m] \to \Sigma^{2^k}$  at the point  $\gamma \in \mathbb{F}^i$  with  $i \in [k]$  is the function  $\text{Fold}(f, \gamma): [m] \to \Sigma^{2^{k-i}}$  defined as follows. We identify  $\{0, 1\}^k \cong \{0, 1\}^i \times \{0, 1\}^{k-i}$ , and for every  $z \in [m]$  we write  $f(z) = (f_{(\boldsymbol{b}, \boldsymbol{c})}(z))_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0, 1\}^i, \boldsymbol{c} \in \{0, 1\}^{k-i}}$ , meaning that  $f_{(\boldsymbol{b}, \boldsymbol{c})}(z) \in \Sigma$  is the coordinate of the vector  $f(z) \in \Sigma^{2^k}$  indexed by  $(\boldsymbol{b}, \boldsymbol{c})$ . Then, for every  $z \in [m]$  we define

$$\mathsf{Fold}(f, \pmb{\gamma})(z) \in \mathbb{F}^{2^{k-i}} \quad s.t. \quad \forall \, \pmb{c} \in \{0, 1\}^{k-i}, \ \left(\mathsf{Fold}(f, \pmb{\gamma})(z)\right)_{\pmb{c}} \coloneqq \sum_{\pmb{b} \in \{0, 1\}^i} \mathsf{eq}(\pmb{b}, \pmb{\gamma}) \cdot f_{(\pmb{b}, \pmb{c})}(z) \,.$$

The expressions are well-defined because  $\Sigma$  is an  $\mathbb{F}$ -linear vector space (e.g.,  $\Sigma = \mathbb{F}^{\iota}$  for some  $\iota \in \mathbb{N}$ ).

The key feature of the above definitions is that "encode then fold" equals "fold then encode".

<span id="page-13-2"></span>Claim 2.9 (informal). For every  $\mathbf{f} \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k \ell}$ ,  $\mathbf{r} \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $i \in [k]$ , and  $\mathbf{\gamma} \in \mathbb{F}^i$ ,

$$\mathsf{Fold}\left(\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^k}}\left(\boldsymbol{f},\boldsymbol{r}\right),\boldsymbol{\gamma}\right) = \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^{k-i}}}\left(\mathsf{Fold}(\boldsymbol{f},\boldsymbol{\gamma}),\mathsf{Fold}(\boldsymbol{r},\boldsymbol{\gamma})\right)\,.$$

The above definitions and identity are a direct extension to zero-knowledge codes of the notions of folding for arbitrary linear codes that appear in [ACFY25; NA25; BCFW25]. These folding notions are similar to, but distinct from, other notions of folding that apply to several classes of codes in [AHIV17; BCG20; BBHR18; BGKS20; GLSTW23; ZCF24; DP24; ACFY24].

<span id="page-13-3"></span>An HVZK committed-sumcheck IOR. Armed with these definitions, we can *fold* an initial interleaved codeword and then claim that the resulting codeword is an interleaved codeword with a *smaller* interleaving factor, while keeping track of the succinct linear constraints. This intuition is what underlies the HVZK committed-sumcheck IOR that we now describe. Specifically, we outline a queryless IOR from  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}\equiv^{2^k},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}}$  to  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}'}$  where  $(n,\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl})$  are as in the definition of the relation in Equation 3,  $\mathcal{C}$  is an  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive code with a zero-knowledge encoding  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}$ , and  $k\in\mathbb{N}$ .

{14}------------------------------------------------

Construction 2.10. The verifier receives as explicit input  $\mathbf{x} \coloneqq (\mu, \mathsf{st}, (\boldsymbol{u}_i)_{i \in [n]})$  and oracle access to the implicit input  $\mathbf{y} \coloneqq (f, (\xi_i)_{i \in [n]})$ . In the honest case, the prover receives as input a witness  $\mathbf{w} \coloneqq (\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{r}, (\boldsymbol{\xi}_i, \boldsymbol{r}_i)_{i \in [n]})$  such that  $(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y}, \mathbf{w}) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^k}, \mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}, \mathsf{sl}}$ .

- 1. The prover sends oracles  $s_1, \ldots, s_k \in \Sigma_{z_k}^{m_{z_k}}$ . The honest prover samples masks  $s_1, \ldots, s_k \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell_{z_k}}$  and randomnesses  $r'_1, \ldots, r'_k \in \mathbb{F}^{r_{z_k}}$ , and sets  $s_j \coloneqq \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{z_k}}(s_j, r'_j)$  for  $j \in [k]$ . The masks are interpreted as univariate polynomials  $\hat{s}_1, \ldots, \hat{s}_k \in \mathbb{F}^{<\ell_{z_k}}[X]$ :  $\forall j \in [k], \ \hat{s}_j(X) \coloneqq \sum_{l=1}^{\ell_{z_k}} (s_j)_l \cdot X^{l-1}$ .
- 2. The prover sends  $\tilde{\mu} \in \mathbb{F}$ . The honest prover sets  $\tilde{\mu} := \sum_{(b_1, \dots, b_k) \in \{0,1\}^k} (\hat{s}_1(b_1) + \dots + \hat{s}_k(b_k))$ .
- 3. The verifier samples and sends  $\varepsilon \leftarrow \mathbb{F}$ .
- 4. The prover and the verifier interpret f as a multilinear polynomial  $\hat{f} \in \mathbb{F}^{<2}[X_1, \dots, X_{k+\log \ell}]$  (see Equation 4) and they run a k-round sumcheck protocol on the claim

$$\sum_{(b_1,\ldots,b_k)\in\{0,1\}^k} \left(\hat{\boldsymbol{s}}_1(b_1) + \cdots + \hat{\boldsymbol{s}}_k(b_k) + \varepsilon \cdot \hat{\boldsymbol{G}}(b_1,\ldots,b_k)\right) = \tilde{\mu} + \varepsilon \cdot \mu$$

where

$$\widehat{G}(\mathtt{X}_1,\ldots,\mathtt{X}_k)\coloneqq \left(\sum_{\boldsymbol{a}\in\{0,1\}^{\log\ell}} \begin{array}{c} \widehat{f}(\mathtt{X}_1,\ldots,\mathtt{X}_k,\boldsymbol{a}) \ \cdot \mathsf{Fold}(\mathsf{sl}(\mathsf{st}),\mathtt{X}_1,\ldots,\mathtt{X}_k)[\boldsymbol{a}] \end{array}\right) + \sum_{i\in[n]} \langle \boldsymbol{\xi}_i,\boldsymbol{u}_i\rangle$$

and Fold is defined to mirror Definition 2.8:

$$\mathsf{Fold}(\mathsf{sl}(\mathsf{st}), \mathsf{X}_1, \dots, \mathsf{X}_k) \coloneqq \left( \sum_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^k} \mathsf{eq}(\mathsf{X}_1, \dots, \mathsf{X}_k, \boldsymbol{b}) \cdot \mathsf{sl}(\mathsf{st})[\boldsymbol{a}, \boldsymbol{b}] \right)_{\boldsymbol{a} \in \{0,1\}^{\log \ell}}.$$

In each round  $j \in [k]$  the prover sends a univariate polynomial  $\hat{h}_j \in \mathbb{F}^{<\ell_{\mathsf{zk}}}[X]$  and the verifier replies with a random  $\gamma_j \leftarrow \mathbb{F}$ . Define  $\gamma_j := (\gamma_1, \ldots, \gamma_j) \in \mathbb{F}^j$  for every  $j \in [k]$ , and set  $\gamma := \gamma_k$ .

5. The verifier sets  $\mu' := \hat{h}_k(\gamma_k)$  and outputs a new instance for the target relation  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl'}}$  with the joint constraint:

$$\hat{\bm{s}}_1(\gamma_1) + \dots + \hat{\bm{s}}_k(\gamma_k) + \langle \mathsf{Fold}(\bm{f}, \bm{\gamma}), \varepsilon \cdot \mathsf{Fold}(\mathsf{sl}(\mathsf{st}), \bm{\gamma}) \rangle + \sum_{i \in [n]} \langle \bm{\xi}_i, \varepsilon \cdot \bm{u}_i \rangle = \mu'$$
.

That is,  $\mathsf{sl}'$  is defined to express the succinct linear constraint  $(\mathsf{st}, \gamma, \varepsilon) \mapsto \varepsilon \cdot \mathsf{Fold}(\mathsf{sl}(\mathsf{st}), \gamma)$  and the outputs are:

- $\mathbf{z}' \coloneqq (\mu', (\mathsf{st}, \boldsymbol{\gamma}, \varepsilon), (\varepsilon \cdot \boldsymbol{u}_i)_{i \in [n]}, ((\gamma_j^{l-1})_{l=1}^{\ell_{\mathsf{zk}}})_{j \in [k]})$  where  $(\mathsf{st}, \boldsymbol{\gamma}, \varepsilon)$  is a succinct state for  $\mathsf{sl}'$  and for each  $j \in [k]$  the vector  $(\gamma_j^{l-1})_{l=1}^{\ell_{\mathsf{zk}}}$  is chosen so that  $\langle \boldsymbol{s}_j, (\gamma_j^{l-1})_{l=1}^{\ell_{\mathsf{zk}}} \rangle = \hat{\boldsymbol{s}}_j(\gamma_j);$
- $y' := (\operatorname{Fold}(f, \gamma), (\xi_i)_{i \in [n]}, (s_j)_{j \in [k]});$  and
- in the honest case  $\mathbb{W}' \coloneqq (\mathsf{Fold}(\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{\gamma}), \mathsf{Fold}(\boldsymbol{r}, \boldsymbol{\gamma}), (\boldsymbol{\xi}_i, \boldsymbol{r}_i)_{i \in [n]}, (\boldsymbol{s}_j, \boldsymbol{r}_i')_{j \in [k]}).$

**Completeness.** The completeness of the construction follows by Claim 2.9 after observing that  $\operatorname{Fold}(\boldsymbol{f},\boldsymbol{\gamma})[a] = \hat{\boldsymbol{f}}(\boldsymbol{\gamma},\operatorname{binary}(a))$  where  $a \in [\ell]$  and  $\operatorname{binary}(a) \in \{0,1\}^{\log \ell}$  is the binary representation of a (the same holds also for the folding of  $\operatorname{sl}(\operatorname{st})$ ). This follows by Definition 2.8:

$$\begin{aligned} \mathsf{Fold}(\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{\gamma})[a] &= \sum_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^k} \mathsf{eq}(\boldsymbol{b}, \boldsymbol{\gamma}) \cdot \boldsymbol{f}_{(\boldsymbol{b})}[a] = \sum_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^k} \mathsf{eq}(\boldsymbol{b}, \boldsymbol{\gamma}) \cdot \boldsymbol{f}_{\boldsymbol{b}}[a] \\ &= \sum_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^k} \mathsf{eq}(\boldsymbol{b}, \boldsymbol{\gamma}) \cdot \boldsymbol{f}_{\boldsymbol{b}}(\mathsf{binary}(a)) = \hat{\boldsymbol{f}}(\boldsymbol{\gamma}, \mathsf{binary}(a)) \,. \end{aligned}$$

Honest-verifier zero-knowledge. We sketch why Construction 2.10 is HVZK.

{15}------------------------------------------------

**Lemma 2.11.** Let  $t \in \mathbb{N}$  and assume  $\operatorname{char}(\mathbb{F}) \neq 2$ . If  $\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}} : \mathbb{F}^{\ell}_{\mathsf{zk}} \times \mathbb{F}^{r_{\mathsf{zk}}} \to \Sigma^{m_{\mathsf{zk}}}_{\mathsf{zk}}$  is a  $t_{\mathsf{zk}}$ -query zero-knowledge encoding for  $\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}$  with error  $\zeta_{\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}}$  and  $\ell_{\mathsf{zk}} \geq 2$ , then Construction 6.3 is honest-verifier zero-knowledge with error  $k \cdot \zeta_{\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}}$  with respect to the family of distinguishers  $\mathfrak{D}$  making t queries to  $\mathsf{y'}$  and  $t_{\mathsf{zk}}$  queries to each of the other oracles in  $\mathsf{y'}$ . The simulator makes t queries to f and  $t_{\mathsf{zk}}$  queries to each oracle  $(\xi_i)_{i \in [n]}$  in  $\mathsf{y}$ .

Proof sketch. We outline the zero-knowledge simulator. First, since the distinguisher class is restricted to make at most  $t_{zk}$  queries to the oracles  $(s_j)_{j\in[k]}$  in y', the simulator uses the simulator for the code  $C_{zk}$  to sample answers for these queries. Taking a union bound gives the simulation error  $k \cdot \zeta_{C_{zk}}$ . Second, for the sumcheck part, the simulator samples  $\tilde{\mu}, \varepsilon, (\gamma_j)_{j\in[k]}$  uniformly at random, and then samples  $(\hat{h}_j)_{j\in[k]}$  uniformly at random conditioned on satisfying all consistency checks in the sumcheck protocol. This matches the distribution of the sumcheck protocol, as we show via a linear-algebraic argument by counting the degrees of freedom provided by the random masks (this part is similar to the analysis of the zero-knowledge sumcheck in [XZZPS19]). The assumption  $\operatorname{char}(\mathbb{F}) \neq 2$  ensures that the mask contribution in the sumcheck claim does not cancel.

Round-by-round knowledge soundness. We sketch why Construction 2.10 satisfies straightline RBR knowledge soundness [BCFW25]. The construction is an IOR of proximity from  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}\equiv 2^k,\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}}$  to  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}'}$ , so the knowledge soundness error depends on proximity parameters for the first relation and for the second relation. Moreover, implicit instances involve multiple oracles of different sizes so we use multiple proximity parameters: a proximity parameter  $\delta$  for the main oracle, and a proximity parameter  $\delta_{\mathsf{zk}}$  for all mask oracles. One way to express this, similarly to [BMNW25; BCFW25], is via the "relaxed" relation corresponding to  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}}$  (Equation 3) defined as follows:

$$\tilde{\mathcal{R}}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}}^{\delta,\delta_{\mathsf{zk}}} \coloneqq \left\{ \begin{pmatrix} \mathbb{X}, & & & \\ \mathsf{y} = (f,(\xi_i)_{i \in [n]}), & & & \\ (\mathbb{w},\bar{\mathbb{y}} \coloneqq (\bar{f},\bar{\xi}_1,\dots,\bar{\xi}_n)) \end{pmatrix} \middle| \begin{array}{l} \Delta(f,\bar{f}) \leq \delta \\ \land \ \forall \ i \in [n], \ \Delta(\xi_i,\bar{\xi}_i) \leq \delta_{\mathsf{zk}} \\ \land \ (\mathbb{x},\bar{\mathbb{y}},\mathbb{w}) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}} \end{pmatrix} \right\}.$$

Finally, we target distance preservation, so we use the *same* proximity parameters  $\delta$ ,  $\delta_{zk}$  to relax the initial and target relations. In light of all this, the round-by-round knowledge soundness of the construction can be stated as follows.

**Lemma 2.12** (informal). For every  $\delta$ ,  $\delta_{\mathsf{zk}} \in (0,1)$ , Construction 2.10 has round-by-round knowledge soundness with relaxation  $(\tilde{\mathcal{R}}_{\mathcal{C}\equiv^{2k},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}}^{\delta,\delta_{\mathsf{zk}}}, \tilde{\mathcal{R}}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}'}^{\delta,\delta_{\mathsf{zk}}})$  with the following round errors:

$$\left( \frac{|\Lambda(\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^k}, \delta)| \cdot |\Lambda(\mathcal{C}^{\equiv n+k}_{\mathsf{zk}}, \delta_{\mathsf{zk}})|}{|\mathbb{F}|}, \\ \left( \epsilon_{\mathsf{mca}}(\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^{k-j}}, \delta) + \frac{\ell_{\mathsf{zk}} \cdot |\Lambda(\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^{k-(j-1)}}, \delta)| \cdot |\Lambda(\mathcal{C}^{\equiv n+k}_{\mathsf{zk}}, \delta_{\mathsf{zk}})|}{|\mathbb{F}|} \right)_{j \in [k]} \right) .$$

Proof sketch. The proof is similar in spirit to analyses in [ACFY25; BCFW25], but is more challenging due to the fact that multiple codewords participate in the constraint being checked. Typically, (knowledge) soundness analyses for IORs involve two types of errors: proximity preservation errors and list size errors. Proximity preservation errors are captured, as in prior work, via a quantity  $\epsilon_{mca}(\mathcal{C}, \delta)$  that equals the MCA (mutual correlated agreement) error of a given code  $\mathcal{C}$  at distance  $\delta$ . List size errors require some care in our setting, as we now explain.

{16}------------------------------------------------

In each round we upper bound the probability that *any* codeword at distance *δ* from the sent oracles causes that round's "claim" to flip from false to true. This leads to taking a union bound over the list size of the code at distance *δ*. However, "breaking" the interleaved word *f* into its 2 *k* parts and then naively applying a union bound over *each* one would lead to a union bound over |Λ(C*, δ*)| 2 *<sup>k</sup>* many events. Similarly, for the *n* + *k* masks allegedly in the code Czk, we may end up with a union bound over |Λ(Czk*, δ*zk)| *<sup>n</sup>*+*<sup>k</sup>* many events. Such an approach would ultimately require selecting an extremely large field F.

Instead, in our analysis we analyze codewords relative to the interleaved codes C ≡2 *k* and C ≡*n*+*k* zk . As interleaving *does not* increase a code's list-size by more than a constant factor [\[GGR11\]](#page-79-8), this yields a significantly improved soundness error. This crucially relies on the fact that our protocol *correlates the queries* (over a larger alphabet) to these oracles (which also reduces the argument size of the resulting argument after compilation).

**Comparisons.** We compare our zero-knowledge sumcheck with prior variants. While all known zero-knowledge sumchecks are similar at a high level in their masking of sumcheck polynomials, our specific construction differs from other variants in crucial aspects motivated by our context.

- [\[CFS17;](#page-78-13) [BCL22;](#page-77-8) [Dia25\]](#page-78-1) considers a "full mask" in the form of a random polynomial added to the original one via a random linear combination; this incurs an overhead of at least 2, which is too expensive for us. Moreover, [\[BCL22\]](#page-77-8) does not employ out-of-domain techniques to achieve list-decoding soundness and, in particular, only targets constant soundness error.
- [\[XZZPS19\]](#page-80-17) describes a zero-knowledge sumcheck, based on polynomial commitment schemes, that uses a small mask for each round rather than a full mask at the start. Our protocol also uses small masks but the different setting of IOPs/IORs demands different techniques: the small masks are *oracles* that we carry throughout the reduction (as opposed to them being committed and opened once). Moreover, the protocol in [\[XZZPS19\]](#page-80-17) reveals an evaluation of the masks and thus an evaluation of the main oracle; but we cannot afford this in our setting, which is why we define the relation to accumulate claims about the masks. Finally, our sumcheck is a reduction from an interleaved code to the base code, while the sumcheck in [\[XZZPS19\]](#page-80-17) serves as an ingredient in the GKR protocol, and hence it is used in a different context.
- [\[RW24\]](#page-80-13) uses zero-knowledge codes for masks tailored to the tensor-code sumcheck. Their context is similar to ours: they reduce the testing of a tensor code to the testing of the base code, and they similarly use small masks to achieve zero-knowledge (in their case they achieve *malicious*-verifier zero-knowledge while we achieve honest-verifier zero-knowledge). The main difference between their approach and ours is that they use tensor codes while we use interleaved codes. Moreover, we achieve list-decoding soundness, while [\[RW24\]](#page-80-13) targets constant soundness, and additionally, our work emphasizes the efficiency of the prover and the verifier, whereas the prover in [\[RW24\]](#page-80-13) runs in polynomial time.

There are other variants of zero-knowledge sumchecks in the PCP setting [\[GOS24;](#page-79-9) [GOS25\]](#page-79-10), though these are less relevant to this work as they are closely related to the "full mask" approach in [\[CFS17\]](#page-78-13) (which has an overhead of at least 2) and are adapted to the unique challenges of PCPs.

{17}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-17-0"></span>2.6 HVZK code switching

We describe our HVZK code-switching reduction: a HVZK IOR that reduces a claim in  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}}$  for the code  $\mathcal{C} \subseteq \Sigma^m \equiv (\mathbb{F}^{\iota})^m$  to  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}',\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}'}$  for an arbitrary code  $\mathcal{C}' \subseteq (\Sigma')^{m'} \equiv (\mathbb{F}^{\iota'})^{m'}$ . The setting that we consider presents two significant challenges compared to previous codeswitches (e.g. [RR20; RW24; ACFY24; ACFY25; BCFRRZ25; BMMS25a; BCFW25; BFRW25]).

Code-switching without ZK. Known code-switching methods follow a template.

- 1. The verifier has access to an oracle  $f \in \Sigma^m$ . In the honest case,  $f = \mathcal{C}(\mathbf{f}) \wedge \Phi(\mathbf{f}) = 1$ , i.e., f is the encoding under the code  $\mathcal{C}$  of a message  $\mathbf{f}$  that satisfies a given predicate  $\Phi$ .
- 2. The prover sends  $g \in (\Sigma')^{m'}$ . In the honest case,  $g = \mathcal{C}'(f)$ , i.e., g is the encoding of f under the code  $\mathcal{C}'$ .
- 3. The verifier samples challenges  $x_1, \ldots, x_t \in [m]$  and queries  $f(x_1), \ldots, f(x_t)$ .
- 4. The prover and verifier engage in a proximity protocol for the claim " $\mathbf{g} = \mathcal{C}^{-1}(g) \wedge \Phi'(g) = 1$ " where  $\Phi'(g) := \Phi(g) \wedge \bigwedge_{i \in [t]} \mathcal{C}(\mathbf{g})[x_i] = f(x_i)$ .

The checks  $\bigwedge_{i \in [t]} (C(\boldsymbol{g})[x_i] = f(x_i))$  are a "consistency check" that the new oracle g indeed encodes the same message as the original oracle f. This template has not been applied to zero-knowledge codes, with the notable exception of [RW24], whose code-switching relies on specific properties of (zero-knowledge) tensor codes.

Code-switching with ZK encodings. When using a zero-knowledge encoding  $\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}$  for a code  $\mathcal{C}$ , the codeword  $f = \operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(f, r)$  depends on both the message f and the randomness r. Hence, in order to be complete, a consistency check must also take into account r. One option to address this would be for the new oracle g to encode, under  $\mathcal{C}'$  both the message and the randomness. Unfortunately, since later rounds will require to invoke the sumcheck protocol, that would require introducing padding that would involve at least a  $2\times$  overhead. Instead, we leverage the committed sumcheck relation introduced in Section 2.4 to introduce an additional mask encoding the randomness using  $\mathcal{C}_{zk}$ . Then, supposing that  $g = \operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}'}(f, r')$  and  $s = \operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{zk}}(r, r'')$  (where r', r'' are freshly sampled pieces of randomness), for  $x \in [m \cdot \iota]$  one can write the following claim

$$f(x) = \operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{r})[x] = \left(\boldsymbol{G}_{\mathcal{C}} \cdot \begin{bmatrix} \boldsymbol{f} \\ \boldsymbol{r} \end{bmatrix}\right)[x] = \langle \boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{G}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\#}[x] \rangle + \langle \boldsymbol{r}, \boldsymbol{G}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\$}[x] \rangle,$$

where  $G_{\mathcal{C}} = [G_{\mathcal{C}}^{\#}, G_{\mathcal{C}}^{\$}] \in \mathbb{F}^{(\iota \cdot m) \times (\ell + r)}$  is the linear map associated with  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}$  (recall that we interpret alphabet symbols in  $\Sigma$  as vectors in  $\mathbb{F}^{\iota}$  and then again reinterpret the output of the encoding map as m symbols of  $\mathbb{F}^{\iota}$ ). This claim can be written as a succinct linear form via our committed sumcheck relation.

**OOD sampling.** Efficient support for proximity parameters in the list-decoding regime typically relies on some form of out-of-domain (OOD) sampling [BGKS20]; without it, the above ideas would lead to inefficient protocols (regardless of zero knowledge considerations). Informally, OOD sampling refers to methods that force the prover, after sending an oracle f, to "choose" a single codeword in  $\Lambda(\mathcal{C}, f, \delta)$  (if  $\delta > \delta(\mathcal{C})/2$  then it can be that  $|\Lambda(\mathcal{C}, f, \delta)| > 1$ ). Several methods for this task are used in the literature [BGKS20; ACY23; ACFY24; ACFY25; BMNW25; BCFW25; BFRW25; MZ25]; here we follow the presentation of [BCFW25]. Let  $\mathcal{C} \subseteq \Sigma^m$  be a code with message space  $\mathbb{F}^{\ell}$ , and fix a function  $\mathbf{ze} \colon D_{\mathbf{ze}} \to \mathbb{F}^{t_{\mathsf{ood}} \times \ell}$ . OOD sampling roughly works as follows.

1. The prover sends  $f \in \Sigma^m$ . In the honest case,  $f = \mathcal{C}(\mathbf{f})$ .

{18}------------------------------------------------

- 2. The verifier samples  $\rho \leftarrow D_{ze}$ .
- 3. The prover sends  $\mathbf{y} \in \mathbb{F}^{t_{\text{ood}}}$ . In the honest case  $\mathbf{y} := \mathsf{ze}(\rho) \cdot \mathbf{f}$ .
- 4. The prover and verifier proceed, with the additional constraint " $ze(\rho) \cdot C^{-1}(f) = y$ ".

[BCFW25] shows that, for appropriate choices of ze, with high probability over  $\rho$  a *single* codeword in  $\Lambda(\mathcal{C}, f, \delta)$  satisfies the aforementioned additional constraint, and this property enables building efficient protocols in the list-decoding regime.

However, the above protocol is not compatible with zero-knowledge. The vector y leaks information about f (which encodes information about the private witness). The tension is inherent, in that the OOD protocol is supposed to uniquely identify a single codeword (and thus witness), while the zero-knowledge guarantee mandates that no information is revealed about this witness.

**OOD sampling with ZK.** We resolve this tension by extending the function ze to allow for additional randomization. Let  $ze: D_{ze} \to \mathbb{F}^{t_{ood} \times (\ell+r)}$  be a function. The revised OOD sample is set as

<span id="page-18-0"></span>
$$\mathbf{y} \coloneqq \mathsf{ze}(\rho) \cdot \begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{f} \\ \mathbf{r} \end{bmatrix}$$
, (6)

where  $\rho \leftarrow D_{ze}$  and  $\mathbf{r} \leftarrow \mathbb{F}^r$  are freshly sampled randomness. We require ze to satisfy two conditions: (i) ze must be suitable as an OOD sample, and (ii) ze must guarantee that y leaks no information on f. The first condition, as shown in [BCFW25], is equivalent to ze being a zero-evader (a widely studied notion): for every  $\mathbf{v} \neq \mathbf{0}$  it holds that  $\Pr_{\rho}[ze(\rho) \cdot \mathbf{v} = \mathbf{0}] \leq \varepsilon_{zero}$ . For the second condition, we introduce the notion of a  $(r, \zeta_{ze})$ -private zero-evader which is a zero-evader where  $(y, \rho)$  in Equation 6 can be efficiently simulated (with statistical error at most  $\zeta_{ze}$ ). We show that zero-evaders satisfying the two conditions are plentiful (and in fact with perfect privacy error  $\zeta_{ze} = 0$ ).

**Lemma 2.13.** Let  $ze: D_{ze} \to \mathbb{F}^{t_{\mathsf{ood}} \times \ell}$  be a zero-evader with error  $\varepsilon_{\mathsf{zero}}$ . For every  $r \in \mathbb{N}$  there exists a zero-evader  $ze': D'_{\mathsf{ze}} \to \mathbb{F}^{t_{\mathsf{ood}} \times (\ell+r)}$  with  $error \approx \varepsilon_{\mathsf{zero}}$  that is (r, 0)-private.

One last challenge must be solved. The newly sampled randomness r must somehow be included in the code switching claim. Including r in the message encoded with  $\mathcal{C}'$  involves the same padding complication that we encountered in the zero-knowledge code-switching. Instead, we again make use of the flexibility of the committed sumcheck relation, and perform a joint OOD sample of both g and s. To do so, we rely on the following lemma, which generalizes [BCFW25]. In particular, compatibly with zero-knowledge encodings, we allow many codewords close to the original words to satisfy the same constraint, but we enforce that the "message" parts must indeed be equal.

<span id="page-18-1"></span>**Lemma 2.14.** Let ze:  $D_{ze} \to \mathbb{F}^{t_{\text{ood}} \times (\ell + \ell_{zk})}$  be a zero-evader with error  $\varepsilon_{\text{zero}}$ . Let  $\mathcal{C} \subseteq \Sigma^m \equiv (\mathbb{F}^{\iota})^m$ ,  $\mathcal{C}_{zk} \subseteq \Sigma^{m_{zk}}_{zk} \equiv (\mathbb{F}^{\iota_{zk}})^{m_{zk}}$  be  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive codes with zero-knowledge encodings  $\text{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}} \colon \mathbb{F}^{\ell} \times \mathbb{F}^r \to \mathbb{F}^{\iota \cdot m}$  and  $\text{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{zk}} \colon \mathbb{F}^{\ell}_{zk} \times \mathbb{F}^{r_{zk}} \to \mathbb{F}^{\iota' \cdot m_{zk}}$ . For every f, s,

$$\begin{split} & \operatorname{PC}_{\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}} \colon \mathbb{F}^{\ell}_{\mathsf{zk}} \times \mathbb{F}^{r_{\mathsf{zk}}} \to \mathbb{F}^{\iota' \cdot m_{\mathsf{zk}}}. \ \ For \ every \ f, s, \\ & \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad$$
