Proof sketch. Fix distinct  $(\bar{f}_1, \bar{s}_1), (\bar{f}_2, \bar{s}_2) \in \Lambda(\mathcal{C}, f, \delta) \times \Lambda(\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}, s, \delta_{\mathsf{zk}})$ . Write  $(\boldsymbol{f}_1, \boldsymbol{r}_1) \coloneqq \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}^{-1}(\bar{f}_1), (\boldsymbol{s}_1, \boldsymbol{r}_1') \coloneqq \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}^{-1}(\bar{s}_1)$  and  $(\boldsymbol{f}_2, \boldsymbol{r}_2) \coloneqq \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}^{-1}(\bar{f}_2), (\boldsymbol{s}_2, \boldsymbol{r}_2') \coloneqq \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}}^{-1}(\bar{s}_2)$ . If  $(\boldsymbol{f}_1, \boldsymbol{s}_1) = (\boldsymbol{f}_2, \boldsymbol{s}_2)$  we are

{19}------------------------------------------------

done. Else, since ze is a zero-evader, it must be that except with probability  $\varepsilon_{\sf zero}$ ,

$$\mathsf{ze}(\rho) \cdot \begin{bmatrix} \bm{f}_1 - \bm{f}_2 \ \bm{s}_1 - \bm{s}_2 \end{bmatrix} \neq \bm{0} \, .$$

Taking a union bound over the  $\binom{|\Lambda(\mathcal{C},\delta)|\cdot|\Lambda(\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\delta_{\mathsf{zk}})|}{2}$  many such words concludes the proof.

In particular, supposing that  $g = \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}'}(\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{r}')$  and  $s = \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}}(\boldsymbol{r}, \boldsymbol{r}'')$  (where  $\boldsymbol{r}', \boldsymbol{r}''$  are freshly sampled choices of randomness), Equation 6 can be rewritten as the claims that

$$\forall \, i \in [t_{\text{ood}}]: \; \boldsymbol{y}_i = \left( \text{ze}(\rho) \cdot \begin{bmatrix} \boldsymbol{f} \ \boldsymbol{r} \end{bmatrix} \big)[i] = \langle \boldsymbol{f}, \text{ze}_i^\#(\rho) \rangle + \langle \boldsymbol{r}, \text{ze}_i^\$(\rho) \; \rangle \, ,$$

where  $\mathsf{ze}_i^\#(\rho)$  (resp.  $\mathsf{ze}_i^\$(\rho)$ ) denote the first  $\ell$  (resp. last r) elements of the i-th row of  $\mathsf{ze}(\rho)$ . Again, these are claims compatible with our committed sumcheck relation.

<span id="page-19-1"></span>Codeswitch construction. We incorporate the ideas so far into the following construction.

Construction 2.15. The verifier receives explicit input  $x = (\mu, \mathsf{st}, \boldsymbol{u}_1, \dots, \boldsymbol{u}_n)$  and implicit input  $y = (f, \xi_1, \dots, \xi_n)$ . The prover receives witness  $w = (\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{r}, (\boldsymbol{\xi}_i, \boldsymbol{r}_i)_{i \in [n]})$  such that  $(x, y, w) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}, \mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}, \mathsf{sl}}$ .

- 1. The prover sends oracles  $g \in (\Sigma')^{m'}$  and  $s \in \Sigma_{zk}^{m_{zk}}$ . In the honest case, the prover samples  $r' \in \mathbb{F}^{r'}$ ,  $s \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell_{zk}-r}$ ,  $r'' \leftarrow \mathbb{F}^{r_{zk}}$ , and sets  $g \coloneqq \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}'}(f, r')$  and  $s \coloneqq \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{zk}}((s, r), r'')$ .
- 2. The verifier samples and sends out-of-domain randomness  $\rho \leftarrow D_{ze}$ .
- 3. The prover replies with  $\mathbf{y} \in \mathbb{F}^{t_{\text{ood}}}$ . In the honest case the prover sets  $\mathbf{y} \coloneqq \mathsf{ze}(\rho) \cdot (\mathbf{f}, \mathbf{r}, \mathbf{s})^\mathsf{T}$ .
- 4. The verifier samples and sends in-domain query randomness  $x_1, \ldots, x_t \leftarrow [m]$  and batching randomness  $\varepsilon \leftarrow \mathbb{F}^{t_{\mathsf{ood}} + t \cdot \iota}$ .
- 5. The verifier queries  $f(x_1), \ldots, f(x_t) \in \Sigma \equiv \mathbb{F}^t$  and sets

$$\mu' \coloneqq \mu + \sum_{i \in [t_{\mathsf{ood}}]} \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_i \cdot \boldsymbol{y}_i + \sum_{i \in [t]} \sum_{l \in [\iota]} \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_{t_{\mathsf{ood}} + i \cdot \iota + l} \cdot f(x_i)_l.$$

The verifier outputs a new instance for the target relation  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}',\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}'}$  with the joint constraint

<span id="page-19-0"></span>
$$\langle \boldsymbol{f}, \mathsf{sl}(\mathsf{st}) \rangle + \sum_{i \in [n]} \langle \boldsymbol{\xi}_{i}, \boldsymbol{u}_{i} \rangle$$

$$+ \sum_{i \in [t_{\mathsf{ood}}]} \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_{i} \cdot \langle (\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{s}, \boldsymbol{r}), \mathsf{ze}_{i}(\rho) \rangle$$

$$+ \sum_{i \in [t]} \sum_{l \in [\iota]} \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}_{t_{\mathsf{ood}} + i \cdot \iota + l} \cdot \langle (\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{r}), \boldsymbol{G}_{\mathcal{C}}[x_{i,l}] \rangle = \mu',$$

$$(7)$$

where  $G_{\mathcal{C}}[x_{i,l}] \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell+r}$  denotes the row of  $\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}$ 's generator matrix corresponding to the l-th symbol of the  $x_i$ -th position and  $\operatorname{ze}_i$  denotes the restriction of  $\operatorname{ze}$  to the i-th row. The output consists of:

- $\mathbf{x}' \coloneqq (\mu', \mathsf{st}', (\boldsymbol{u}_i)_{i \in [n]}, \boldsymbol{u}_{n+1})$  where  $\mathsf{st}' \coloneqq (\mathsf{st}, \boldsymbol{\varepsilon}, \rho, x_1, \dots, x_t)$  denotes the state for the linear form associated with  $\boldsymbol{f}$  and  $\boldsymbol{u}_{n+1}$  denotes the vector associated with  $(\boldsymbol{s}, \boldsymbol{r})$  (both the linear form and the vector are obtained by appropriately grouping terms in Equation 7),
- $y' := (g, (\xi_i)_{i \in [n]}, s)$ , and

{20}------------------------------------------------

• in the honest case  $w' \coloneqq (\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{r}', (\boldsymbol{\xi}_i, \boldsymbol{r}_i)_{i \in [n]}, ((\boldsymbol{r}, \boldsymbol{s}), \boldsymbol{r}'')).$ 

Analysis of Construction 2.15. We outline how we analyze the construction above.

**Lemma 2.16.** Assume that ze is a  $(\ell_{zk} - r, \zeta_{ze})$ -private zero-evader,  $\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}'}$  is a t'-zero-knowledge encoding for  $\mathcal{C}'$  with error  $\zeta_{\mathcal{C}'}$ , and that  $\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{zk}}$  is  $t_{zk}$ -zero-knowledge encoding for  $\mathcal{C}_{zk}$ . Then, Construction 2.15 is HVZK with error  $\zeta_{\mathcal{C}'} + \zeta_{\mathcal{C}_{zk}} + \zeta_{ze}$  with respect to the family of distinguishers  $\mathfrak{D}$  making t' queries to the first oracle of y' and  $t_{zk}$  queries to the other oracles in y'. The simulator performs t queries to the first oracle of y and  $t_{zk}$  queries to the remaining oracles.

Proof sketch. Since ze is a  $(\ell_{zk} - r, \zeta_{ze})$ -private zero-evader, there exists an efficient algorithm that simulates the distribution of  $(y, \rho)$ . Since  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}'}$  and  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{zk}}$  are zero-knowledge encodings, there are also efficient simulators for the distribution of queries to their encodings. The simulator for Construction 2.15 samples  $x_1, \ldots, x_t$  uniformly at random, queries f at the corresponding positions, and then runs the three simulators described above (answering queries to  $\xi_1, \ldots, \xi_n$  by querying the corresponding oracle).

**Lemma 2.17** (informal). For every  $\delta, \delta', \delta_{\mathsf{zk}} \in (0, 1)$ , Construction 2.15 has round-by-round knowledge soundness with relaxation  $(\tilde{\mathcal{R}}_{\mathcal{C}, \mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}, \mathsf{sl}}^{\delta, \delta_{\mathsf{zk}}}, \tilde{\mathcal{R}}_{\mathcal{C}', \mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}, \mathsf{sl}'}^{\delta', \delta_{\mathsf{zk}}})$  with errors

$$\left(|\Lambda(\mathcal{C}',\delta')|^2\cdot |\Lambda(\mathcal{C}_{\sf zk},\delta_{\sf zk})|^2\cdot \varepsilon_{\sf zero},\ \frac{|\Lambda(\mathcal{C}',\delta')|\cdot |\Lambda(\mathcal{C}^{\equiv n+1}_{\sf zk},\delta_{\sf zk})|}{|\mathbb{F}|}+(1-\delta)^t\right)\,.$$

*Proof sketch.* We only sketch soundness; the round-by-round knowledge soundness proof is given in Lemma 9.9.

Fix an arbitrary  $(\bar{g}, \bar{s}) \in \Lambda(\mathcal{C}', g, \delta') \times \Lambda(\mathcal{C}_{zk}, s, \delta_{zk})$  and let  $(\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{r}) \coloneqq \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}'}^{-1}(\bar{g}), (\boldsymbol{s}, \boldsymbol{r}') \coloneqq \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{zk}}^{-1}(\bar{s}).$  By Lemma 2.14 there is at most a single pair  $(\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{s})$  such that  $\mathsf{ze}(\rho) \cdot (\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{s})^\mathsf{T} = \boldsymbol{y}$ . Write  $\boldsymbol{s} = (\boldsymbol{r}^*, \boldsymbol{s}')$  and  $\bar{f} = \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{r}^*)$ . Note that  $\Delta(f, \bar{f}) > \delta$ , or else  $\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{r}^*$  constitute (together with a valid witness for the other masks) a valid witness for the relation, a contradiction to soundness. In this case, except with probability  $(1 - \delta)^t$ , one of the consistency claims is not satisfied. Thus, for arbitrary  $(\bar{g}, \bar{s}) \in \Lambda(\mathcal{C}', g, \delta') \times \Lambda(\mathcal{C}_{zk}, s, \delta_{zk})$  it must either be that: (i) the OOD condition is not satisfied; (ii) the associated joint constraint is not satisfied; or (iii) one of the consistency claims is not satisfied. Taking a final union bound over the batch yields the final bound.

Efficiency of Construction 2.15. The prover time in Construction 2.15 is dominated by the time to compute the encoding  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}'}$ . The verifier time is dominated by the time to compute  $\mu'$ . Both of these quantities are *independent* of the starting code  $\mathcal{C}$ , which is counterintuitive. The resolution is that  $\mathcal{C}$  affects future protocols used to test claims for  $\tilde{\mathcal{R}}_{\mathcal{C}',\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl'}}^{\delta,\delta_{\mathsf{zk}}}$ , since  $\mathsf{sl'}$  does depend  $\mathcal{C}$ . This added complexity of  $\mathsf{sl'}$  was informally reflected in the quantity  $T_{\mathcal{C}}$  introduced in Theorem 2. More formally, in protocols for  $\tilde{\mathcal{R}}_{\mathcal{C}',\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl'}}^{\delta,\delta_{\mathsf{zk}}}$  (such as Constructions 2.2 and 2.10) the prover time depends on  $t_{\mathsf{sl'}}$  and the verifier time depends on the cost of evaluating "folds" of the vectors specified in  $\mathsf{sl'}$ .

- The prover time will depend on the time required to compute  $t \cdot \iota$  rows of  $G_{\mathcal{C}}^{\#}$  and  $t \cdot \iota$  rows of  $G_{\mathcal{C}}^{\$}$ . We refer to this quantity as  $\mathsf{t}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathbf{P}}$ .
- The verifier time will depend on the time required to evaluate  $t \cdot \iota$  folded rows of  $\mathbf{G}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\#}$  according to Definition 2.8), and  $t \cdot \iota$  (non-folded) rows of  $\mathbf{G}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\$}$ . We refer to this quantity as  $\mathbf{t}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathbf{V}}$ .

{21}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-21-0"></span>2.7 From building blocks to our main results

Theorem 1 follows by composing the zero-knowledge sumcheck IOR from Section 2.5 with the non-succinct IOPP from Section 2.2. Theorem 2 further uses the code-switching protocol from Section 2.6. This sequential composition satisfies HVZK because we establish the composable notion of HVZK for IORs described in Section 2.3: in each IOR we explicitly address a "future distinguisher" that is going to query that IOR's output implicit instance, capturing as a special case the subsequent IOR invoked on that implicit instance. Making these dependencies explicit and instantiating the distinguishers based on the "next" IORs allows us to safely compose the sub-protocols while maintaining honest-verifier zero-knowledge throughout.

On the code for the small masks. The relation  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}}$  (Equation 3) is defined with respect to the two zero-knowledge codes  $\mathcal{C}$  and  $\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}$ . Extending the non-succinct "base case" protocol from Section 2.2 from the initial relation  $\bar{\mathcal{R}}_{\mathcal{C}}$  (Equation 2) to the relation  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}}$  requires additional care, incurring an additional term of  $(1 - \delta_{\mathsf{zk}})^{t_{\mathsf{zk}}}$  to the soundness error, where  $\delta_{\mathsf{zk}}$  is  $\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}$ 's distance and  $t_{\mathsf{zk}}$  is the number of queries to each mask oracle. In our constructions  $\mathcal{C}$  and  $\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}$  can be any  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive code (with a zero-knowledge simulator supporting the appropriate number of queries).

To state Theorem 1 we fix  $C_{\mathsf{zk}}$  to a Reed–Solomon code with message length  $O(\lambda/\log\log\lambda)$  and rate  $1/\log\lambda$ . Then each mask has size  $O(\lambda \cdot \log \lambda/\log\log\lambda) = \widetilde{O}(\lambda)$  and the distance  $\delta_{\mathsf{zk}} \approx 1-1/\log\lambda$  is such that the number of queries  $t_{\mathsf{zk}}$  required for  $(1-\delta_{\mathsf{zk}})^{t_{\mathsf{zk}}} \approx 2^{-\lambda}$  is  $t_{\mathsf{zk}} = O(\lambda/\log\log\lambda) = o(\lambda)$ .

To state Theorem 2 (as well as Theorem 4 below), instead  $C_{zk}$  must encode messages with size  $O(\lambda)$ . Again choosing rate  $1/\log \lambda$ , each mask has size  $O(\lambda \cdot \log \lambda) = \widetilde{O}(\lambda)$ , and the number of queries  $t_{zk}$  required is  $t_{zk} = O(\lambda/\log\log \lambda) = o(\lambda)$ .

Other choices of  $C_{zk}$  are possible; the above choices achieve o(1) overhead in the query complexity of the protocol over the non-private protocols (and in fact over any such IOPP, which requires at least  $O(\lambda)$  queries [BMMS25b]).

Theorem 1: sublinear HVZK IOPP for constrained interleaved codes. First we run the zero-knowledge sumcheck from Section 2.5 to reduce from  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}\equiv^{2k},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}'}$  to  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}'}$ , reducing a committed sum over the interleaved code  $\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^k}$  to a committed sum over the base code  $\mathcal{C}$ . Then, we use a *variant* of the non-succinct IOPP from Section 2.2 for the relation  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}'}$ . The sumcheck IOR reduces the tested object size from  $2^k \cdot m$  alphabet symbols to m alphabet symbols, the size of the base code. The verifier's runtime is dominated by the queries made by the non-succinct IOPP to the *folded* oracle, with each query mapping to a single query to the original oracle over the alphabet  $\Sigma^{2^k}$ .

One parameter regime of interest is the following. Assume the encoded message in  $\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^k} \subseteq (\Sigma^{2^k})^m$  has size  $\ell := 2^k \cdot \ell'$  for  $\ell' := \sqrt{\ell}$  and that  $k := \log \ell'$ . This implies that  $\mathcal{C} \subseteq \Sigma^m$  encodes messages of size  $\ell'$ . After running the sumcheck IOR, the queries made by the non-succinct IOPP to the tested codeword, i.e., the virtual oracle that the sumcheck IOR reduction outputs, amount to a query of the original oracle over alphabet  $\Sigma^{2^k}$ , whose size is  $|\Sigma|^{2^k} = |\Sigma|^{\ell'}$ . Since the problem size has been reduced by a square-root factor, the full-mask strategy in the non-succinct protocol over messages of sublinear size compared to the original oracle yields an o(1) overhead, as desired.

Theorem 2: HVZK code-switching. We combine components developed in prior subsections to obtain a protocol for reducing proximity testing for  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^k},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}}$  to proximity testing for  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}',\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}'}$ . The construction first runs the zero-knowledge sumcheck from Section 2.5 to reduce from testing

<sup>&</sup>lt;sup>1</sup>A more concretely efficient version can be obtained by balancing the interleaving factor with the message size to minimize the overall argument size (see e.g. [AHIV17; GLSTW23; BFRW25]).

{22}------------------------------------------------

the interleaved code  $\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^k}$  to testing the base code  $\mathcal{C}$ . Then, we apply the code-switching from Section 2.6 to reduce to the relation  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C}',\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}'}$ . As before, the queries by the verifier in the code-switching sub-protocol are made to the "virtual" oracle in the output of the sumcheck IOR, and are simulated by queries to the initial interleaved codeword (this dominates the verifier's runtime).

**HVZK polylogarithmic-verifier protocol.** To achieve much improved verifier complexity we fix a folding parameter  $k \in \mathbb{N}$  and a sequence of codes  $C_1, \ldots, C_n$ , selected so that the  $2^k$  interleaving of  $C_{i+1}$  has the same message size as (the non-interleaved)  $C_i$ . The input relation is  $\mathcal{R}_{C_1^{\equiv 2^k}, C_{2k}, sl}$ , which we apply Construction 2.10 and Construction 2.15 (i.e. Theorem 2) to reduce to an instance of  $\mathcal{R}_{C_2^{\equiv 2^k}, C_{2k}, sl}$ . The size of the problem has been reduced by a factor of  $2^k$ , and we iterate this process multiple times, concluding with the same variant of Section 2.2 used in the proof of Theorem 1. Similar to [ACFY24; ACFY25; NA25; BFRW25], the theorem is then instantiated with codes whose distances increase progressively across the reduction, which yields improved query complexity (an example instantiation yielding Corollary 3 follows). We give a generic formulation of the theorem, for any sequence of codes  $C_1, \ldots, C_n$  satisfying the conditions previously specified.

<span id="page-22-0"></span>**Theorem 4** (informal, polylogarithmic HVZK). Let  $C_1 \subseteq \Sigma^{m_{C_1}}, \ldots, C_{\mathfrak{n}} \subseteq \Sigma^{m_{C_{\mathfrak{n}}}}$  be  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive codes, and fix a folding parameter  $k \in \mathbb{N}$  such that for each  $i \in [\mathfrak{n}]$ ,  $C_i$  has a  $t_{C_i}$ -query zero-knowledge encoding  $\mathsf{Enc}_{C_i} \colon \mathbb{F}^{\ell_{C_i}} \times \mathbb{F}^{r_{C_i}} \to \Sigma^{m_{C_i}}$  with error  $\zeta_{C_i}$  and encoding time  $\tau_{C_i}$ . For each  $i \in [\mathfrak{n}-1]$ , let  $\ell_{C_i} \coloneqq 2^k \cdot \ell_{C_{i+1}}$ . For a security parameter  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , proximity parameters  $\delta_1, \ldots, \delta_{\mathfrak{n}} \in (0,1)$ , letting  $t_i = O(-\frac{\lambda}{\log(1-\delta_i)})$ , we construct a  $O(k \cdot \mathfrak{n})$ -round IOPP for  $\mathcal{R}_{C_1^{\equiv 2^k},T}^{\mathsf{lin}}$ , with the following features.

- The prover sends  $O\left(\sum_{i\in[2,\mathfrak{n}]}m_{\mathcal{C}_i}\right)+\widetilde{O}(\mathfrak{n}\cdot k\cdot \lambda)$  alphabet symbols, and the verifier makes  $\sum_{i\in[\mathfrak{n}]}t_i$  queries over alphabet  $\Sigma^{2^k}$ .
- The prover time is  $O\left(T + 2^k \cdot \ell_{\mathcal{C}_1} + \sum_{i \in [2,\mathfrak{n}]} \tau_{\mathcal{C}_i} + \sum_{i \in [2,\mathfrak{n}]} \mathsf{t}_{\mathcal{C}_{i-1}}^{\mathbf{P}}\right) + \widetilde{O}(\mathfrak{n} \cdot k \cdot \lambda)$  and the verifier time is  $O\left(T^{(k \cdot \mathfrak{n})} + m_{\mathcal{C}_{\mathfrak{n}}} + \sum_{i \in [\mathfrak{n}]} \left(2^k \cdot t_i + \mathsf{t}_{\mathcal{C}_i}^{\mathbf{V}}\right)\right) + \widetilde{O}(\mathfrak{n} \cdot k \cdot \lambda)$ .
- The protocol has round-by-round knowledge soundness error at most  $2^{-\lambda}$  assuming that, for every  $i \in [\mathfrak{n}]$ ,  $\mathbb{F}$  is large enough with respect to  $C_i$ 's mutual correlated agreement radius and list-size at distance  $\delta_i$ .
- If, for  $i \in [2, \mathfrak{n}]$ ,  $t_{\mathcal{C}_i} \geq t_i$ , the protocol is honest-verifier zero-knowledge with error  $\sum_{i \in [2, \mathfrak{n}]} 2^k \cdot \zeta_{\mathcal{C}_i}$ .

To achieve the parameters of Corollary 3, we consider an instantiation of Theorem 4 with Reed–Solomon codes, resembling [ACFY25]. Fix an initial message size  $\ell \in \mathbb{N}$  and an initial evaluation domain of size  $m \in \mathbb{N}$ , set  $k \coloneqq O(\log \log \ell)$  and  $\mathfrak{n} \coloneqq O(\log \ell / \log \log \ell)$ .

- For  $i \in [\mathfrak{n}]$ , let  $\mathcal{C}_i$  be a Reed-Solomon code with evaluation domain size  $m_{\mathcal{C}_i} \coloneqq 2^{-i} \cdot m$  and let  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_i} \colon \mathbb{F}^{\ell_{\mathcal{C}_i}} \times \mathbb{F}^{r_{\mathcal{C}_i}} \to \mathbb{F}^{m_{\mathcal{C}_i}}$  be the corresponding  $r_{\mathcal{C}_i}$ -query zero-knowledge encoding.
- Enforce that  $\ell = 2^k \cdot \ell_{\mathcal{C}_1}$  and, for every  $i \in [\mathfrak{n} 1]$ , enforce that  $\ell_{\mathcal{C}_i} = 2^k \cdot \ell_{\mathcal{C}_{i+1}}$ .

The rest of Corollary 3 follows by accounting, and noticing that if  $\mathcal{C}$  is the Reed–Solomon code encoding messages of size  $\ell$  with randomness size r into codewords of size m,  $\mathsf{t}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathbf{P}} = O(\ell + r)$  and  $\mathsf{t}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathbf{V}} = O(\log m + r)$ . (Both are implicit in [ACFY25; NA25] and made explicit in [BFRW25].)

A zero-knowledge reduction from R1CS to proximity-testing. We additionally provide a (1+o(1))-overhead reduction from R1CS to  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}}$  (in particular,  $\mathcal{C}$  can be an interleaved code, for use with our testing protocols). Using our masking technique as in Section 2.5, we achieve sublinear zero-knowledge overhead in the encoded codeword, whereas [BCL22] obtains overhead O(1) due to

{23}------------------------------------------------

full masking. We note that since, for simplicity, we do not employ a holographic/pre-processing step (as done in [BCL22; RW24]) the verifier in this protocol is non-succinct.

On a high level, our R1CS reduction is similar to the construction in [ACFY25] (generalized to work with interleaved codes). To add zero-knowledge, instead of padding the R1CS instance with randomness (as done in [BCL22]), we use a similar masking technique to mask the matrix-vector multiplications for each of the R1CS matrices. Then, the reduction uses sumcheck as its main building block, but now with two types of sublinear masks: the first are masks "protecting" the R1CS witness, and the second are the sumcheck masks as in Section 2.4. We can propagate the constraints for both mask types (which are syntactically the same) into an instance of the relation  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{zk},sl}$  (which our protocols can test in zero knowledge).

### <span id="page-23-0"></span>2.8 Bonus: distance-amplified codes

The HVZK protocols in this work are *code-agnostic*: they can be instantiated with any F-additive zero-knowledge code. The concrete efficiency of our protocols, like that of other recent code-agnostic IOPs/IORs based on interleaving, depends on the code's parameters; for example, the *encoding time* impacts the IOP prover time, the *distance* impacts the query complexity of the IOP verifier, and the *alphabet size* impacts how many bits each query reads. In this section, we propose **distance** amplification via dispersers (i.e., the ABNNR construction [ABNNR92]) as a way to achieve concrete improvements in the prover time and argument size for the resulting succinct arguments. This provides further opportunities for optimizations of zero-knowledge succinct arguments based on our techniques.

The code-design tension. In typical IOPs, a larger code distance enables fewer verifier queries for a fixed soundness target, which reduces the number of Merkle commitment openings in the resulting succinct argument. At the same time, the argument prover must (i) encode to obtain a codeword and (ii) commit to the codeword via a Merkle commitment; so it is desirable for the code to have a fast encoding and a small alphabet. Ideally, one would like a linear-time encodable code that is also nearly MDS (approaching the Singleton bound) over a small alphabet. Unfortunately, known families with near-MDS distance and linear-time encoding [GI05] are not known to be competitive in the parameter regimes relevant for implementations, whereas Reed-Solomon codes are MDS (meet the Singleton bound) but they have quasilinear-time encoding (via FFT-style multipoint evaluation) and their alphabet grows with block length.

**ABNNR distance**—amplification. High-distance linear-time encodable codes rely on distance amplification, and here we discuss one such procedure from [ABNNR92]. A bipartite graph  $G = (L \cup R, E)$  with |L| = |R| = m is a  $(\epsilon, \gamma)$ -disperser if every left set  $S \subseteq L$  with  $|S| \ge \epsilon$  has neighbors  $N(S) := \{j \in R : \exists i \in S \text{ with } (i, j) \in E\}$  covering at least a  $(1 - \gamma)$ -fraction of the right set R, and also (within this paper) G is right-regular of some degree d. Given a base code  $C \subseteq \mathbb{F}^m$  with distance  $\delta(C) \ge 1 - \varepsilon$ , the ABNNR amplification map  $A_G : \mathbb{F}^m \to (\mathbb{F}^d)^m$  is defined by fixing, for each right vertex  $j \in R$ , an arbitrary ordering of its left neighbors  $N(j) = \{i_1, \ldots, i_d\}$  and setting  $(A_G(c))_j := (c_{i_1}, \ldots, c_{i_d}) \in \mathbb{F}^d$ . This leads to a new code  $G(C) := \{A_G(c) : c \in C\} \subseteq (\mathbb{F}^d)^m$  with distance  $\delta(G(C)) \ge 1 - \gamma$ . In the parameters achieved by probabilistic constructions, with all but negligible probability, a random right-regular G of degree  $d = O(1/\gamma)$  is such a disperser; the transformation then increases any constant base code distance  $1 - \varepsilon$  to distance  $1 - \gamma$  while keeping blocklength |R| = m and incurring an alphabet blow-up from  $\mathbb{F}$  to  $\mathbb{F}^{O(1/\gamma)}$ . Explicit constructions of dispersers with these parameters achieve only a larger degree  $d = \text{poly}(1/\gamma) \cdot \text{poly}(\log m)$  [GUV09; TUZ07],

{24}------------------------------------------------

and reducing the gap between explicit and probabilistic constructions can further accentuate the efficiency benefits that we discuss below.

**Efficiency benefits.** We illustrate the efficiency benefits of distance amplification via the features of recent IOP-based succinct arguments [\[ACFY24;](#page-76-4) [ACFY25;](#page-76-6) [BCFW25\]](#page-77-6), where the dominant error term in the soundness error is ≈ (1 − *δ*) *<sup>t</sup>* where *δ* is the distance of the code and *t* is the number of spot checks by the verifier. In such protocols, the prover encodes a message via the given code and then commits to the resulting codeword via a Merkle commitment, and then (in a later round) sends the requested *t* random locations of the codeword along with the corresponding Merkle openings. We consider two efficiency metrics in this setting: encoding time and argument size.

• **Improved encoding time via amplified Reed–Solomon.** We instantiate the distance amplification transformation with a Reed–Solomon code as the base code, yielding an *amplified Reed–Solomon code* (ARS code). Fix a constant *c >* 1 and encode a message of length *ℓ* as a Reed–Solomon codeword on an evaluation domain of size *m* = ⌈*cℓ*⌉, achieving a relative distance ≈ 1 − 1 *c* ; then amplify to a target distance 1 − *γ* using a disperser. (See [Definition 12.5.](#page-72-1))

The encoding procedure in an ARS code separates the *expensive* FFT-based evaluation step performed at size Θ(*ℓ*) and the *fast* distance-boosting step performed by simply gathering neighbors; this yields an improved encoding time precisely in the regime where plain RS would require evaluation at Θ(*ℓ/γ*) points. Overall, encoding for plain RS takes time *O*( *ℓ γ* · log *<sup>ℓ</sup> γ* ) while encoding for ARS takes the (improved) time *O*( *ℓ <sup>γ</sup>* + *ℓ* · log *ℓ*).

While improving encoding time, using ARS *increases* argument size. The number of spot checks by the verifier depends on the code's distance alone and each is answered via the requested symbol plus the corresponding Merkle opening. Plain RS and ARS with the same distance yield the same number of spot checks but have different argument sizes: for plain RS the requested symbol is one field element and for ARS the requested symbol is *d* = *O*(1*/γ*) field elements.

• **Reducing argument size (with an increase in prover time).** Increasing distance by amplification reduces the number of spot checks for a fixed soundness target while increasing alphabet size, as each opened leaf now contains *d* field symbols. This leads to a parameter regime in which one benefits from the reduction in the number of queries, while the cost of the increase in the alphabet size is not too big. Under reasonable parameters, which we give in [Section 12,](#page-71-0) one can obtain an improvement of ≈ 30% in argument size.

This comes at a cost: the prover has to perform an additional distance amplification, making *O*(1*/γ*) copies of each symbol in the original codeword, increasing its runtime.

**Compatibility with our protocols.** While the amplified symbols have large alphabet F *d* , the resulting code remains F-additive (by viewing each alphabet symbol as a *d*-tuple over F). Moreover, if a base code admits a *t*-query zero-knowledge encoding, then placing *d* base symbols per amplified symbol yields a corresponding encoding for the amplified code that is (*t/d*)-query zero-knowledge. Thus, amplified codes can be used in all of our main theorems.

{25}------------------------------------------------

# <span id="page-25-0"></span>3 Preliminaries

We define objects and state results that we use in this paper. We use the following notation.

- For two functions  $f, g: [m] \to \Sigma$  and a set  $S \subseteq [m]$ , we write f(S) = g(S) to mean f(x) = g(x) for every  $x \in S$ . Conversely,  $f(S) \neq g(S)$  means there exists  $x \in S$  with  $f(x) \neq g(x)$ .
- For two functions  $f, g: [m] \to \Sigma$ ,  $\Delta(f, g)$  is the fractional Hamming distance between f and g (the fraction of points in which they disagree). For a set  $S \subseteq \Sigma^{[m]}$ ,  $\Delta(f, S) := \min_{h \in S} \Delta(f, h)$  (or if S is empty,  $\Delta(f, S) := 1$ ).
- The equality polynomial eq for n variables is  $eq(X,Y) := \prod_{i=1}^{n} (X_i \cdot Y_i + (1 X_i) \cdot (1 Y_i))$ .
- $\mathbb{F}^{< d}[X_1, \dots, X_n]$  is the ring of polynomials over  $\mathbb{F}$  with variables  $X_1, \dots, X_n$  of total degree < d.

Claim 3.1. For every  $\hat{f} \in \mathbb{F}^{<2}[X_1, \dots, X_n]$  and  $\boldsymbol{\tau} \in \mathbb{F}^n$ ,  $\hat{f}(\boldsymbol{\tau}) = \sum_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^n} \hat{f}(\boldsymbol{b}) \cdot \operatorname{eq}(\boldsymbol{\tau}, \boldsymbol{b})$ .

**Lemma 3.2.** For every non-zero polynomial  $\hat{p} \in \mathbb{F}^{< d}[X_1, \dots, X_n]$ ,  $\Pr_{\boldsymbol{\tau} \leftarrow \mathbb{F}^n} [\hat{p}(\boldsymbol{\tau}) = 0] \leq \frac{n \cdot (d-1)}{|\mathbb{F}|}$ .

<span id="page-25-2"></span>**Definition 3.3.** A zero-evader over  $\mathbb{F}$  with error  $\varepsilon_{\mathsf{zero}}$  is a function  $\mathsf{ze} \colon D_{\mathsf{ze}} \to \mathbb{F}^{t \times \ell}$  such that

$$\forall\, \boldsymbol{v} \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell} \setminus \{0^{\ell}\}\,, \, \Pr_{\boldsymbol{\rho} \leftarrow D_{\mathsf{ze}}} \left[ \mathsf{ze}(\boldsymbol{\rho}) \cdot \boldsymbol{v} = 0^{t} \right] \leq \varepsilon_{\mathsf{zero}}\,.$$

**Relations.** A relation  $\mathcal{R}$  consists of triples (x, y, w) where x is the explicit instance, y the implicit instance, and w the witness. The corresponding language is  $\mathcal{L}(\mathcal{R}) := \{(x, y) : \exists w, (x, y, w) \in \mathcal{R}\}.$ 

#### <span id="page-25-1"></span>3.1 Interactive oracle reductions

Interactive Oracle Reductions (IORs) [BCGGRS19; BMNW25] are a generalization of Interactive Oracle Proofs (IOPs) [BCS16; RRR16]. Below we describe the flavor of IOR relevant to this paper.

A public-coin IOR of proximity from relation  $\mathcal{R}$  to relation  $\mathcal{R}'$  is a tuple IOR =  $(\mathbf{P}, \mathbf{V})$  that works as follows. The prover  $\mathbf{P}$  is an interactive algorithm that receives as input  $(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y}, \mathbf{w}) \in \mathcal{R}$ , and the verifier  $\mathbf{V}$  is an interactive oracle algorithm that receives input  $\mathbf{x}$  and query access to  $\mathbf{y}$  (consisting of  $I_0$  symbols over some alphabet  $\Sigma_0$ ). They interact over  $\mathbf{k}$  rounds: in each round  $i \in [\mathbf{k}]$ ,  $\mathbf{P}$  sends a proof string  $\Pi_i$  consisting of  $I_i$  symbols over some alphabet  $\Sigma_i$ , and  $\mathbf{V}$  sends a uniformly random message  $\rho_i \in \{0,1\}^{r_i}$ . After  $\mathbf{k}$  rounds of interaction,  $\mathbf{V}$  makes queries to  $\mathbf{y}, \Pi_1, \ldots, \Pi_{\mathbf{k}}$  (each query to a symbol). After that: (i)  $\mathbf{V}$  either rejects or outputs a new explicit instance  $\mathbf{x}'$  and implicit instance  $\mathbf{y}'$  (which is implicitly specified in terms of the oracles  $\mathbf{y}$  and  $\Pi_1, \ldots, \Pi_{\mathbf{k}}$ ), and (ii)  $\mathbf{P}$  outputs a new witness  $\mathbf{w}'$  such that  $(\mathbf{x}', \mathbf{y}', \mathbf{w}') \in \mathcal{R}'$ . We write  $\langle \mathbf{P}, \mathbf{V} \rangle (\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y}, \mathbf{w})$  for the interaction of  $\mathbf{P}, \mathbf{V}$  above.

**Definition 3.4.** IOR = (**P**, **V**) has (**perfect**) completeness if, for every  $(x, y, w) \in \mathcal{R}$ ,

$$\Pr\left[\;(\mathbf{x}',\mathbf{y}',\mathbf{w}')\in\mathcal{R}'\;\middle|\;(\mathbf{x}',\mathbf{y}',\mathbf{w}')\leftarrow\langle\mathbf{P},\mathbf{V}\rangle(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{y},\mathbf{w})\;\right]=1\,.$$

**Efficiency measures.** Efficiency measures are implicitly functions of **V**'s inputs.

- Rounds: there are k rounds of interaction (a round is a prover message and verifier message).
- Prover communication: for each  $i \in [k]$ ,  $\Pi_i$  consists of  $I_i$  symbols over alphabet  $\Sigma_i$ ; in this paper the alphabets  $\Sigma_1, \ldots, \Sigma_k$  are  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive spaces, so we simply count the total number of elements of  $\mathbb{F}$  in  $\Pi_1, \ldots, \Pi_k$ , and we refer to this quantity as prover communication.

{26}------------------------------------------------

- Input queries:  $q_y$  is the number of alphabet symbols read by the verifier from y.
- Proof queries:  $q_{\Pi}$  is the number of alphabet symbols read by the verifier from  $\Pi_1, \ldots, \Pi_k$ .
- Input query blowup:  $m_{y'\to y}$  is the number of queries that a query to y' induces to y.
- Proof query blowup:  $m_{y'\to\Pi}$  is the number of queries that a query to y' induces to  $\Pi_1,\ldots,\Pi_k$ .
- Randomness:  $r := \sum_{i \in [k]} r_i$  is the total number of random bits sent by the verifier.
- Prover time: pt is P' time measured in algebraic field operations.
- Verifier time: vt is V's time measured in algebraic field operations.

**Remark 3.5.** An IOP for a relation  $\mathcal{R}$  is an IOR from the relation  $\mathcal{R}$  to the trivial relation  $\mathcal{R}_{triv} := \{(\mathbf{x} = \bot, \mathbf{y} = \bot, \mathbf{w} = \bot)\}.$ 

The IORs that we consider in this paper satisfy a strong notion of knowledge soundness, namely round-by-round knowledge soundness [BCFW25], extended to handle relaxed relations.

**Definition 3.6.** Let  $IOR = (\mathbf{P}, \mathbf{V})$  be an IOR from a relation  $\mathcal{R}$  to  $\mathcal{R}'$ . A knowledge state function with relaxation  $(\tilde{\mathcal{R}}, \tilde{\mathcal{R}}')$  for IOR is a (possibly inefficient) function KState that, on input a statement  $(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y})$ , interaction transcript tr, and knowledge state witness  $\mathbf{w}$ , outputs a bit and has the following properties.

- Empty transcript: if  $\operatorname{tr} = \emptyset$  is the empty transcript, then  $\operatorname{\mathsf{KState}}(\mathsf{x},\mathsf{y},\mathsf{tr},\mathsf{w}) = 1$  if and only  $(\mathsf{x},\mathsf{y},\mathsf{w}) \in \tilde{\mathcal{R}}.$
- Prover moves: if tr is a transcript where the prover is about to move and  $\mathsf{KState}(\mathtt{x},\mathtt{y},\mathsf{tr},\mathtt{w}) = 0$ , then  $\mathsf{KState}(\mathtt{x},\mathtt{y},\mathsf{tr}||\Pi,\mathtt{w}) = 0$  for every prover message  $\Pi$ .
- Full transcript: if  $\operatorname{tr} = (\Pi_1, \rho_1, \dots, \Pi_k, \rho_k)$  is a full transcript, then  $\operatorname{\mathsf{KState}}(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y}, \operatorname{\mathsf{tr}}, \mathbf{w}) = 1$  if and only if  $\mathbf{V}^{\mathbf{y}, \Pi_1, \dots, \Pi_k}(\mathbf{x}, \rho_1, \dots, \rho_k)$  outputs  $(\mathbf{x}', \mathbf{y}')$  such that  $(\mathbf{x}', \mathbf{y}', \mathbf{w}) \in \tilde{\mathcal{R}}'$ .

Definition 3.7. IOR = (P, V) from a relation  $\mathcal{R}$  to  $\mathcal{R}'$  has round-by-round knowledge soundness with relaxation  $(\tilde{\mathcal{R}}, \tilde{\mathcal{R}}')$ , errors  $(\varepsilon_1, \ldots, \varepsilon_k)$ , and extraction times  $(\mathsf{et}_1, \ldots, \mathsf{et}_k)$  if there exist a knowledge state function KState with relaxation  $\tilde{\mathcal{R}}$  to  $\tilde{\mathcal{R}}'$  for IOR and a deterministic extractor  $\mathbf{E}_{\mathsf{rbr}}$  with the following property: for every statement  $(\mathsf{x}, \mathsf{y})$ , round index  $i \in [\mathsf{k}]$ , and interaction transcript  $\mathsf{tr} = (\Pi_1, \rho_1, \ldots, \Pi_{i-1}, \rho_{i-1}, \Pi_i)$  where the verifier is about to move,  $\mathbf{E}_{\mathsf{rbr}}$  runs in time at most  $\mathsf{et}_i$  and

$$\Pr_{\rho_i} \left[ \exists \, \mathsf{w} : \begin{array}{l} \mathsf{KState}(\mathsf{x}, \mathsf{y}, \mathsf{tr}, \mathbf{E}_{\mathsf{rbr}}(\mathsf{x}, \mathsf{y}, \mathsf{tr} \| \rho_i, \mathsf{w})) = 0 \\ \land \, \mathsf{KState}(\mathsf{x}, \mathsf{y}, \mathsf{tr} \| \rho_i, \mathsf{w}) = 1 \end{array} \right] \leq \varepsilon_i(\mathsf{x}, \mathsf{y}) \,.$$

The total extraction time is  $\sum_{i \in [k]} et_i$ .

#### <span id="page-26-0"></span>3.2 Coding theory

We provide definitions for (error-correcting) codes and associated notions.

Definition 3.8. Let  $\mathbb{F}$  be a finite field. A code  $\mathcal{C}$  over an alphabet  $\Sigma$  with message size  $\ell$  and block length m is an injective map  $\mathcal{C} \colon \mathbb{F}^{\ell} \to \Sigma^{m}$ . The minimum distance of  $\mathcal{C}$  is

$$\delta(\mathcal{C}) \coloneqq \min_{\substack{f,g \in \mathcal{C} \\ f \neq g}} \Delta(f,g).$$

We say that C is  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive if  $\Sigma = \mathbb{F}^{\iota}$ , where  $\mathbb{F}$  is a finite field, and C is a linear subspace of  $\mathbb{F}^{\iota \cdot m}$ . When  $\iota = 1$  we say that C is  $\mathbb{F}$ -linear (or just linear when  $\mathbb{F}$  is clear from context). 

{27}------------------------------------------------

We sometimes highlight the alphabet structure  $\Sigma = \mathbb{F}^{\iota}$  when we explicitly think of an alphabet symbol as a vector of field elements. We often identify a code with its image  $\mathcal{C}(\mathbb{F}^{\ell}) \subseteq \Sigma^{m}$ . We consider several algorithmic measures associated to a code  $\mathcal{C}$ :

- $tenc(\mathcal{C})$  is the number of field operations required to encode a message  $x \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell}$  into its corresponding codeword  $u := \mathcal{C}(x)$ ;
- tcor(C) is the number of field operations required to *erasure correct* a codeword (Definition 3.9); every linear code has efficient erasure correction (Lemma 3.10, see [GRS12]).

<span id="page-27-1"></span>**Definition 3.9.** A code  $C \subseteq \Sigma^m$  has **erasure correction with time**  $\mathsf{tcor}(C)$  if there exists a deterministic algorithm  $\mathbf{E}_{\mathcal{C}}$  that on input  $f \in \Sigma^m$  and  $S \subseteq [m]$ : (i) if  $|S| \ge (1 - \delta(C)) \cdot m$  and there exists a codeword  $u \in C$  such that f(i) = u(i) for all  $i \in S$ , then  $\mathbf{E}_{\mathcal{C}}(f, S) = u$  (the codeword u is unique); and (ii) otherwise,  $\mathbf{E}_{\mathcal{C}}(f, S) = \bot$ . Moreover,  $\mathbf{E}_{\mathcal{C}}$  performs at most  $\mathsf{tcor}(C)$  field operations.

<span id="page-27-2"></span>**Lemma 3.10.** Every additive code  $C \subseteq \Sigma^m$  has erasure correction with time  $tcor(C) = O((\iota \cdot m)^3)$ 

**Interleaving.** An interleaved code is obtained by "stacking" a code's codewords.

**Definition 3.11.** Let  $C \subseteq \Sigma^m$  be a code and let  $K \in \mathbb{N}$ . The K-interleaved code  $C^{\equiv K}$  is the code over alphabet  $\Sigma^K$  with block length m defined as

$$\mathcal{C}^{\equiv K} := \{(u_1, \dots, u_K) \mid \forall i \in [K] : u_i \in \mathcal{C}\} \subseteq (\Sigma^K)^m.$$

A codeword  $u \in \mathcal{C}^{\equiv K}$  consists of  $K \cdot m$  symbols in  $\Sigma$  viewed as m symbols in  $\Sigma^K$ .

If  $\mathcal{C}$  is  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive then  $\mathcal{C}^{\equiv K}$  is  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive.

**Lists.** We recall the combinatorial list-decoding of a code.

**Definition 3.12.** Let  $C \subseteq \Sigma^m$  be a code. The list around  $f \in \Sigma^m$  at distance  $\delta \in [0,1]$  is

$$\Lambda(\mathcal{C}, f, \delta) := \{ g \in \mathcal{C} \mid \Delta(f, g) \leq \delta \} .$$

Moreover we define  $|\Lambda(\mathcal{C}, \delta)| := \max_{f \in \Sigma^m} |\Lambda(\mathcal{C}, f, \delta)|$ .

For every  $K \in \mathbb{N}$  and  $\delta \in (0,1)$  it holds that  $|\Lambda(\mathcal{C},\delta)| \leq |\Lambda(\mathcal{C}^{\equiv K},\delta)| \leq |\Lambda(\mathcal{C},\delta)|^K$ . In fact the list size of an interleaved code at some distance is larger than the list size of the base code by a factor independent of the interleaving factor K (but which depends on the distance  $\delta$  considered).

**Lemma 3.13** ([GGR11]). Let  $C \subseteq \Sigma^m$  be a code and let  $\delta \in [0, \delta(C))$ . Let  $b := \left\lceil \frac{\delta}{\delta(C) - \delta} \right\rceil$  and  $r := \left\lceil \log \frac{\delta(C)}{\delta(C) - \delta} \right\rceil$ . For every  $K \ge 1$ ,  $|\Lambda(C^{\equiv K}, \delta)| \le {b+r \choose r} \cdot |\Lambda(C, \delta)|^r$ .

**MCA.** We recall the notion of mutual correlated agreement [ACFY25]. When  $\Sigma$  is an  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive alphabet, scalar multiplication and sums in the definition below are interpreted componentwise in the fixed  $\mathbb{F}$ -vector-space structure of  $\Sigma$ .

<span id="page-27-0"></span>**Definition 3.14.** Let  $C \subseteq \Sigma^m$  be an  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive code. The mutual correlated agreement (MCA) error of a function  $PG: D \to \mathbb{F}^n$  for C at distance  $\delta \in [0,1]$  is

$$\epsilon_{\text{mca}}(\mathcal{C}, \delta) \coloneqq \max_{\substack{f_1, \dots, f_n \in \Sigma^m \\ \gamma \coloneqq \mathsf{PG}(\alpha)}} \Pr_{\substack{\alpha \leftarrow D \\ \gamma \coloneqq \mathsf{PG}(\alpha)}} \left[ \exists \, S \subseteq [m] : \, \exists \, u \in \mathcal{C} : \sum_i \gamma_i \cdot f_i(S) = u(S) \\ \exists \, i \in [n], \forall \, u \in \mathcal{C} : f_i(S) \neq u(S) \, \right]$$

{28}------------------------------------------------

Upper bounds on  $\epsilon_{mca}(C, \delta)$  are known for every  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive code (see e.g. [AHIV17; BCIKS20; ACFY25; Zei24; GKL24; BCGM25]). In this work we only consider functions  $PG: \mathbb{F} \to \mathbb{F}^2$  where  $\gamma \mapsto (1 - \gamma, \gamma)$  or  $\gamma \mapsto (1, \gamma)$ ; these two have the same error, as can be seen by considering the identity  $(1 - \gamma) \cdot f_1 + \gamma \cdot f_2 = f_1 + \gamma \cdot (f_2 - f_1)$ .

**Lemma 3.15.** Let  $C \subseteq \Sigma^m \equiv (\mathbb{F}^{\iota})^m$  be an  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive code and let  $PG: D \to \mathbb{F}^n$  have MCA error  $\epsilon_{\mathsf{mca}}$ . For every  $K \in \mathbb{N}$ ,  $\epsilon_{\mathsf{mca}}(C^{\equiv K}, \delta) \leq K \cdot \epsilon_{\mathsf{mca}}(C, \delta)$ .

*Proof.* For  $f \in (\Sigma^K)^m$  and  $j \in [K]$ , we denote by  $f[j] \in \Sigma^m$  the j-th row of f. We write  $\epsilon_{\mathsf{mca}}(\mathcal{C}^{\equiv K}, \delta)$ 

$$\begin{split} &= \max_{f_1, \dots, f_n \in (\Sigma^K)^m} \ \Pr_{\substack{\alpha \leftarrow D \\ \gamma := \mathsf{PG}(n; \alpha)}} \left[ \exists S \subseteq [m] : \ \exists u \in \mathcal{C}^{\equiv K} : \sum_i \gamma_i \cdot f_i(S) = u(S) \\ &\exists i \in [n], \forall u \in \mathcal{C}^{\equiv K} : f_i(S) \neq u(S) \right] \\ &= \max_{f_1, \dots, f_n \in (\Sigma^K)^m} \ \Pr_{\substack{\alpha \leftarrow D \\ \gamma := \mathsf{PG}(n; \alpha)}} \left[ \exists S \subseteq [m] : \ \exists (u_1, \dots, u_K) \in \mathcal{C}, \forall j \in [K] : \sum_i \gamma_i \cdot f_i[j](S) = u_j(S) \\ &\exists i \in [n], \forall (u_1, \dots, u_K) \in \mathcal{C}, \exists j \in [K] : f_i[j](S) \neq u_j(S) \right] \\ &< K \cdot \epsilon_{\mathsf{mca}}(\mathcal{C}, \delta) \,. \end{split}$$

where the last inequality follows from a union bound over the K events.

#### 3.2.1 Zero-knowledge codes

<span id="page-28-2"></span>**Definition 3.16.** Let  $C \subseteq \Sigma^m$  be a code. For message length  $\ell$  and randomness length r, a t-query **zero-knowledge encoding for** C **with error**  $\zeta_C$  is a function  $\mathsf{Enc}_C \colon \mathbb{F}^\ell \times \mathbb{F}^r \to \Sigma^m$  such that there exists a polynomial-time probabilistic simulator  $\mathsf{Sim}_C$  such that, for every  $\mathsf{msg} \in \mathbb{F}^\ell$  and  $S \subseteq [m]$  with  $|S| \leq t$ , the following two random variables have statistical distance at most  $\zeta_C$ :

- The random variable  $\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(\operatorname{msg}, \boldsymbol{r})[S]$  consisting of  $\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(\operatorname{msg}, \boldsymbol{r})$  restricted to locations in S, where the randomness is over  $\boldsymbol{r} \leftarrow \mathbb{F}^r$ .
- The random variable  $Sim_{\mathcal{C}}(S)$ , where the randomness is over  $Sim_{\mathcal{C}}$ 's randomness.

<span id="page-28-1"></span>**Definition 3.17.** Let  $C \subseteq (\mathbb{F}^{\iota})^m$  be an  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive code with a zero-knowledge encoding  $\mathsf{Enc}_C \colon \mathbb{F}^{\ell} \times \mathbb{F}^{r} \to (\mathbb{F}^{\iota})^m$ . A generating matrix for  $\mathsf{Enc}_C$  is  $G_C = \begin{bmatrix} G_C^\# & G_C^\$ \end{bmatrix} \in \mathbb{F}^{(\iota \cdot m) \times \ell} \times \mathbb{F}^{(\iota \cdot m) \times r}$  such that

$$\forall \, \bm{f} \in \mathbb{F}^\ell, \ \forall \, \bm{r} \in \mathbb{F}^r, \ \mathsf{Enc}_\mathcal{C}(\bm{f},\bm{r}) = \bm{G}_\mathcal{C} \cdot \begin{bmatrix} \bm{f} \ \bm{r} \end{bmatrix} = \bm{G}_\mathcal{C}^\# \cdot \bm{f} + \bm{G}_\mathcal{C}^\$ \cdot \bm{r} \,,$$

viewing a vector  $\mathbb{F}^{\iota \cdot m}$  as m symbols of  $(\mathbb{F}^{\iota})$ . For  $i \in [\iota \cdot m]$ ,  $\mathbf{G}_{\mathcal{C}}[i, \cdot]$  denotes the i-th row of  $\mathbf{G}_{\mathcal{C}}$ .

We recall the definition of the Reed–Solomon code and its zero-knowledge encodings, and a linear-time encodable code with a zero-knowledge encoding.

<span id="page-28-0"></span>**Definition 3.18.** Let  $\mathbb{F}$  be a finite field,  $\mathcal{L} \subseteq \mathbb{F}$ , and  $\ell \in \mathbb{N}$ . We define

$$\mathsf{RS}[\mathbb{F},\mathcal{L},\ell] \coloneqq \left\{ f \colon \mathcal{L} \to \mathbb{F} \ : \ \exists \, \hat{f} \in \mathbb{F}^{<\ell}[\mathtt{X}] \ s.t. \ \forall \, x \in \mathcal{L} \ \hat{f}(x) = f(x) \right\} \, .$$

{29}------------------------------------------------

**Proposition 3.19.** For every  $t \in \mathbb{N}$ ,  $C := \mathsf{RS}[\mathbb{F}, \mathcal{L}, \ell]$  has a t-query zero-knowledge encoding with message length  $\ell - t$ , randomness length t, and error  $\zeta_{\mathcal{C}} = 0$ .

**Theorem 3.20** ([BCL22, Theorem 6], informal). For every  $\varepsilon \in (0,1), \ell, t \in \mathbb{N}$ , there exists a randomized linear time encoding algorithm that maps messages in  $\mathbb{F}^{\ell}$  to a code  $\mathcal{C} \subseteq \mathbb{F}^{O_{\varepsilon}(\ell+t)}$  such that this encoding is t-query zero-knowledge with error  $\varepsilon$ .

#### 3.2.2 Interleaving and folding for zero-knowledge encodings

We define interleaving and folding for zero-knowledge codes. These operations are designed to be compatible with the *multilinear polynomials* we use later in the paper.

**Definition 3.21.** For a function  $f: \mathbb{F}^{n+m} \to \mathbb{F}$  and  $\mathbf{b} \in \mathbb{F}^n$  we write  $f_{\mathbf{b}}: \mathbb{F}^m \to \mathbb{F}$  as  $f_{\mathbf{b}}(x) := f(\mathbf{b}, x)$ .

<span id="page-29-0"></span>**Definition 3.22.** Let  $C \subseteq \Sigma^m$  be a code with a zero-knowledge encoding  $\operatorname{Enc}_C : \mathbb{F}^\ell \times \mathbb{F}^r \to \Sigma^m$ . For a folding parameter  $k \in \mathbb{N}$ , the  $2^k$ -interleaved zero-knowledge encoding  $\operatorname{Enc}_{C \equiv 2^k} : \mathbb{F}^{2^k \ell} \times \mathbb{F}^{2^k r} \to (\Sigma^{2^k})^m$  is defined as follows. We split a message  $\mathbf{f} \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k \ell}$  and randomness  $\mathbf{r} \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$  into  $2^k$  pieces  $(\mathbf{f_b})_{\mathbf{b} \in \{0,1\}^k}$  and  $(\mathbf{r_b})_{\mathbf{b} \in \{0,1\}^k}$  by viewing both as multilinear polynomials  $\hat{\mathbf{f}} \in \mathbb{F}^{<2}[X_1, \dots, X_{k+\log \ell}]$  and  $\hat{\mathbf{r}} \in \mathbb{F}^{<2}[X_1, \dots, X_{k+\log \ell}]$  and writing

$$\begin{split} \hat{\boldsymbol{f}}(\mathbf{X}_1, \dots, \mathbf{X}_{k + \log \ell}) &= \sum_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^k} \operatorname{eq}(\boldsymbol{b}, (\mathbf{X}_1, \dots, \mathbf{X}_k)) \cdot \boldsymbol{f_b}(\mathbf{X}_{k+1}, \dots, \mathbf{X}_{k + \log \ell}) \,, \\ \hat{\boldsymbol{r}}(\mathbf{X}_1, \dots, \mathbf{X}_{k + \log r}) &= \sum_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^k} \operatorname{eq}(\boldsymbol{b}, (\mathbf{X}_1, \dots, \mathbf{X}_k)) \cdot \boldsymbol{r_b}(\mathbf{X}_{k+1}, \dots, \mathbf{X}_{k + \log r}) \,. \end{split}$$

Then we define

$$\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^k}}(\boldsymbol{f},\boldsymbol{r}) \coloneqq \big(\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(\boldsymbol{f_b},\boldsymbol{r_b})\big)_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^k} \in (\boldsymbol{\Sigma}^{2^k})^m \,.$$

<span id="page-29-3"></span>Claim 3.23. Let  $C \subseteq \Sigma^m$  be a code and let  $\operatorname{Enc}_C \colon \mathbb{F}^\ell \times \mathbb{F}^r \to \Sigma^m$  be a t-query zero-knowledge encoding for C. For every  $k \in \mathbb{N}$ ,  $\operatorname{Enc}_{C^{\equiv 2^k}} \colon \mathbb{F}^{2^k \cdot \ell} \times \mathbb{F}^{2^k \cdot r} \to (\Sigma^{2^k})^m$  from Definition 3.22 is a t-query zero-knowledge encoding for  $C^{\equiv 2^k} \subseteq (\Sigma^{2^k})^m$  with error  $2^k \cdot \zeta$ .

Proof. Let  $\operatorname{Sim}_{\mathcal{C}}$  be the simulator for  $\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}$ . We define a simulator  $\operatorname{Sim}_{\mathcal{C}\equiv 2^k}$  for  $\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}\equiv 2^k}$ . For a query set S, let  $S_b$  be the projection of queries in S of the block  $b \in \{0,1\}^k$ . We define  $\operatorname{Sim}_{\mathcal{C}\equiv 2^k}(S) \coloneqq \{\operatorname{Sim}_{\mathcal{C}}(S_b)\}_{b\in\{0,1\}^k}$ . Let S be any query set where  $|S| \leq t$ , which implies  $|S_b| \leq t$  for every b. For every message f and randomness f,  $\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}\equiv 2^k}(f,f)[S] = \{\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(f_b,f_b)[S_b]\}_{b\in\{0,1\}^k}$ . For each  $b \in \{0,1\}^k$ , by the zero-knowledge property of  $\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}$ ,  $\delta\left(\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(f_b,f_b)[S_b],\operatorname{Sim}_{\mathcal{C}}(S_b)\right) \leq \zeta$ . By union bound over all  $2^k$  blocks,  $\Delta\left(\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}\equiv 2^k}(f,f)[S],\operatorname{Sim}_{\mathcal{C}\equiv 2^k}(S)\right) \leq \sum_{b\in\{0,1\}^k} \zeta = 2^k \cdot \zeta$ .

<span id="page-29-1"></span>**Definition 3.24.** Let  $\Sigma$  be  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive, and let  $f \in (\Sigma^{2^k})^m$ . For  $i \in [k]$ , the **interleaved fold** of f at  $\gamma \in \mathbb{F}^i$  is the function  $\operatorname{Fold}(f,\gamma) \in (\Sigma^{2^{k-i}})^m$  defined as follows. We identify  $\{0,1\}^k \cong \{0,1\}^i \times \{0,1\}^{k-i}$ , and for every  $z \in [m]$  we write  $f(z) = (f_{(\boldsymbol{b},\boldsymbol{c})}(z))_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^i, \boldsymbol{c} \in \{0,1\}^{k-i}}$  to mean that  $f_{(\boldsymbol{b},\boldsymbol{c})}(z) \in \Sigma$  is the  $(\boldsymbol{b},\boldsymbol{c})$ -th coordinate of  $f(z) \in \Sigma^{2^k}$ . Then we define

$$\forall\,z\in[m],\;\mathsf{Fold}(f,\pmb{\gamma})(z)\coloneqq\left(\sum_{\pmb{b}\in\{0,1\}^i}\mathsf{eq}(\pmb{b},\pmb{\gamma})\cdot f_{(\pmb{b},\pmb{c})}(z)\right)_{\pmb{c}\in\{0,1\}^{k-i}}$$

<span id="page-29-2"></span>where the product  $eq(\boldsymbol{b}, \boldsymbol{\gamma}) \cdot f_{(\boldsymbol{b}, \boldsymbol{c})}(z)$  and the sum are computed after identifying  $\Sigma$  with  $\mathbb{F}^{\iota}$ .

{30}------------------------------------------------

**Remark 3.25.** Let  $\Sigma$ , f, i be as in Definition 3.24. The interleaved fold of f is well-defined when  $\gamma \in \mathbb{F}^i$  is replaced with indeterminates  $(\mathbf{X}_1, \dots, \mathbf{X}_i)$ . Indeed, for every  $z \in [m]$ ,

$$\mathsf{Fold}(f,(\mathbf{X}_1,\ldots,\mathbf{X}_i))(z) \coloneqq \left(\sum_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^i} \mathsf{eq}(\boldsymbol{b},\mathbf{X}_1,\ldots,\mathbf{X}_i) \cdot f_{(\boldsymbol{b},\boldsymbol{c})}(z)\right)_{\boldsymbol{c} \in \{0,1\}^{k-i}} \in (\Sigma^{<2}[\mathbf{X}_1,\ldots,\mathbf{X}_i])^{2^{k-i}}\,.$$

<span id="page-30-0"></span>**Lemma 3.26.** Let  $C \subseteq \Sigma^m \equiv (\mathbb{F}^{\iota})^m$  be an  $\mathbb{F}$ -additive code with a zero-knowledge encoding  $\mathsf{Enc}_C \colon \mathbb{F}^{\ell} \times \mathbb{F}^r \to \Sigma^m$  and generating matrix  $G_C$  (as in Definition 3.17). For every  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k \ell}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^{2^k r}$ ,  $f \in \mathbb{F}^2$ 

$$\mathsf{Fold}\left(\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^k}}(\boldsymbol{f},\boldsymbol{r}),\boldsymbol{\gamma}\right) = \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^{k-j}}}\left(\mathsf{Fold}(\boldsymbol{f},\boldsymbol{\gamma}),\mathsf{Fold}(\boldsymbol{r},\boldsymbol{\gamma})\right)\,.$$

*Proof.* Identify  $\{0,1\}^k \cong \{0,1\}^j \times \{0,1\}^{k-j}$ . By the definition of  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^k}}$  (Definition 3.22),

$$\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^k}}(\boldsymbol{f},\boldsymbol{r}) = \big(\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(\boldsymbol{f_{(b,c)}},\boldsymbol{r_{(b,c)}})\big)_{(b,c)\in\{0,1\}^j\times\{0,1\}^{k-j}}\,.$$

By the definition of Fold over  $\Sigma$  (Definition 3.24) and the above equality,

$$\mathsf{Fold}\left(\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}^{\equiv 2^k}}(\boldsymbol{f},\boldsymbol{r}),\boldsymbol{\gamma}\right) = \left(\sum_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^j} \mathsf{eq}(\boldsymbol{b},\boldsymbol{\gamma}) \cdot \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(\boldsymbol{f}_{(\boldsymbol{b},\boldsymbol{c})},\boldsymbol{r}_{(\boldsymbol{b},\boldsymbol{c})})\right)_{\boldsymbol{c} \in \{0,1\}^{k-j}}.$$

Using Definition 3.17 for each summand, for every  $\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^j$  and  $\boldsymbol{c} \in \{0,1\}^{k-j}$ ,

$$\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(f_{(b,c)},r_{(b,c)}) = G_{\mathcal{C}} \cdot \begin{bmatrix} f_{(b,c)} \ r_{(b,c)} \end{bmatrix} = G_{\mathcal{C}}^{\#} \cdot f_{(b,c)} + G_{\mathcal{C}}^{\$} \cdot r_{(b,c)} \,.$$

Therefore,

$$\begin{split} &\left(\sum_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^j} \operatorname{eq}(\boldsymbol{b}, \boldsymbol{\gamma}) \cdot \operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(\boldsymbol{f}_{(\boldsymbol{b},\boldsymbol{c})}, \boldsymbol{r}_{(\boldsymbol{b},\boldsymbol{c})})\right)_{\boldsymbol{c} \in \{0,1\}^{k-j}} \\ &= \boldsymbol{G}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\#} \cdot \left(\sum_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^j} \operatorname{eq}(\boldsymbol{b}, \boldsymbol{\gamma}) \cdot \boldsymbol{f}_{(\boldsymbol{b},\boldsymbol{c})}\right)_{\boldsymbol{c} \in \{0,1\}^{k-j}} + \boldsymbol{G}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\$} \cdot \left(\sum_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^j} \operatorname{eq}(\boldsymbol{b}, \boldsymbol{\gamma}) \cdot \boldsymbol{r}_{(\boldsymbol{b},\boldsymbol{c})}\right)_{\boldsymbol{c} \in \{0,1\}^{k-j}} \\ &\qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \text{(by linearity)} \\ &= \boldsymbol{G}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\#} \cdot \operatorname{Fold}(\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{\gamma}) + \boldsymbol{G}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\$} \cdot \operatorname{Fold}(\boldsymbol{r}, \boldsymbol{\gamma}) \\ &= \operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C} \equiv 2^{k-j}} \left(\operatorname{Fold}(\boldsymbol{f}, \boldsymbol{\gamma}), \operatorname{Fold}(\boldsymbol{r}, \boldsymbol{\gamma})\right) \,. \end{split}$$

{31}------------------------------------------------

# <span id="page-31-0"></span>**4 Zero-knowledge for IORs and composition**

**Definition 4.1.** *Let* IOR = (**P***,* **V**) *be an IOR from* R *to* R′ *and let* D *be an algorithm. The* D**-extended view for** IOR *on input* (**x***,* **y***,***w**) ∈ R *is the random variable*

$$\mathsf{View}(\mathbf{P},\mathbf{V},\mathcal{D},\mathbf{x},\mathbf{y},\mathbf{w}) = ((\rho_1,\ldots,\rho_{\mathsf{k}}),(Q_{\mathsf{y}},Q_1,\ldots,Q_{\mathsf{k}}),(\boldsymbol{a}_{\mathsf{y}},\boldsymbol{a}_1,\ldots,\boldsymbol{a}_{\mathsf{k}}),\mathsf{out})$$

*obtained by running the experiment*

$$\begin{bmatrix} (\mathbf{z}', \mathbf{y}', \mathbf{w}') \leftarrow \langle \mathbf{P}, \mathbf{V} \rangle (\mathbf{z}, \mathbf{y}, \mathbf{w}) \\ \text{out} \leftarrow \mathcal{D}^{\mathbf{y}'} (\mathbf{z}') \end{bmatrix}$$

*where* out *may be any output string of* D*, and making the following definitions for every i* ∈ [k]*:*

- *ρ<sup>i</sup>* ∈ {0*,* 1} r*i is the verifier randomness in round i;*
- *Q***<sup>y</sup>** ⊆ [l**y**] *and a***<sup>y</sup>** ∈ Σ *<sup>Q</sup>***<sup>y</sup>** *are the queries of* **V** *and* D *to* **y** *along with their answers; and*
- *Q<sup>i</sup>* ⊆ [l*<sup>i</sup>* ] *and a<sup>i</sup>* ∈ Σ *<sup>Q</sup><sup>i</sup> are the queries of* **V** *and* D *to the proof oracle* Π*<sup>i</sup> sent in round i along with their answers.*

<span id="page-31-1"></span>**Definition 4.2.** *Let* IOR *be an IOR from* R *to* R′ *and let* D *be a set of algorithms.* IOR *is* **honestverifier zero-knowledge for** D **with error** *ζ* **and query complexity** b *if and only if there exists a polynomial-time probabilistic simulator* **S** *such that for every* D ∈ D *and* (**x***,* **y***,* **w**) ∈ R *the two distributions* View(**P***,* **V***,* D*,* **x***,* **y***,* **w**) *and* **S <sup>y</sup>***,*D(**x**) *have statistical distance at most ζ*(D)*, and* **S <sup>y</sup>***,*D(**x**) *makes at most* b(D) *queries to* **y***. We let ζ* := maxD∈<sup>D</sup> *ζ*(D) *and* b := maxD∈<sup>D</sup> b(D)*.*

**Remark 4.3.** When the IOR is from R to the trivial relation Rtriv (i.e., the IOR is an IOP) one can verify that [Definition 4.2](#page-31-1) coincides with honest-verifier zero-knowledge for the IOP.

<span id="page-31-2"></span>**Definition 4.4.** *Given algorithms* **S** *and* D*, we let* D**<sup>S</sup>** *be the algorithm such that* D **y S** (**x**) := **S <sup>y</sup>***,*D(**x**)*. For a set of algorithms* D *we define the* **S-wise distinguisher class** D**<sup>S</sup>** := {D**<sup>S</sup>** : D ∈ D}*.*

<span id="page-31-3"></span>**Theorem 4.5.** *Let* IOR<sup>1</sup> *be an IOR from* Rstart *to* Rint *and let* IOR<sup>2</sup> *be an IOR from* Rint *to* Rfin *that have the following parameters (respectively).*

- *Round complexities* k<sup>1</sup> *and* k2*.*
- *Prover communication* l<sup>1</sup> *and* l2*.*
- *Input query complexities* q**y***,*<sup>1</sup> *and* q**y***,*2*.*
- *Proof query complexities* q<sup>1</sup> *and* q2*.*
- *Prover times* pt<sup>1</sup> *and* pt<sup>2</sup> *.*
- *Verifier times* vt<sup>1</sup> *and* vt2*.*
- IOR<sup>1</sup> *has input query blow up m***<sup>y</sup>** ′→**<sup>y</sup>** *and proof query blowup m***<sup>y</sup>** ′→Π*.*

*[Construction 4.6](#page-32-0) is an IOR* IOR *from* Rstart *to* Rfin *with the following parameters.*

- *Round complexity* k<sup>1</sup> + k2*.*
- *Prover communication* l<sup>1</sup> + l2*.*
- *Input query complexity* q**y***,*<sup>1</sup> + *m***<sup>y</sup>** ′→**<sup>y</sup>** · q**y***,*2*.*
- *Proof query complexity* q<sup>1</sup> + q<sup>2</sup> + *m***<sup>y</sup>** ′→<sup>Π</sup> · q**y***,*2*.*
- *Prover time* pt<sup>1</sup> + pt<sup>2</sup> *.*
- *Verifier time* vt<sup>1</sup> + vt2*.*

*Moreover:*

{32}------------------------------------------------

- RBR security. If IOR<sub>1</sub> has RBR knowledge soundness with relaxation  $(\tilde{\mathcal{R}}_{\text{start}}, \tilde{\mathcal{R}}_{\text{int}})$  and errors  $(\varepsilon_1^{(1)}, \ldots, \varepsilon_{k_1}^{(1)})$  and IOR<sub>2</sub> has RBR knowledge soundness with relaxation  $(\tilde{\mathcal{R}}_{\text{int}}, \tilde{\mathcal{R}}_{\text{fin}})$  and errors  $(\varepsilon_1^{(2)}, \ldots, \varepsilon_{k_2}^{(2)})$ , then IOR has RBR knowledge soundness with relaxation  $(\tilde{\mathcal{R}}_{\text{start}}, \tilde{\mathcal{R}}_{\text{fin}})$  and errors  $(\varepsilon_1^{(1)}, \ldots, \varepsilon_{k_1}^{(2)}, \varepsilon_1^{(2)}, \ldots, \varepsilon_{k_2}^{(2)})$ .
- **Zero-knowledge.** If  $IOR_2$  is HVZK for  $\mathfrak D$  with error  $\zeta_2$  via the simulator  $\mathbf S_2$ , and  $IOR_1$  is HVZK for  $\mathfrak D_{\mathbf S_2}$  with error  $\zeta_1$  and query complexity  $\mathsf b$ , then IOR is HVZK for  $\mathfrak D$  with error  $\zeta_1 + \zeta_2$  and query complexity  $\mathsf b$ .

<span id="page-32-0"></span>Construction 4.6. The IOR consists of the concatenation of  $IOR_1$  and  $IOR_2$ .

- Inputs. The verifier receives as explicit input  $z_1$  and query access to  $y_1$ . The honest prover receives as input  $w_1$  such that  $(z_1, y_1, w_1) \in \mathcal{R}_{start}$ .
- Protocol.
  - 1. **First IOR.** The prover and verifier run  $\mathsf{IOR}_1$  for  $(\varkappa_1, y_1, w_1)$ . After the interaction, the verifier outputs an explicit instance  $\varkappa_2$  and implicit instance  $y_2$  (defined via  $y_1$  and proof oracles sent in the execution of  $\mathsf{IOR}_1$ ). The honest prover outputs a witness  $w_2$  such that  $(\varkappa_2, y_2, w_2) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{int}}$ .
  - 2. **Second IOR.** The prover and verifier run IOR<sub>2</sub> for  $(x_2, y_2, w_2)$ . After the interaction, the verifier outputs an explicit instance  $x_3$  and implicit instance  $y_3$ . The honest prover outputs a witness  $w_3$  such that  $(x_3, y_3, w_3) \in \mathcal{R}_{fin}$ .
- Output. The verifier outputs explicit instance  $x_3$  and implicit instance  $y_3$ . The honest prover outputs a witness  $w_3$ .

*Proof.* The efficiency parameters follow via inspection and the RBR knowledge soundness errors follow similarly to prior work. Here we focus on establishing HVZK of the composed IOR, demonstrating how to use Definition 4.2. Consider the following simulator.

 $\mathbf{S}^{\mathsf{y}_1,\mathcal{D}_2}(\mathsf{x}_1)$ :

- 1. Define the algorithm  $\mathcal{D}_1^{y_2}(\mathbf{x}_2) \coloneqq \mathbf{S}_2^{y_2,\mathcal{D}_2}(\mathbf{x}_2)$ .
- 2. Run and output  $\mathbf{S}_1^{y_1,\mathcal{D}_1}(\mathbf{x}_1)$ .

We argue that, for every  $(x_1, y_1, w_1) \in \mathcal{R}_{start}$  and  $\mathcal{D}_2 \in \mathfrak{D}$ , the following two distributions are  $(\zeta_1 + \zeta_2)$ -close in statistical distance:

$$\mathsf{View}(\mathbf{P}, \mathbf{V}, \mathcal{D}_2, \mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{y}_1, \mathbf{w}_1) \quad \text{and} \quad \mathbf{S}^{\mathbf{y}_1, \mathcal{D}_2}(\mathbf{x}_1),$$

Note that  $\mathsf{View}(\mathbf{P}, \mathbf{V}, \mathcal{D}_2, \mathsf{x}_1, \mathsf{y}_1, \mathsf{w}_1)$  is equivalent to the following experiment.

 $\mathsf{View}(\mathbf{P}, \mathbf{V}, \mathcal{D}_2, \mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{y}_1, \mathbf{w}_1)$ :

- 1. Compute  $(\mathbf{z}_2, \mathbf{y}_2, \mathbf{w}_2) \xleftarrow{\mathsf{view}_1} \langle \mathbf{P}_1, \mathbf{V}_1 \rangle (\mathbf{z}_1, \mathbf{y}_1, \mathbf{w}_1)$  where  $\mathsf{view}_1$  is  $\mathbf{V}_1$ 's view in the interaction.
- 2. Compute  $(x_3, y_3, w_3) \stackrel{\mathsf{view}_2}{\longleftarrow} \langle \mathbf{P}_2, \mathbf{V}_2 \rangle (x_2, y_2, w_2)$  where  $\mathsf{view}_2$  is  $\mathbf{V}_2$ 's view in the interaction.
- 3. Compute  $\operatorname{out}_2 \leftarrow \mathcal{D}_2^{y_3}(z_3)$ .
- 4. Let view be V's view obtained by combining view<sub>1</sub> and view<sub>2</sub>.
- 5. Output (view,  $out_2$ ).

We rely on a two-step hybrid argument. For that, we define a hybrid simulator  $\tilde{\mathbf{S}}$ .

{33}------------------------------------------------

 $\widetilde{\mathbf{S}}^{\mathbf{y}_1,\mathcal{D}_2}(\mathbf{x}_1,\mathbf{w}_1)$ :

- 1. Define the algorithm  $\mathcal{D}_1^{y_2}(\mathbf{x}_2) \coloneqq \mathbf{S}_2^{y_2,\mathcal{D}_2}(\mathbf{x}_2)$ .
- 2. Compute and output  $View(\mathbf{P}_1, \mathbf{V}_1, \mathcal{D}_1, \mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{y}_1, \mathbf{w}_1)$ .

We consider the two steps of the hybrid argument.

- $\Delta(\mathsf{View}(\mathbf{P}, \mathbf{V}, \mathcal{D}_2, \mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{y}_1, \mathbf{w}_1), \widetilde{\mathbf{S}}^{\mathbf{y}_1, \mathcal{D}_2}(\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{w}_1)) \leq \zeta_2$ . By  $\mathsf{IOR}_2$ 's HVZK property, since  $\mathcal{D}_2 \in \mathfrak{D}$ , the statistical distance of  $\mathsf{View}(\mathbf{P}_2, \mathbf{V}_2, \mathcal{D}_2, \mathbf{x}_2, \mathbf{y}_2, \mathbf{w}_2)$  and  $\mathbf{S}_2^{\mathbf{y}_2, \mathcal{D}_2}(\mathbf{x}_2)$  is at most  $\zeta_2$ . Moreover, first running  $\langle \mathbf{P}_1, \mathbf{V}_1 \rangle (\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{y}_1, \mathbf{w}_1)$  and then  $\mathbf{S}_2^{\mathbf{y}_2, \mathcal{D}_2}(\mathbf{x}_2)$  is equivalent to  $\mathsf{View}(\mathbf{P}_1, \mathbf{V}_1, \mathcal{D}_1, \mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{y}_1, \mathbf{w}_1)$ , which is the experiment in  $\widetilde{\mathbf{S}}^{\mathbf{y}_1, \mathcal{D}_2}(\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{w}_1)$ .
- $\Delta(\widetilde{\mathbf{S}}^{y_1,\mathcal{D}_2}(\mathbf{z}_1,\mathbf{w}_1), \mathbf{S}^{y_1,\mathcal{D}_2}(\mathbf{z}_1)) \leq \zeta_1$ . Since  $\mathcal{D}_2 \in \mathfrak{D}$  we know that  $\mathcal{D}_1 \in \mathfrak{D}_{\mathbf{S}_2}$  (by definition of  $\mathcal{D}_1$ ). By IOR<sub>1</sub>'s HVZK property, the statistical distance of  $\mathsf{View}(\mathbf{P}_1,\mathbf{V}_1,\mathcal{D}_1,\mathbf{z}_1,\mathbf{y}_1,\mathbf{w}_1)$  and  $\mathbf{S}_1^{y_1,\mathcal{D}_1}(\mathbf{z}_1)$  is at most  $\zeta_1$ .

We conclude that

$$\begin{split} & \Delta(\mathsf{View}(\mathbf{P}, \mathbf{V}, \mathcal{D}_2, \mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{y}_1, \mathbf{w}_1), \mathbf{S}^{\mathbf{y}_1, \mathcal{D}_2}(\mathbf{x}_1)) \\ & \leq \Delta(\mathsf{View}(\mathbf{P}, \mathbf{V}, \mathcal{D}_2, \mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{y}_1, \mathbf{w}_1), \widetilde{\mathbf{S}}^{\mathbf{y}_1, \mathcal{D}_2}(\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{w}_1)) + \Delta(\widetilde{\mathbf{S}}^{\mathbf{y}_1, \mathcal{D}_2}(\mathbf{x}_1, \mathbf{w}_1), \mathbf{S}^{\mathbf{y}_1, \mathcal{D}_2}(\mathbf{x}_1)) \\ & \leq \zeta_1 + \zeta_2 \,. \end{split}$$

The simulator is polynomial-time as a composition of polynomial-time algorithms, and its number of queries to  $y_1$  is exactly that of  $S_1$ , i.e., at most b.

We introduce the class of query-bounded distinguishers, which are distinguishers that make a bounded number of queries to the implicit instance (and are otherwise unbounded). We only consider distinguishers  $\mathcal{D}$  that are *non-adaptive*, i.e.,  $\mathcal{D}$  is specified by a pair  $(\mathcal{D}_0, \mathcal{D}_1)$  such that

$$\forall \, \mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y} : \, \mathcal{D}^{\mathbf{y}}(\mathbf{x}) = \left[ \begin{array}{l} (\mathsf{st}, S) \leftarrow \mathcal{D}_0(\mathbf{x}) \\ \mathcal{D}_1(\mathsf{st}, \mathbf{y}[S]) \end{array} \right] \, .$$

<span id="page-33-0"></span>**Definition 4.7.** Let  $t \in \mathbb{N}$ . We define the class of query-bounded distinguishers  $\mathfrak{D}^{\leq t}$  as the set of algorithms that perform at most t (non-adaptive) queries to their implicit input.

Multiple implicit instances. Many IORs that we consider in this work have as target relations where the implicit instance can be decomposed in a number of oracles. When considering honest-verifier zero-knowledge against query-bounded adversaries, we write  $\mathfrak{D}^{\leq t_1} \times \cdots \times \mathfrak{D}^{\leq t_n}$  for the class of query-bounded distinguishers that query the *i*-th oracle in the implicit instance at most  $t_i$  times. Alternatively, we let  $\mathbf{t} = (t_1, \dots, t_n)$  and write  $\mathfrak{D}^{\leq t}$  for conciseness. For the notions of honest-verifier zero-knowledge that we consider this suffices and we abuse notation by simulating the view of each distinguisher individually.

{34}------------------------------------------------

# <span id="page-34-0"></span>5 Succinct linear forms

We define a notion of succinct linear forms. These capture the fact that some linear claims can be succinctly described and efficiently computed from such succinct descriptions.

**Definition 5.1.** A succinct linear form sl is a map that takes as input a state st and outputs a matrix  $sl(st) \in \mathbb{F}^{t \times n}$ ; we use the notation  $sl \in \langle \mathbb{F}^{t \times n} |$  to indicate the dimensions of the matrix. We write  $t_{sl}$  for the maximum number (over the choice of st) of field operations for computing sl(st).

We define a few succinct linear form that we use.

**Definition 5.2.** The **identity linear form** is the linear form  $\mathsf{sl}_{id} \in \langle \mathbb{F}^{t \times n} | \text{ that takes as input a state } \boldsymbol{V} \in \mathbb{F}^{t \times n} \text{ and outputs } \boldsymbol{V} \text{ (i.e., } \mathsf{sl}_{id} \text{ is the identity map). Note that } t_{\mathsf{sl}_{id}} = 0.$ 

**Definition 5.3.** Let  $ze: D_{ze} \to \mathbb{F}^{t \times n}$  be a zero-evader. The ze-linear form is the linear form  $sl_{ze} \in \langle \mathbb{F}^{t \times n} | \ that \ takes \ as \ input \ a \ state \ \rho \in D_{ze} \ and \ outputs \ ze(\rho)$ . Note that  $t_{sl_{ze}} = teval(ze)$ .

Succinct linear forms can be combined and operated on.

**Definition 5.4.** The scalar multiplied linear form for  $\mathsf{sl} \in \langle \mathbb{F}^{t \times n} | is the linear form <math>\times (\mathsf{sl}) \in \langle \mathbb{F}^{t \times n} | mapping (\mathsf{st}, \varepsilon \in \mathbb{F}) to \varepsilon \cdot \mathsf{sl}(\mathsf{st}).$  Note that  $t_{\times (\mathsf{sl})} \leq t_{\mathsf{sl}} + O(t \cdot n).$ 

**Definition 5.5.** For  $k \in \mathbb{N}$ , the k-zero-padded linear form for  $\mathsf{sl} \in \langle \mathbb{F}^{t \times n} | \text{ is the linear form } 0^k(\mathsf{sl}) \in \langle \mathbb{F}^{t \times (n+k)} | \text{ mapping } \mathsf{st } \text{ to } [\mathsf{sl}(\mathsf{st}), 0^{t \times k}]. \text{ Note that } t_{0^k(\mathsf{sl})} \leq t_{\mathsf{sl}}.$ 

 $\begin{array}{lll} \textbf{Definition 5.6.} \ \ Let \ \mathsf{sl}_1, \dots, \mathsf{sl}_k \in \langle \mathbb{F}^{t \times n}| \ \ and \ \ let \ \mathsf{ze} \colon D_{\mathsf{ze}} \to \mathbb{F}^k \ \ be \ \ a \ \ zero\text{-}evader. \ \ The \ \ \mathsf{ze}\text{-}\mathbf{batched} \\ \textbf{linear form } \ \mathsf{sl}[\mathsf{ze}, (\mathsf{sl}_1, \dots, \mathsf{sl}_k)] \in \langle \mathbb{F}^{t \times n}| \ \ maps \ (\mathsf{st}_1, \dots, \mathsf{st}_k, \rho) \ \ to \ \sum_{i \in [k]} \mathsf{ze}(\rho)_i \cdot \mathsf{sl}_i(\mathsf{st}_i). \ \ Note \ \ that \\ t_{\mathsf{sl}[\mathsf{ze}, (\mathsf{sl}_1, \dots, \mathsf{sl}_k)]} \leq \mathsf{teval}(\mathsf{ze}) + \sum_{i \in [k]} t_{\mathsf{sl}_i} + O(k \cdot t \cdot n). \end{array}$ 

The most important operation that we apply on succinct linear form is that of "fixing variables". This operation takes a succinct linear form (say of size  $2^n$ ) and a vector of field elements  $\alpha \in \mathbb{F}^i$  (where  $i \leq n$ ), and outputs a succinct linear form of size  $2^{n-i}$ , in a way that is compatible with the folding operation in Definition 3.24.

**Definition 5.7.** Let  $i \in \mathbb{N}$  and  $\mathsf{sl} \in \langle \mathbb{F}^{t \times 2^n} |$ . The *i*-fixing linear form associated to  $\mathsf{sl}$  is the linear form  $\odot_i \mathsf{sl} \in \langle \mathbb{F}^{t \times 2^{n-i}} |$  that maps  $(\mathsf{st}, \boldsymbol{\gamma} \in \mathbb{F}^i)$  to  $\boldsymbol{V}' \in \mathbb{F}^{t \times 2^{n-i}}$  defined as follows:

- 1. Let  $V = \operatorname{sl}(\operatorname{st}) \in \mathbb{F}^{t \times 2^n}$
- 2. For each  $row v_j \in \mathbb{F}^{2^n}$  of V, set  $v_j' := \mathsf{Fold}(v_j, \gamma) \in \mathbb{F}^{2^{n-i}}$  as in Definition 3.24 i.e.

$$\forall \, \boldsymbol{c} \in \{0,1\}^{n-i}, \; \boldsymbol{v}_j'[\boldsymbol{c}] \coloneqq \sum_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^i} \operatorname{eq}(\boldsymbol{b}, \boldsymbol{\gamma}) \cdot \boldsymbol{v}_j[\boldsymbol{b}, \boldsymbol{c}] \, .$$

3. let  $\mathbf{V}' \in \mathbb{F}^{t \times 2^{n-i}}$  to be the matrix whose rows are  $\mathbf{v}'_j$ . Note that  $t_{\odot_i \text{sl}} \leq t_{\text{sl}} + O(t \cdot n)$ .

The fixing operation is used to reduce the instance size, and we illustrate this via an example. Consider the linear form  $\mathsf{sl}_{\mathsf{ze}_{\mathsf{eq}}}$  associated to the zero evader  $\mathsf{ze}_{\mathsf{eq}}$  mapping  $\boldsymbol{\alpha} \mapsto (\mathsf{eq}(\boldsymbol{\alpha}, \boldsymbol{b}))_{\boldsymbol{b} \in \{0,1\}^n}$ . By [VSBW13],  $t_{\mathsf{sl}_{\mathsf{ze}_{\mathsf{eq}}}} = O(2^n)$ . Consider now  $\odot_n \mathsf{sl}_{\mathsf{ze}_{\mathsf{eq}}}$ , which (by definition) is the map that maps  $(\boldsymbol{\alpha}, \boldsymbol{\gamma}) \mapsto \mathsf{eq}(\boldsymbol{\alpha}, \boldsymbol{\gamma})$ . Note that  $t_{\odot_n \mathsf{sl}_{\mathsf{ze}_{\mathsf{eq}}}} \leq n$ , which is succinct in the original linear form size.

{35}------------------------------------------------

Following Remark 3.25, the fixing linear form is well-defined when part of the input state consists of indeterminates. For  $sl \in \langle \mathbb{F}^{2^n} |$ ,

<span id="page-35-0"></span>
$$(\odot_{i}\mathsf{sl})(\mathsf{st},(\mathbf{X}_{1},\ldots,\mathbf{X}_{i})) = \left(\sum_{\boldsymbol{b}\in\{0,1\}^{i}}\mathsf{eq}(\boldsymbol{b},\mathbf{X}_{1},\ldots,\mathbf{X}_{i})\cdot\mathsf{sl}(\mathsf{st})[\boldsymbol{b},\boldsymbol{c}]\right)_{\boldsymbol{c}\in\{0,1\}^{n-i}}; \tag{8}$$

and this extends naturally to  $sl \in \langle \mathbb{F}^{t \times 2^n} | .$ 

We introduce the main relation that we use in this work.

#### <span id="page-35-1"></span>**Definition 5.8.** *Let:*

- $n \in \mathbb{N}$ ;

- $\mathcal{C} \subseteq \Sigma^m$  with a zero-knowledge encoding  $\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}} \colon \mathbb{F}^{\ell} \times \mathbb{F}^r \to \Sigma^m$ ;  $\mathcal{C}_{\operatorname{zk}} \subseteq \Sigma^{m_{\operatorname{zk}}}_{\operatorname{zk}}$  with a zero-knowledge encoding  $\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{\operatorname{zk}}} \colon \mathbb{F}^{\ell_{\operatorname{zk}}} \times \mathbb{F}^{r_{\operatorname{zk}}} \to \Sigma^{m_{\operatorname{zk}}}_{\operatorname{zk}}$ ;  $\operatorname{sl} = (\operatorname{sl}, (\operatorname{sl}_i)_{i \in [n]}, (\operatorname{sl}_{\circ,i})_{i \in [n]})$  where  $\operatorname{sl} \in \langle \mathbb{F}^{\ell} |, \operatorname{sl}_i \in \langle \mathbb{F}^{\ell_{\operatorname{zk}}} |, \operatorname{sl}_{\circ,i} \in \langle \mathbb{F}^{t_i \times \ell_{\operatorname{zk}}} |$ . Define:

$$\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}} \coloneqq \left\{ \begin{pmatrix} \mathbf{x} = ((\mu,\mathsf{st},(\mathsf{st}_i)_{i \in [n]}), ((\boldsymbol{\mu}_i,\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i})_{i \in [n]})), \\ \mathbf{y} = (f,(\xi_i)_{i \in [n]}), \\ \mathbf{w} = (\boldsymbol{f},\boldsymbol{r},(\boldsymbol{\xi}_i,\boldsymbol{r}_i)_{i \in [n]}) \end{pmatrix} \right. \begin{pmatrix} f = \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(\boldsymbol{f},\boldsymbol{r}) \\ \forall \, i \in [n] \colon \, \xi_i = \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}}}(\boldsymbol{\xi}_i,\boldsymbol{r}_i) \\ \forall \, i \in [n] \colon \, \mathsf{sl}_{\circ,i}(\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}) \cdot \boldsymbol{\xi}_i = \boldsymbol{\mu}_i \\ \langle \boldsymbol{f},\mathsf{sl}(\mathsf{st}) \rangle + \sum_{i \in [n]} \langle \boldsymbol{\xi}_i,\mathsf{sl}_i(\mathsf{st}_i) \rangle = \mu \end{pmatrix}.$$

For every  $\delta, \delta_{\mathsf{zk}} \in (0,1)$  we define a corresponding relaxed relation for proximity testing:

$$\tilde{\mathcal{R}}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}}^{\delta,\delta_{\mathsf{zk}}} \coloneqq \left\{ \ (\mathtt{x},\mathtt{y},(\mathtt{w},\bar{\mathtt{y}})) \ \middle| \ \Delta(\mathtt{y},\bar{\mathtt{y}}) \leq (\delta,\delta_{\mathtt{zk}},\ldots,\delta_{\mathtt{zk}}) \land (\mathtt{x},\bar{\mathtt{y}},\mathtt{w}) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}} \ \right\} \, .$$

{36}------------------------------------------------

#### <span id="page-36-0"></span>Zero-knowledge sumcheck IOR 6

We formally describe and analyze our zero-knowledge sumcheck IOR. First, we introduce some notation for the univariate evaluation zero-evader.

**Definition 6.1.** For  $n \in \mathbb{N}$ ,  $\mathsf{ze}_n^{\star} \colon \mathbb{F} \to \mathbb{F}^n$  is the zero-evader mapping  $\rho \mapsto (1, \rho, \dots, \rho^{n-1})$ , which has error  $\frac{n-1}{|\mathbb{F}|}$ .

<span id="page-36-2"></span>**Theorem 6.2.** Consider the following ingredients:

- $n \in \mathbb{N}$  be a number of functions;
- $k \in \mathbb{N}$  be a folding parameter;
- $\mathcal{C} \subseteq \Sigma^m$  with a zero-knowledge encoding  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}} \colon \mathbb{F}^\ell \times \mathbb{F}^r \to \Sigma^m$ ;
