(\mu_{i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}}, \\ & (\mu_{i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}}, \\ & (\mu_{i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}}, \\ & (\mu_{i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}}, \\ & (\mu_{i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}}, \\ & (\mu_{i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}}, \\ & (\mu_{i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}}, \\ & (\mu_{i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out$$

For every  $\delta, \delta_{\mathsf{zk}} \in (0,1)$  we define a corresponding relaxed relation for proximity testing:

$$\tilde{\mathcal{R}}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathrm{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}},\mathcal{C}_{\mathrm{out}}^{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}}^{\delta,\delta_{\mathsf{zk}}} \coloneqq \left\{ \begin{array}{l} (\mathbf{x},\mathbf{y},(\mathbf{w},\bar{\mathbf{y}})) \end{array} \right| \quad \frac{\Delta(\mathbf{y},\bar{\mathbf{y}}) \leq (\delta,\underbrace{\delta_{\mathsf{zk}},\ldots,\delta_{\mathsf{zk}}}_{n_{\mathsf{in}}+n_{\mathsf{out}}}) \wedge (\mathbf{x},\bar{\mathbf{y}},\mathbf{w}) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathrm{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}},\mathcal{C}_{\mathrm{out}}^{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}} \\ \\ n_{\mathsf{in}}+n_{\mathsf{out}} \ terms \end{array} \right\}.$$

This definition is a minor extension of Definition 5.8 that we use to illustrate the different roles each zero-knowledge code plays in the reduction. By padding the shorter mask messages (and the corresponding succinct-evaluation states/targets) with zeros to a common length, both mask codes (the inner and outer codes) can be the same code, returning to the relation in Definition 5.8.

**Definition 11.2.** The relation  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{R1CS}}$  is the set of all pairs  $((\mathbb{F}, n_0, \ell, A, B, C, v), w)$  where  $\mathbb{F}$  is a finite field,  $n_0, \ell \in \mathbb{N}$ , A, B, C are  $(\ell + n_0) \times (\ell + n_0)$  matrices over  $\mathbb{F}$ ,  $v \in \mathbb{F}^{n_0}$ , and  $w \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell}$ , such that for all  $i \in [(\ell + n_0)]$ :

$$\left(\sum_{j=1}^{(\ell+n_0)} A_{i,j} \cdot z_j\right) \cdot \left(\sum_{j=1}^{(\ell+n_0)} B_{i,j} \cdot z_j\right) = \left(\sum_{j=1}^{(\ell+n_0)} C_{i,j} \cdot z_j\right)$$

for  $z := (v, w) \in \mathbb{F}^{(\ell+n_0)}$ .

<span id="page-65-1"></span>**Theorem 11.3.** Assume  $\ell = n_0$  and that  $\ell$  is a power of 2.

- Let  $C \subseteq \Sigma^m \equiv (\mathbb{F}^{\iota})^m$  be a code with zero-knowledge encoding  $\operatorname{Enc}_C \colon \mathbb{F}^{\ell} \times \mathbb{F}^r \to \Sigma^m$ . Let  $C_{\operatorname{out}}^{\operatorname{zk}} \subseteq \Sigma_{\operatorname{zk}}^{m_{\operatorname{out}}^{\operatorname{zk}}} \equiv (\mathbb{F}^{\iota_{\operatorname{out}}^{\operatorname{zk}}})^{m_{\operatorname{out}}^{\operatorname{zk}}}$  be a code with zero-knowledge encoding  $\operatorname{Enc}_{C_{\operatorname{out}}^{\operatorname{zk}}} \colon \mathbb{F}^{\ell_{\operatorname{out}}^{\operatorname{zk}}} \times \mathbb{F}^{r_{\operatorname{out}}^{\operatorname{zk}}} \to$  $\Sigma_{\sf zk}^{m^{\sf zk}}$  .

{66}------------------------------------------------

- Let  $C_{\mathrm{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}} \subseteq \Sigma_{\mathsf{zk}}^{m_{\mathrm{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}}} \equiv (\mathbb{F}^{\iota_{\mathrm{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}}})^{m_{\mathrm{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}}}$  be a code with zero-knowledge encoding  $\mathsf{Enc}_{C_{\mathrm{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}}} \colon \mathbb{F}^{\ell_{\mathrm{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}}} \times \mathbb{F}^{r_{\mathrm{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}}} \to \Sigma_{\mathsf{zk}}^{m_{\mathrm{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}}}$ .
- Let  $ze: D_{ze} \to \mathbb{F}^3$  be a zero-evader with error  $\varepsilon_{zero}$ .

Define:

$$\begin{split} \mathbf{sl} &\coloneqq \left( \mathsf{sl}, \left( \mathsf{sl}_{M,i}^\mathsf{in} \right)_{M \in \{A,B,C\}, i \in [\log \ell + 1]}, \left( \mathsf{sl}_{\circ,M,i}^\mathsf{in} \right)_{M \in \{A,B,C\}, i \in [\log \ell + 1]}, \left( \mathsf{sl}_{\circ,i}^\mathsf{out} \right)_{i \in [\log \ell + 1]} \right) \\ where & \mathsf{sl}_{\circ,M,i}^\mathsf{in} \coloneqq \left( \mathsf{sl}_\mathsf{id}, \mathsf{sl}_\mathsf{id} \right)^\mathsf{T}, \\ & \mathsf{sl}_{\circ,j}^\mathsf{out} \coloneqq \mathsf{sl}_\mathsf{id}, \\ & \mathsf{sl} \coloneqq \mathsf{sl} [\mathsf{ze}, \left( \mathsf{sl}_\mathsf{id}, \mathsf{sl}_\mathsf{id}, \mathsf{sl}_\mathsf{id} \right)], \\ & \mathsf{sl}_{M,i}^\mathsf{in} \coloneqq \mathsf{sl}_\mathsf{id}. \end{split}$$

There exists an IOR from  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{R1CS}}$  to  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}^{\mathsf{zk}}_{\mathsf{in}},\mathcal{C}^{\mathsf{zk}}_{\mathsf{out}},\mathsf{sl}}$  with the following properties.

- Round complexity:  $O(\log \ell)$ .
- Prover communication (in field elements):  $m \cdot \iota + 3(\log \ell + 1) \cdot m_{\text{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}} \cdot \iota_{\text{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}} + (\log \ell + 1) \cdot m_{\text{out}}^{\mathsf{zk}} \cdot \iota_{\text{out}}^{\mathsf{zk}} + (\log \ell + 1) \cdot (\ell_{\text{out}}^{\mathsf{zk}} + 1) + 4$ . Of these,
  - $-m \cdot \iota$  are sent as an oracle over alphabet  $\Sigma$ ,
  - $-3(\log\ell+1)\cdot m_{\rm in}^{\sf zk}\cdot \iota_{\rm in}^{\sf zk}+(\log\ell+1)\cdot m_{\rm out}^{\sf zk}\cdot \iota_{\rm out}^{\sf zk}\ are\ sent\ as\ mask\ oracles\ over\ alphabet\ \Sigma_{\sf zk}\ (consisting\ of\ 3(\log\ell+1)\ inner-mask\ oracles\ and\ \log\ell+1\ outer-mask\ oracles),\ and$
  - $(\log \ell + 1) \cdot (\ell_{out}^{zk} + 1) + 4$  are sent as non-oracle messages.
- Verifier queries: none (a queryless IOR).
- Prover time (in field operations):  $O(\operatorname{tenc}(\mathcal{C}) + \log \ell \cdot (\operatorname{tenc}(\mathcal{C}_{\operatorname{out}}^{\operatorname{zk}}) + \operatorname{tenc}(\mathcal{C}_{\operatorname{in}}^{\operatorname{zk}})) + \ell)$ .
- Verifier time (in field operations):  $O(\log \ell \cdot \ell_{\text{out}}^{\text{zk}} + \ell)$ .
- Round-by-round security: For every  $\delta, \delta_{\mathsf{zk}} \in (0,1)$ , Construction 11.4 has RBR knowledge soundness with relaxation  $(\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{R1CS}}, \tilde{\mathcal{R}}^{\delta, \delta_{\mathsf{zk}}}_{\mathcal{C}, \mathcal{C}^{\mathsf{zk}}_{\mathsf{in}}, \mathcal{C}^{\mathsf{zk}}_{\mathsf{out}}, \mathsf{sl}})$  and errors

$$\left(\frac{(\ell_{\text{out}}^{\mathsf{zk}} + 1) \cdot |\Lambda(\mathcal{C}, \delta)| \cdot |\Lambda|}{|\mathbb{F}|}, \left(\frac{|\Lambda(\mathcal{C}, \delta)| \cdot |\Lambda| \cdot \ell_{\text{out}}^{\mathsf{zk}}}{|\mathbb{F}|}\right)_{j \in [\log \ell + 1]}, \frac{|\Lambda(\mathcal{C}, \delta)| \cdot |\Lambda| \cdot \ell_{\text{out}}^{\mathsf{zk}}}{|\mathbb{F}| - 2}, |\Lambda(\mathcal{C}, \delta)| \cdot |\Lambda| \cdot \varepsilon_{\mathsf{zero}}\right)$$

where  $|\Lambda| := |\Lambda((\mathcal{C}_{\text{out}}^{\mathsf{zk}})^{\equiv \log \ell + 1}, \delta_{\mathsf{zk}})| \cdot |\Lambda((\mathcal{C}_{\text{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}})^{\equiv 3(\log \ell + 1)}, \delta_{\mathsf{zk}})|$ . The total extraction time is  $O(\log \ell \cdot (\mathsf{cor}(\mathcal{C}_{\text{out}}^{\mathsf{zk}}) + \mathsf{tcor}(\mathcal{C}_{\text{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}})))$ .

• **Zero-knowledge:** If  $\operatorname{char}(\mathbb{F}) \neq 2$ ,  $\ell_{\operatorname{out}}^{\operatorname{zk}} \geq 2 \cdot \ell_{\operatorname{in}}^{\operatorname{zk}}$ ,  $\ell_{\operatorname{in}}^{\operatorname{zk}} \geq 4$ , and  $\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}$ ,  $\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{\operatorname{out}}^{\operatorname{zk}}}$ ,  $\operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{\operatorname{in}}^{\operatorname{zk}}}$  are t-query zero-knowledge encodings with error  $\zeta$ , then the reduction is HVZK for  $\mathfrak{D}^{\leq t}$  with error  $(4\log \ell + 5) \cdot \zeta$  (and no query complexity as  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{R1CS}}$  has no implicit instance).

<span id="page-66-0"></span>Construction 11.4. Let  $\mathbb{F}$  be a field. Consider the following ingredients and notation.

- We assume that  $\ell$  is a power of 2. Sometimes we refer to elements of  $\{0,1\}^{\log \ell}$  as elements in  $[\ell]$ . Implicitly, we assume a bijection between the two domains and use it as appropriate to translate between them. We also identify  $\{0,1\}^{\log \ell+1}$  with  $[(\ell+n_0)]$  (recall that we assume  $(\ell+n_0)=2\ell$ ).
- We let  $\hat{f}_v \in \mathbb{F}^{<2}[X_1, \dots, X_{\log \ell}]$  be the unique multilinear polynomial satisfying  $\hat{f}_v(b) = v(b)$  for every  $b \in \{0, 1\}^{\log \ell}$ .
- For every  $M \in \{A, B, C\}$  let  $\hat{f}_M \in \mathbb{F}^{<2}[X_1, \dots, X_{\log \ell+1}, Y_1, \dots, Y_{\log \ell+1}]$  be the unique multilinear polynomial such that

$$\hat{f}_M(a,b) = M[a,b]$$
 for all  $a, b \in \{0,1\}^{\log \ell + 1}$ .

{67}------------------------------------------------

**Inputs.** The honest prover receives  $((\mathbb{F}, n_0, \ell, A, B, C, v), w) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{R1CS}}$  and the verifier receives  $(\mathbb{F}, n_0, \ell, A, B, C, v)$ . Define  $\mathsf{st}_1 \coloneqq (1, 0^{\ell_{\mathrm{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}} - 1})$  and  $\mathsf{st}_2 \coloneqq (0, 1, 0^{\ell_{\mathrm{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}} - 2})$ . **Interaction phase.** 

- 1. Witness hiding masks. For each  $M \in \{A, B, C\}$ ,  $i \in [\log \ell + 1]$  send  $\bar{\xi}_{M,i} \in \Sigma_{zk}^{m_{in}^{zk}}$ . In the honest case, the prover samples a coefficient vector  $\bar{\xi}_{M,i} \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell_{in}^{zk}}$  (equivalently, a polynomial in  $\mathbb{F}^{<\ell_{in}^{zk}}[X]$ ), conditioned on  $\bar{\xi}_{M,i}(0) = \bar{\xi}_{M,i}(1) = 0$ , samples  $\bar{r}_{M,i} \in \mathbb{F}^{r_{in}^{zk}}$ , and sends  $\bar{\xi}_{M,i} := \operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{in}^{zk}}(\bar{\xi}_{M,i}, \bar{r}_{M,i})$ . Succinct evaluation: define  $\mu_{M,i}^{in} = (0,0)^{\mathsf{T}}$  and  $\operatorname{st}_{\circ,M,i}^{in} = (\operatorname{st}_1,\operatorname{st}_2)^{\mathsf{T}}$ .
- 2. **Send witness encoding.** The prover sends a  $f \in \Sigma^m$ . In the honest case, the prover sets  $\mathbf{f} := w$ , samples  $\mathbf{r} \in \mathbb{F}^r$ , and sends  $f := \operatorname{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}(\mathbf{f}, \mathbf{r})$ , where  $\hat{f}_w$  is the unique multilinear extension of w, i.e.

$$\hat{f}_w(b) = w(b)$$
  $\forall b \in \{0, 1\}^{\log \ell}$ .

- 3. **Sumcheck.** The prover and verifier engage in a sumcheck protocol. In the honest case, the prover defines the following polynomials:
  - Boolean extension of the witness/input:

$$\hat{f}_z(\mathbf{X}_1, \dots, \mathbf{X}_{\log \ell+1}) \coloneqq (1 - \mathbf{X}_{\log \ell+1}) \cdot \hat{f}_v(\mathbf{X}_1, \dots, \mathbf{X}_{\log \ell}) + \mathbf{X}_{\log \ell+1} \cdot \hat{f}_w(\mathbf{X}_1, \dots, \mathbf{X}_{\log \ell})$$

• Constraint polynomial: Define:

$$\begin{split} \hat{g}(\mathtt{X}) \coloneqq \left( \sum_{b \in \{0,1\}^{\log \ell + 1}} \hat{f}_A(\mathtt{X}, b) \hat{f}_z(b) + \left( \sum_{i=1}^{\log \ell + 1} \bar{\boldsymbol{\xi}}_{A,i}(\mathtt{X}_i) \right) \right) \cdot \left( \sum_{b \in \{0,1\}^{\log \ell + 1}} \hat{f}_B(\mathtt{X}, b) \hat{f}_z(b) + \left( \sum_{i=1}^{\log \ell + 1} \bar{\boldsymbol{\xi}}_{B,i}(\mathtt{X}_i) \right) \right) \\ - \left( \sum_{b \in \{0,1\}^{\log \ell + 1}} \hat{f}_C(\mathtt{X}, b) \hat{f}_z(b) + \left( \sum_{i=1}^{\log \ell + 1} \bar{\boldsymbol{\xi}}_{C,i}(\mathtt{X}_i) \right) \right). \end{split}$$

4. Sumcheck masks. For each  $j \in \{1, ..., \log \ell + 1\}$  send  $\xi_j \in \Sigma_{zk}^{m_{\text{out}}^{zk}}$ , and send one value  $\tilde{\mu}$ . In the honest case, for each j the prover samples a coefficient vector  $\boldsymbol{\xi}_j \in \mathbb{F}^{\ell_{\text{out}}^{zk}}$  (equivalently, a polynomial in  $\mathbb{F}^{<\ell_{\text{out}}^{zk}}[X]$ ), samples  $\boldsymbol{r}_j \in \mathbb{F}^{r_{\text{out}}^{zk}}$ , and sends  $\xi_j \coloneqq \mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}_{\text{out}}^{zk}}(\boldsymbol{\xi}_j, \boldsymbol{r}_j)$ . Then it sends

$$\tilde{\mu} \coloneqq \sum_{a \in \{0,1\}^{\log \ell + 1}} \left( \xi_1(a_1) + \dots + \xi_{\log \ell + 1}(a_{\log \ell + 1}) \right) .$$

- 5. Sumcheck combining randomness. The verifier sends  $\varepsilon \leftarrow \mathbb{F}$  and  $r \leftarrow \mathbb{F}^{\log \ell + 1}$ .
- 6. **Sumcheck messages.** The prover and the verifier engage with a sumcheck protocol on the claim  $\sum_{a \in \{0,1\}^{\log \ell+1}} \varepsilon \cdot \hat{g}(a) \cdot \operatorname{eq}(\boldsymbol{r}, a) + \left(\sum_{i=1}^{\log \ell+1} \boldsymbol{\xi}_i(a_i)\right) = \tilde{\mu}. \text{ Let } \alpha \coloneqq \emptyset. \text{ For each } j \in \{1, \dots, \log \ell+1\}:$ 
  - The prover sends a polynomial  $\hat{h}_j \in \mathbb{F}^{<\ell_{\text{out}}^{\text{zk}}}[X]$ . In the honest case,  $\hat{h}_j(X)$  is equal to the following:

$$\sum_{a \in \{0,1\}^{\log \ell + 1 - j}} \varepsilon \cdot \hat{g}(\alpha, \mathbf{X}, a) \cdot \operatorname{eq}(\boldsymbol{r}, (\alpha, \mathbf{X}, a)) + \left(\sum_{i=1}^{j-1} \boldsymbol{\xi}_i(\alpha_i)\right) + \boldsymbol{\xi}_j(\mathbf{X}) + \left(\sum_{i=j+1}^{\log \ell + 1} \boldsymbol{\xi}_i(a_i)\right)$$

{68}------------------------------------------------

where  $\hat{g}(\alpha, X, a)$  is:

$$\begin{split} &\hat{g}(\boldsymbol{\alpha}, \mathbf{X}, \boldsymbol{a}) \\ &\coloneqq \left( \sum_{b \in \{0,1\}^{\log \ell + 1}} \hat{f}_A((\boldsymbol{\alpha}, \mathbf{X}, \boldsymbol{a}), b) \hat{f}_z(b) + \left( \left( \sum_{i=1}^{j-1} \bar{\boldsymbol{\xi}}_{A,i}(\boldsymbol{\alpha}_i) \right) + \bar{\boldsymbol{\xi}}_{A,j}(\mathbf{X}) + \left( \sum_{i=j+1}^{\log \ell + 1} \bar{\boldsymbol{\xi}}_{A,i}(\boldsymbol{a}_i) \right) \right) \right) \\ &\cdot \left( \sum_{b \in \{0,1\}^{\log \ell + 1}} \hat{f}_B((\boldsymbol{\alpha}, \mathbf{X}, \boldsymbol{a}), b) \hat{f}_z(b) + \left( \left( \sum_{i=1}^{j-1} \bar{\boldsymbol{\xi}}_{B,i}(\boldsymbol{\alpha}_i) \right) + \bar{\boldsymbol{\xi}}_{B,j}(\mathbf{X}) + \left( \sum_{i=j+1}^{\log \ell + 1} \bar{\boldsymbol{\xi}}_{B,i}(\boldsymbol{a}_i) \right) \right) \right) \\ &- \left( \sum_{b \in \{0,1\}^{\log \ell + 1}} \hat{f}_C((\boldsymbol{\alpha}, \mathbf{X}, \boldsymbol{a}), b) \hat{f}_z(b) + \left( \left( \sum_{i=1}^{j-1} \bar{\boldsymbol{\xi}}_{C,i}(\boldsymbol{\alpha}_i) \right) + \bar{\boldsymbol{\xi}}_{C,j}(\mathbf{X}) + \left( \sum_{i=j+1}^{\log \ell + 1} \bar{\boldsymbol{\xi}}_{C,i}(\boldsymbol{a}_i) \right) \right) \right) \end{split}$$

- The verifier checks that  $\sum_{a \in \{0,1\}} \hat{h}_j(a) = \hat{h}_{j-1}(\alpha_{j-1})$  (for j = 1, the verifier compares to  $\tilde{\mu}$ ).
- The verifier samples  $\alpha_j \leftarrow \mathbb{F}$  (except for the last round where  $\alpha_{\log \ell+1} \leftarrow \mathbb{F} \setminus \{0,1\}$ ) and updates  $\alpha \coloneqq (\alpha \| \alpha_j)$ .
- 7. Reveal outer masks evaluations. The prover sends  $\forall j \in \{1, ..., \log \ell + 1\}, \ \tilde{\mu}_j$ . In the honest case,  $\tilde{\mu}_j = \boldsymbol{\xi}_j(\alpha_j)$ .

Succinct evaluation: define  $\mu_j^{\text{out}} = \tilde{\mu}_j$  and  $\mathsf{st}_{\circ,j}^{\text{out}} = \mathsf{pow}(\alpha_j)$ .

<span id="page-68-1"></span>8. Sumcheck's final check. The prover sends  $v_M$  for every  $M \in \{A, B, C\}$ . In the honest case:

$$\begin{split} v_M &\coloneqq \sum_{b \in \{0,1\}^{\log \ell + 1}} \hat{f}_M(\alpha, b) \cdot \hat{f}_z(b) + \left(\sum_{i=1}^{\log \ell + 1} \bar{\boldsymbol{\xi}}_{M,i}(\alpha_i)\right) \\ &= \sum_{b \in \{0,1\}^{\log \ell}} \left(\hat{f}_M(\alpha, b, 0) \cdot \hat{f}_v(b) + \hat{f}_M(\alpha, b, 1) \cdot \hat{f}_w(b)\right) + 2 \cdot \left(\sum_{i=1}^{\log \ell + 1} \bar{\boldsymbol{\xi}}_{M,i}(\alpha_i)\right) \end{split}$$

The verifier checks that:

$$\varepsilon \cdot (v_A \cdot v_B - v_C) \cdot \operatorname{eq}(\boldsymbol{r}, \alpha) + \sum_{j=1}^{\log \ell + 1} \tilde{\mu}_j = \hat{h}_{\log \ell + 1}(\alpha_{\log \ell + 1})$$

Finally, the verifier computes:

$$u_M = \sum_{b \in \{0,1\}^{\log \ell}} \left( \hat{f}_M(\alpha, b, 0) \cdot \hat{f}_v(b) \right).$$

<span id="page-68-0"></span>9. **Joint constraint.** The verifier samples and sends  $\rho \leftarrow \mathbb{F}$ .

Succinct evaluation: Let  $\operatorname{st}_M = M$  for all  $M \in \{A, B, C\}$  (note that we abuse the notation but we mean that the state is the succinct description of the matrix) and  $\operatorname{sl}_M = \operatorname{sl}_{\operatorname{id}} {}^3$ . Set:  $\operatorname{sl} = \operatorname{sl}[\operatorname{ze}, (\operatorname{sl}_A, \operatorname{sl}_B, \operatorname{sl}_C)]$  and  $\operatorname{st} = (\operatorname{st}_A, \operatorname{st}_B, \operatorname{st}_C, \rho)$ . Define also, for each  $M \in \{A, B, C\}$  and  $i \in [\log \ell + 1]$ ,  $\operatorname{sl}_{M,i}^{\operatorname{in}} = \operatorname{sl}_{\operatorname{id}}$  and  $\operatorname{st}_{M,i}^{\operatorname{in}} = (\operatorname{pow}(\alpha_i), \operatorname{ze}(\rho)_M)$ , and  $\operatorname{set} \mu = \sum_{M \in \{A, B, C\}} \operatorname{ze}(\rho)_M \cdot (v_M - u_M)$  (where  $\operatorname{ze}(\rho)_M \in \mathbb{F}$  denotes the coordinate associated with  $M \in \{A, B, C\}$ ).

<sup>&</sup>lt;sup>3</sup>Note the slight abuse of notation where we treat the entire description of the matrix as the succinct description from which we compute the MLE as in the beginning of the construction.

{69}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-69-0"></span>10. **Output claim:** The prover and the verifier both implicitly define the output instance for  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C}_{\mathrm{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}},\mathcal{C}_{\mathrm{out}}^{\mathsf{zk}},\mathsf{sl}}$ :

$$\begin{split} \mathbf{x} &= ((\mu, \mathsf{st}, (\mathsf{st}_{M,i}^{\mathsf{in}})_{M \in \{A,B,C\}, i \in [\log \ell + 1]}), (\pmb{\mu}_{M,i}^{\mathsf{in}}, \mathsf{st}_{\circ,M,i}^{\mathsf{in}})_{M \in \{A,B,C\}, i \in [\log \ell + 1]}, (\mu_i^{\mathsf{out}}, \mathsf{st}_{\circ,i}^{\mathsf{out}})_{i \in [\log \ell + 1]}) \\ \mathbf{y} &= (f, (\bar{\xi}_{M,i})_{M \in \{A,B,C\}, i \in [\log \ell + 1]}, (\xi_i)_{i \in [\log \ell + 1]}). \end{split}$$

In the honest case, the prover also defines the witness:

$$\mathbb{W} = (\bm{f}, \bm{r}, (\bar{\bm{\xi}}_{M,i}, \bar{\bm{r}}_{M,i})_{M \in \{A,B,C\}, i \in [\log \ell + 1]}, (\bm{\xi}_i, \bm{r}_i)_{i \in [\log \ell + 1]}).$$

*Proof sketch of Theorem 11.3.* We sketch the proof for round-by-round knowledge soundness, and honest-verifier zero-knowledge.

Round-by-round knowledge soundness. Ignoring masks, the protocol follows the  $\Sigma$ -IOP analysis for  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{R1CS}}$  in [ACFY25]. The differences are the added inner/outer masking layers and the final ze-based joint constraint, and these are handled with the same tools as in Section 6. Writing

$$|\Lambda| \coloneqq |\Lambda((\mathcal{C}_{\mathrm{out}}^{\mathsf{zk}})^{\equiv \log \ell + 1}, \delta_{\mathsf{zk}})| \cdot |\Lambda((\mathcal{C}_{\mathrm{in}}^{\mathsf{zk}})^{\equiv 3(\log \ell + 1)}, \delta_{\mathsf{zk}})|$$

as in the theorem statement, the round-by-round error vector is

$$\left(\frac{(\ell_{\mathrm{out}}^{\mathsf{zk}} + 1) \cdot |\Lambda(\mathcal{C}, \delta)| \cdot |\Lambda|}{|\mathbb{F}|}, \left(\frac{|\Lambda(\mathcal{C}, \delta)| \cdot |\Lambda| \cdot \ell_{\mathrm{out}}^{\mathsf{zk}}}{|\mathbb{F}|}\right)_{j \in [\log \ell + 1]}, \frac{|\Lambda(\mathcal{C}, \delta)| \cdot |\Lambda| \cdot \ell_{\mathrm{out}}^{\mathsf{zk}}}{|\mathbb{F}| - 2}, |\Lambda(\mathcal{C}, \delta)| \cdot |\Lambda| \cdot \varepsilon_{\mathsf{zero}}\right).$$

The first coordinate corresponds to the initial masked-sumcheck consistency check, the vector coordinates correspond to the per-round sumcheck checks for rounds  $j \in [\log \ell + 1]$ , the third coordinate corresponds to the final check at  $\alpha_{\log \ell + 1} \in \mathbb{F} \setminus \{0, 1\}$  (hence denominator  $|\mathbb{F}| - 2$ ), and the fourth coordinate comes from the final random linear combination with ze in the joint constraint. Extraction time is  $O(\log \ell \cdot (\mathsf{tcor}(\mathcal{C}^{\mathsf{zk}}_{\mathrm{out}}) + \mathsf{tcor}(\mathcal{C}^{\mathsf{zk}}_{\mathrm{in}}))$ .

**Honest-verifier zero-knowledge.** Fix any  $\mathcal{D} \in \mathfrak{D}^{\leq t}$  and define a simulator for its view. Let  $\mathbf{S}_{\mathcal{C}^{\mathsf{zk}}_{\mathrm{out}}}$  and  $\mathbf{S}_{\mathcal{C}^{\mathsf{zk}}_{\mathrm{in}}}$  denote the simulators for the t-query zero-knowledge encodings of  $\mathcal{C}^{\mathsf{zk}}_{\mathrm{out}}$  and  $\mathcal{C}^{\mathsf{zk}}_{\mathrm{in}}$  (with error  $\zeta$ ). If  $\mathcal{D}$  also queries f, we use the analogous simulator  $\mathbf{S}_{\mathcal{C}}$  for  $\mathsf{Enc}_{\mathcal{C}}$ .

 $\mathbf{S}^{\mathcal{D}}(\mathbb{F}, \ell, A, B, C, v)$ :

- 1. Sample verifier randomness exactly as in the protocol:  $\varepsilon \leftarrow \mathbb{F}, \mathbf{r} \leftarrow \mathbb{F}^{\log \ell+1}, (\alpha_1, \dots, \alpha_{\log \ell}) \leftarrow \mathbb{F}^{\log \ell}, \alpha_{\log \ell+1} \leftarrow \mathbb{F} \setminus \{0,1\}, \rho \leftarrow \mathbb{F}.$
- <span id="page-69-2"></span>2. Sample  $(v_A, v_B, v_C) \leftarrow \mathbb{F}^3$ .
- <span id="page-69-1"></span>3. Sample  $(\tilde{\mu}, (\hat{h}_j)_{j \in [\log \ell + 1]}, (\tilde{\mu}_j)_{j \in [\log \ell + 1]})$  uniformly from  $\mathcal{T}(v_A, v_B, v_C)$  (defined below).
- 4. Compute the explicit output instance  $\mathbb{X}$  exactly as in Items 9 and 10, using the sampled values  $(v_A, v_B, v_C)$  and the public quantities  $(u_M)_{M \in \{A, B, C\}}$  computed from (A, B, C, v) and  $\alpha$ , and setting

$$\mu \coloneqq \sum_{M \in \{A,B,C\}} \mathsf{ze}(\rho)_M \cdot (v_M - u_M)$$
 .

(Also set all inner and outer targets/states as in the construction.)

- 5. Run  $\mathcal{D}$  on  $\mathbb{X}$ , giving it oracle access to  $\mathbb{Y} = (f, (\bar{\xi}_{M,i})_{M,i}, (\xi_j)_j)$ , and answer its (adaptive) oracle queries as follows:
  - (a) for each (M, i), answer queries to  $\bar{\xi}_{M,i}$  using  $\mathbf{S}_{\mathcal{C}_{in}^{\mathsf{zk}}}$ ;

{70}------------------------------------------------

- (b) for each j, answer queries to  $\xi_j$  using  $\mathbf{S}_{\mathcal{C}_{\text{out}}^{\text{zk}}}$ ;
- (c) answer queries to f using  $S_{\mathcal{C}}$ .
- 6. Output the simulated view (including  $\mathcal{D}$ 's query/answer transcript).

Fix verifier randomness  $(\varepsilon, \mathbf{r}, \alpha, \rho)$ . For any fixed triple  $(v_A, v_B, v_C) \in \mathbb{F}^3$ , define  $\mathcal{T}(v_A, v_B, v_C)$  to be the set of all tuples  $(\tilde{\mu}, (\hat{h}_j)_{j \in [\log \ell + 1]}, (\tilde{\mu}_j)_{j \in [\log \ell + 1]})$  that satisfy

$$\hat{h}_1(0) + \hat{h}_1(1) = \tilde{\mu},$$

$$\forall j \in \{2, \dots, \log \ell + 1\}: \quad \hat{h}_j(0) + \hat{h}_j(1) = \hat{h}_{j-1}(\alpha_{j-1}),$$

and

$$\varepsilon \cdot (v_A \cdot v_B - v_C) \cdot \operatorname{eq}(\boldsymbol{r}, \alpha) + \sum_{j=1}^{\log \ell + 1} \tilde{\mu}_j = \hat{h}_{\log \ell + 1}(\alpha_{\log \ell + 1}).$$

For fixed  $(v_A, v_B, v_C)$ , these are linear constraints in the coefficients of  $(\hat{h}_j)_j$  and in  $(\tilde{\mu}, \tilde{\mu}_1, \dots, \tilde{\mu}_{\log \ell+1})$ , hence  $\mathcal{T}(v_A, v_B, v_C)$  is the solution set of a linear system (i.e., an affine subspace). Also,  $\mathcal{T}(v_A, v_B, v_C) \neq \emptyset$  for every  $(v_A, v_B, v_C)$ : choose  $\hat{h}_1, \dots, \hat{h}_{\log \ell+1}$  recursively so that  $\hat{h}_j(0) + \hat{h}_j(1) = \hat{h}_{j-1}(\alpha_{j-1})$  (with  $\hat{h}_1(0) + \hat{h}_1(1) = \tilde{\mu}$ ), set  $\tilde{\mu}_1 = \dots = \tilde{\mu}_{\log \ell} = 0$ , and then define  $\tilde{\mu}_{\log \ell+1}$  to satisfy the final equation. Hence the simulator's sampling in Item 3 is well-defined.

We compare distributions in two claims, conditioning on verifier randomness.

Outer transcript claim. Fix the prover's inner-layer randomness (thus fixing  $\hat{g}$  and the values  $(v_A, v_B, v_C)$  from Item 8). In the honest execution, the tuple  $(\tilde{\mu}, (\hat{h}_j)_j, (\tilde{\mu}_j)_j)$  is an affine function of the outer-mask coefficients, and the verifier's checks are exactly the constraints defining  $\mathcal{T}(v_A, v_B, v_C)$ . Under  $\ell_{\text{out}}^{\text{zk}} \geq 2 \cdot \ell_{\text{in}}^{\text{zk}}$  and  $\ell_{\text{in}}^{\text{zk}} \geq 4$ , the same linear-algebra argument as in Lemma 6.4 gives: the image is  $\mathcal{T}(v_A, v_B, v_C)$ , and every point in  $\mathcal{T}(v_A, v_B, v_C)$  has the same number of preimages. Therefore uniform outer masks induce the uniform distribution on  $\mathcal{T}(v_A, v_B, v_C)$ , which matches the simulator's choice in Item 3.

Value claim. Using the decomposition in Item 8, in the honest execution

$$v_{M} = \sum_{b \in \{0,1\}^{\log \ell}} \left( \hat{f}_{M}(\alpha, b, 0) \cdot \hat{f}_{v}(b) + \hat{f}_{M}(\alpha, b, 1) \cdot \hat{f}_{w}(b) \right) + 2 \cdot \sum_{i=1}^{\log \ell + 1} \bar{\xi}_{M,i}(\alpha_{i}) \qquad (M \in \{A, B, C\}),$$

where each  $\bar{\xi}_{M,i}$  is sampled uniformly subject to  $\bar{\xi}_{M,i}(0) = \bar{\xi}_{M,i}(1) = 0$ . For each M, condition on all inner masks except  $\bar{\xi}_{M,\log \ell+1}$ , and rewrite

$$v_M = \operatorname{const}_M + 2 \cdot \bar{\xi}_{M,\log \ell+1}(\alpha_{\log \ell+1}).$$

Because  $\alpha_{\log \ell+1} \in \mathbb{F} \setminus \{0,1\}$  and  $\ell_{\text{in}}^{\text{zk}} \geq 4$ , the value  $\bar{\xi}_{M,\log \ell+1}(\alpha_{\log \ell+1})$  is uniform over  $\mathbb{F}$ ; and because  $\text{char}(\mathbb{F}) \neq 2$ , multiplication by 2 is a bijection on  $\mathbb{F}$ . Hence each  $v_M$  is uniform over  $\mathbb{F}$  conditioned on public randomness and witness, and by independence of the three masks at index  $\log \ell + 1$ , the triple  $(v_A, v_B, v_C)$  is jointly uniform over  $\mathbb{F}^3$  and independent of witness/input. This matches the simulator's choice in Item 2.

Combining the two claims, the sumcheck part is perfectly simulated.

Finally, we replace the honest encodings of the oracles  $(\bar{\xi}_{M,i})_{M,i}$ ,  $(\xi_j)_j$ , and f, one oracle at a time, by  $\mathbf{S}_{\mathcal{C}_{\mathrm{out}}^{\mathrm{zk}}}$ ,  $\mathbf{S}_{\mathcal{C}_{\mathrm{out}}^{\mathrm{zk}}}$ , and  $\mathbf{S}_{\mathcal{C}}$  respectively (similarly to the hybrid argument in Lemma 6.4). Each replacement changes  $\mathcal{D}$ 's view by at most  $\zeta$  in statistical distance, because each encoding is t-query zero-knowledge and  $\mathcal{D} \in \mathfrak{D}^{\leq t}$ . There are exactly  $3(\log \ell + 1) + (\log \ell + 1) + 1 = 4\log \ell + 5$  such oracles, so by a union bound the simulation error is at most  $(4\log \ell + 5) \cdot \zeta = O(\log \ell \cdot \zeta)$ .

{71}------------------------------------------------

# <span id="page-71-0"></span>12 High-distance codes from dispersers

We discuss the use of distance amplification via disperser graphs [ABNNR92; GRS12], and its use for certain efficiency gains.

## <span id="page-71-1"></span>12.1 Preliminaries

The following two definitions and subsequent proposition are from [GRS12].

<span id="page-71-3"></span>**Definition 12.1.** Let  $G = (L \cup R, E)$  be a bipartite graph with |L| = |R| = m. We say that G is a  $(\varepsilon, \gamma)$ -disperser if for every  $S \subseteq L$  with  $|S| \ge (1 - \varepsilon)m$  the neighborhood  $N(S) := \{j \in R \mid \exists i \in S : (i, j) \in E\}$  satisfies  $|N(S)| \ge (1 - \gamma)|R| = (1 - \gamma)m$ .

<span id="page-71-2"></span>**Definition 12.2.** Let  $C \subseteq \mathbb{F}^m$  be a code with minimum distance  $\delta(C) \geq 1-\varepsilon$ . Let  $G = (L \cup R, E)$  be a  $(\varepsilon, \gamma)$ -disperser with |L| = |R| = m, and assume G is right-regular of degree d. For every  $j \in R$ , fix an ordered list  $(i_{j,1}, i_{j,2}, \ldots, i_{j,d}) \in L^d$  of its d left incidences (allowing repetitions when G has multi-edges). Define the **amplification map**  $A_G \colon \mathbb{F}^m \to (\mathbb{F}^d)^m$  by  $(A_G(c))_j \coloneqq (c_{i_{j,1}}, \ldots, c_{i_{j,d}})$  for each  $j \in R$ .

<span id="page-71-4"></span>**Proposition 12.3** (Code–amplification lemma). Under the hypotheses of Definition 12.2, the amplified code  $G(\mathcal{C}) := \{A_G(c) \mid c \in \mathcal{C}\} \subseteq (\mathbb{F}^d)^m$  has minimum distance  $\delta(G(\mathcal{C})) \ge 1 - \gamma$ .

Proof. Take distinct  $c, c' \in \mathcal{C}$  and let  $S := \{i \in L \mid c_i \neq c_i'\}$ . Since  $\delta(\mathcal{C}) \geq 1 - \varepsilon$ , we have  $|S| \geq (1 - \varepsilon)m$  and hence  $|N(S)| \geq (1 - \gamma)m$  by Definition 12.1. For any  $j \in N(S)$  there exists  $k \in \{1, \ldots, d\}$  with  $i_{j,k} \in S$ , so the j-th symbols of  $A_G(c)$  and  $A_G(c')$  differ in coordinate k of the d-tuple. Therefore the number of right indices where the amplified codewords differ is at least  $|N(S)| \geq (1 - \gamma)m$ .

Below we show via a standard probabilistic method argument that random graphs are good dispersers (similar lemmas can be found in [Coh91; TUZ07]). The edges of the graph are sampled at random such that each right vertex has d left-neighbors. The following gives a lower bound on the required degree to guarantee the parameters of the disperser.

<span id="page-71-5"></span>**Proposition 12.4** (Probabilistic sampling of dispersers). Let L, R be vertex sets with |L| = |R| = m. Construct a random bipartite graph  $G = (L \cup R, E)$  (with multi-edges) by choosing, for each  $j \in R$ , a multiset of d neighbors in L uniformly at random with replacement (right-degree d). If

$$d > \frac{\lceil (1-\varepsilon)m \rceil \ln \frac{me}{\lceil (1-\varepsilon)m \rceil} + \lceil \gamma m \rceil \ln \frac{me}{\lceil \gamma m \rceil} - \ln \alpha}{\lceil \gamma m \rceil \cdot (-\ln \varepsilon)},$$

then, with probability at least  $1-\alpha$ , the graph G is a  $(\varepsilon,\gamma)$ -disperser.

*Proof.* G fails to be a  $(\varepsilon, \gamma)$ -disperser iff there exist  $S \subseteq L$  and  $T \subseteq R$  with  $|S| \ge \lceil (1 - \varepsilon)m \rceil$  and  $|T| \ge \lceil \gamma m \rceil$  such that every neighbor of each  $j \in T$  lies in  $L \setminus S$ , i.e.,  $N(S) \subseteq R \setminus T$  (equivalently,  $|N(S)| \le (1 - \gamma)m$ ). We may assume  $|S| = \lceil (1 - \varepsilon)m \rceil$  and  $|T| = \lceil \gamma m \rceil$ .

Fix S, T of sizes  $|S| = \lceil (1 - \varepsilon)m \rceil$  and  $|T| = \lceil \gamma m \rceil$ . Let  $p := \frac{m - |S|}{m} \le \varepsilon$  be the probability that a single random neighbor (one draw into L) lands in  $L \setminus S$ . For a fixed  $j \in T$ , the probability that all d independently sampled neighbors (with replacement) lie in  $L \setminus S$  is at most  $p^d \le \varepsilon^d$ .

{72}------------------------------------------------

Independence across *j* ∈ *T* yields Pr<sup>h</sup> all neighbors of all *j* ∈ *T* avoid *S* i ≤ *ε d* |*T*<sup>|</sup> = *ε d*⌈*γm*⌉ . By a union bound over all choices of *S* and *T* of these sizes, Pr[failure] ≤ *<sup>m</sup>* ⌈(1−*ε*)*m*⌉ *<sup>m</sup>* ⌈*γm*⌉ *ε d*⌈*γm*⌉ . Using *N k* ≤ *Ne k k* gives Pr[failure] ≤ *me* ⌈(1−*ε*)*m*⌉ ⌈(1−*ε*)*m*⌉ *me* ⌈*γm*⌉ ⌈*γm*⌉ *ε d*⌈*γm*⌉ . We conclude by making the right-hand side at most *α*.

**Use in our protocols.** Our protocols can be instantiated with any F-additive code with a zeroknowledge encoding, including distance-amplified codes. Although amplified codewords are over a larger alphabet, they remain F-additive: a vector in (F *d* ) *<sup>m</sup>* is equivalently a vector in F *<sup>d</sup>*·*m*. There are generic results on mutual correlated agreement for every F-additive code [\[GKL24;](#page-79-14) [GCXK25;](#page-79-15) [BCGM25\]](#page-77-13). Moreover, if C ⊆ F *<sup>m</sup>* has a *t*-query zero-knowledge encoding Enc<sup>C</sup> : F *<sup>ℓ</sup>* × F *<sup>r</sup>* → F *<sup>m</sup>*, then the amplified code *G*(C) = {*AG*(*c*) : *c* ∈ C} has a ⌊*t/d*⌋-query zero-knowledge encoding: each query to one amplified symbol reveals *d* base-code symbols. In sum, distance-amplified codes can be used in our constructions both as the zero-knowledge masking code Czk and as the main code C (for example in [Theorem 8.1\)](#page-48-2).

# <span id="page-72-0"></span>**12.2 Amplified Reed–Solomon code**

We describe how distance amplification can be used to improve the encoding time of codes, in particular Reed–Solomon codes, given any target distance. The distance of the codes used in the protocols plays a crucial role in the number of queries made (and hence the resulting argument size after compilation via Merkle commitments), and it is beneficial to have large distance (that is, to have a code with distance 1 − *γ* with small *γ*).

In general, distance amplification allows one to take any code, and obtain any distance using graphs with sufficiently large degrees (which correspond to sufficiently large alphabet for the output code). In the following, we highlight the resulting code from distance amplification on the Reed– Solomon code, which allows for faster encoding for a target distance.

<span id="page-72-1"></span>**Definition 12.5** (Amplified Reed–Solomon code)**.** *Fix c >* 1 *and a message length ℓ* ∈ N*. Set m* := ⌈*cℓ*⌉*, choose a field* F *with* |F| ≥ *m, and fix an evaluation domain* [*m*] ⊆ F *of size m. Let L* := [*m*] *and let G* = (*L*∪*R, E*) *be a right-regular bipartite graph of degree d that is a ε, γ -disperser with ε* := *ℓ*−1 *m . The code* ARS[F*, m, ℓ, G*] *is defined via this encoding: for a message* msg ∈ F *ℓ , set u* := EncRS(msg) ∈ RS[F*, m, ℓ*] *and output AG*(*u*) ∈ (F *d* ) |*R*| *(following [Definition 12.2\)](#page-71-2).*

The following claim follows by [Definition 12.2](#page-71-2) and [Proposition 12.3.](#page-71-4)

**Claim 12.6** (Parameters of the amplified RS code)**.** *The code in [Definition 12.5](#page-72-1) has alphabet* F *d , block length* |*R*| *(in alphabet symbols), and minimum distance at least* 1 − *γ.*

**Encoding time for two ways to reach relative distance 1−***γ***.** We want to encode a message msg ∈ F *ℓ* into a codeword with minimum distance at least 1 − *γ*. We compare two methods.

1. Plain Reed–Solomon: Choose an evaluation domain of size *<sup>ℓ</sup>*−<sup>1</sup> *γ* (and a field F large enough) so that *δ*(RS[F*, ℓ*−1 *γ , ℓ*]) ≥ 1 − *γ*. Encoding msg consists of (i) interpolating the degree-(*ℓ* − 1) polynomial from the *ℓ* message symbols, and (ii) evaluating it at all points of the chosen domain. Assuming FFT-style algorithms on [*m*], this requires at most

$$c_{\text{FFT}} \cdot \ell \cdot \log_2 \ell + c_{\text{FFT}} \cdot \left(\frac{\ell - 1}{\gamma}\right) \cdot \log_2 \left(\frac{\ell - 1}{\gamma}\right)$$

{73}------------------------------------------------

field operations. (If Enc<sub>RS</sub> treats the message as polynomial coefficients, omit the interpolation term and keep only the evaluation term.)

2. Amplified Reed–Solomon (with factor  $c := m/\ell$  and a right-regular disperser of degree d from the main bound): First encode  $\mathsf{msg} \in \mathbb{F}^\ell$  as a Reed–Solomon codeword on an evaluation domain of size  $c \cdot \ell$ ; this takes at most  $c_{\mathrm{FFT}} \cdot \ell \cdot \log_2 \ell + c_{\mathrm{FFT}} \cdot (c \cdot \ell) \cdot \log_2 (c \cdot \ell)$  field operations (again, drop the interpolation term if the message is given as coefficients). Then apply the amplification map  $A_G$  using the disperser graph; this step performs  $d \cdot |R|$  copy operations. If, as in Definition 12.5, one sets  $|R| = c \cdot \ell$ , this amplification step costs  $d \cdot c \cdot \ell$  symbols. Overall, the encoding time amounts to:

$$c_{\text{FFT}} \cdot \ell \cdot \log_2 \ell + c_{\text{FFT}} \cdot (c \cdot \ell) \cdot \log_2 (c \cdot \ell) + d \cdot c \cdot \ell$$
.

For the amplified option, we instantiate the disperser degree at the smallest integer strictly larger than the lower bound in Proposition 12.4 with  $n \coloneqq c \cdot \ell$ ,  $\varepsilon \coloneqq \frac{\ell-1}{c \cdot \ell}$ ,  $\gamma$  as in the target distance, and any fixed  $\alpha \in (0,1)$  for the construction's failure probability. Concretely, take d to be the least integer bigger than

$$\frac{(1-\varepsilon)n\cdot\ln\frac{ne}{(1-\varepsilon)n}+\gamma\,n\cdot\ln\frac{ne}{\gamma n}-\ln\alpha}{\gamma n\cdot(-\ln\varepsilon)}\quad\text{with}\quad n=c\cdot\ell,\ \varepsilon=\frac{\ell-1}{c\cdot\ell}.$$

With this choice, the amplified variant is faster than plain RS exactly when

$$\underbrace{c_{\mathrm{FFT}} \cdot (c \cdot \ell) \cdot \log_2(c \cdot \ell)}_{\mathrm{RS \ on \ size} \ c \cdot \ell} + \underbrace{d \cdot c \cdot \ell}_{\mathrm{amplification \ copies}} \leq \underbrace{c_{\mathrm{FFT}} \cdot \left(\frac{\ell - 1}{\gamma}\right) \cdot \log_2\left(\frac{\ell - 1}{\gamma}\right)}_{\mathrm{RS \ on \ size} \ (\ell - 1)/\gamma}.$$

Intuitively, when  $\gamma$  is small (demanding very large plain-RS block length), the right-hand side grows like  $\frac{\ell}{\gamma}\log_2\frac{\ell}{\gamma}$ , while the extra cost of amplification grows only linearly in  $\frac{\ell}{\gamma}$  through  $d\cdot c\cdot \ell$  (since

d from the lemma scales like  $\frac{(1-\frac{1}{c})\ln\frac{e}{1-\frac{1}{c}}+\gamma\ln\frac{e}{\gamma}}{\gamma\ln c}$  with  $n=c\cdot\ell$ ). Thus, for small  $\gamma$  and fixed c, the amplified RS method is cheaper.

Benefits of amplified RS. Amplified Reed-Solomon codes offer benefits in time and rate.

- No large FFTs. The FFT is for size  $\Theta(\ell)$  rather than  $\Theta(\ell/\gamma)$ . This improves encoding time, by trading a  $\Theta(\ell/\gamma)$ -size FFT for a  $\Theta(c\ell)$ -size FFT plus a  $\Theta(\ell/\gamma)$ -time pass to gather neighbors.
- Space complexity. The encoding space required for ARS is also decreased, if one has efficient access to the graph G. Even if one were to use in-place FFTs, the encoding space of the Reed–Solomon code at that distance is  $\ell/\gamma$ , while we can afford smaller FFTs requiring only  $O(\ell)$  size. Storing G in memory takes  $O(\frac{\ell}{\gamma}\log\ell)$ , making the space complexity slightly worse than in-place FFT implementations. Since strongly-explicit dispersers (where the neighborhood of a vertex could be computed in polylogarithmic time) with  $d = O(1/\gamma)$  are not known, one would have to store the sampled G in memory. An alternative approach would be to stream G, performing the alphabet aggregation for the neighbors of each vertex when their neighborhoods are streamed. This streaming option is much less clear for FFTs. Furthermore, in practice, one could use a PRG that, in principle, replaces the notion of a strongly explicit construction, as the neighbor set of each vertex could be sampled "on the fly" in a reproducible way.

{74}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-74-0"></span>12.3 Distance amplification and Merkle-compiled argument size

We discuss potential efficiency gains from distance amplification for argument size. While the proposed application here increases the encoding time (as opposed to the decrease in encoding time mentioned in the previous section), we show that it decreases overall argument size.

A case study. To investigate this point, we consider an example, which is based on the codeword batching protocol from [BCFW25]. In this protocol, the prover sends as an oracle a purported codeword, and the verifier makes t independent spot-checks to an oracle. When the protocol's soundness relies on the fact that two distinct codewords disagree on a  $1-\varepsilon$  fraction of coordinates (i.e., the underlying code has relative distance at least  $1-\varepsilon$ ), then each independent check catches a cheating strategy with probability at least  $1-\varepsilon$ , and hence the overall soundness error is at most  $\varepsilon^t$ . Thus, to drive the soundness error below  $2^{-\lambda}$ , it suffices to take  $t \ge \frac{\lambda}{-\log_2 \varepsilon}$ .

The cost of a Merkle commitment. Consider compiling such a protocol into an argument by committing to the codeword via a Merkle commitment whose leaves are the codeword symbols. Each verifier query is then answered by the leaf value and the authentication path, i.e., the sibling hashes along the root-to-leaf path. For a codeword of block length m (in symbols), the path has length  $\lceil \log_2 m \rceil$ . Hence, measured as "one field symbol plus one hash digest" units, the total proof communication contributed by these t oracle queries is  $t \cdot (1 + \lceil \log_2 m \rceil)$ , up to constant factors that depend only on the fixed hash function.

Merkle compilation cost for an amplified code. Now replace the underlying code  $\mathcal{C} \subseteq \mathbb{F}^m$  by its amplified version  $G(\mathcal{C}) \subseteq (\mathbb{F}^d)^m$  from Definition 12.2, where G is a right-regular  $(\varepsilon, \gamma)$ -disperser of degree d and |R| = m. By Proposition 12.3, the amplified code has relative distance at least  $1 - \gamma$ , so the same "independent spot-check" reasoning yields soundness error at most  $\gamma^t$ , and to achieve soundness error below  $2^{-\lambda}$  it suffices to take  $t \geq \frac{\lambda}{-\log_2 \gamma}$ . After compilation, each query now opens a single amplified symbol, i.e., an element of  $\mathbb{F}^d$ , together with an authentication path of length  $\lceil \log_2 m \rceil$ . Hence the argument size contributed by the t oracle queries becomes  $t \cdot \left(d + \lceil \log_2 m \rceil\right)$ .

In the disperser-based setting of Definitions 12.1 and 12.2, the Merkle depth terms coincide and amplification yields an improvement for this part of the argument when

<span id="page-74-1"></span>
$$d < \left(1 + \lceil \log_2 m \rceil\right) \cdot \frac{-\log_2 \gamma}{-\log_2 \varepsilon} - \lceil \log_2 m \rceil. \tag{10}$$

Instantiating d via Proposition 12.4. Fix any target  $\gamma \in (0,1)$  and any  $\alpha \in (0,1)$ . Let d be the least integer strictly larger than the lower bound from Proposition 12.4. To assess whether Equation 10 can hold for the best (i.e., smallest) degree guaranteed by Proposition 12.4, take d to be the least integer strictly larger than the bound of Proposition 12.4. Ignoring ceiling effects and the additive  $-\ln \alpha$  term (which is divided by  $\gamma m$  and is negligible once m is large), the bound has the following dominant form:

$$d \approx \frac{(1-\varepsilon) \ln \frac{e}{1-\varepsilon}}{\gamma \cdot (-\ln \varepsilon)} + \frac{\ln \frac{e}{\gamma}}{-\ln \varepsilon}.$$

In particular, for fixed  $\varepsilon$  this degree grows proportionally to  $1/\gamma$  as  $\gamma \to 0$ . Since the required number of openings decreases only as  $1/(-\log_2 \gamma)$ , very small  $\gamma$  forces d so large that Equation 10 eventually fails: in this regime, amplifying to extremely high relative distance makes each Merkle leaf so large that the reduction in the number of openings cannot compensate.

{75}------------------------------------------------

**Concrete parameters.** The condition in [Equation 10](#page-74-1) *can* hold for moderate target distances. For instance, take *m* = 220*, α* = 2−40*, λ* = 128*, ε* = 1 3 . Then the plain compilation needs *t* ≈ 81 openings and the query/opening contribution is approximately 81 ·(1 + 20) ≈ 1*.*70 · 10<sup>3</sup> units. If we target *γ* = 0*.*1 (relative distance 0*.*9), the smallest *d* certified by [Proposition 12.4](#page-71-5) is *d* = 12, and the amplified compilation needs *t* ≈ 39 openings, contributing about 39 · (12 + 20) ≈ 1*.*23 · 10<sup>3</sup> units, a reduction of roughly 27%. In contrast, if we target *γ* = 0*.*01 (relative distance 0*.*99), the same calculation yields *d* = 91 and *t* ≈ 19, giving about 19 · (91 + 20) ≈ 2*.*14 · 10<sup>3</sup> units, which is *larger* than plain. These examples illustrate that, under [Proposition 12.4,](#page-71-5) the Merkle-query part improves only when the requested distance increase is substantial but not so extreme that the required *d* becomes the dominant term in each opening.

We compare the prover's slowdown when using distance amplification. To compare *prover* runtime, we separate (a) committing to the oracle (encoding, plus building the Merkle commitment) from (b) answering the *t* openings. In the comparison we ignore the base encoding time of C (it is the same in both variants) and we count the total number of field-symbol copies/hashes.

As before, we assume that *m* = 2<sup>20</sup> so the Merkle commitment has 2 <sup>20</sup> leaves and 2 <sup>20</sup>−1 internal nodes. In the *plain* case, committing to C ⊆ F *<sup>m</sup>* requires hashing 2 <sup>20</sup> leaves (each containing one field symbol) and 2 <sup>20</sup>−1 internal nodes, i.e., about (220)+ (220−1) = 2*,*097*,*151 hash computations. Answering *t* ≈ 81 openings transmits 81 field symbols and 81 · 20 sibling hashes, i.e., 81 ·(1 + 20) = 1*,*701 units in the same model. Overall, the plain prover's Merkle-related work is ≈ 2*,*098*,*852 units.

For *γ* = 0*.*1, the probabilistic-method bound yields *d* = 12 and *t* ≈ 39. In the *amplified* case, beyond the same base encoding, the prover must (i) compute *A<sup>G</sup>* (about *dm* = 12·2 <sup>20</sup> = 12*,*582*,*912 symbol copies), and (ii) hash 2 <sup>20</sup> leaves each containing *d* = 12 field symbols (about another 12*,*582*,*912 symbol-hash units), plus the same 2 <sup>20</sup> −1 internal-node hashes. Answering the openings transmits 39 leaves of size 12 plus 39·20 sibling hashes, i.e., 39·(12+ 20) = 1*,*248 units. Altogether, this totals roughly 12*,*582*,*912 + 12*,*582*,*912 + (2<sup>20</sup> − 1) + 1*,*248 = 26*,*215*,*647 units, i.e., about a 12*.*5× increase over the plain prover time in this model. In other words, even though the *communication* in openings improves (roughly 27% above), the *prover* becomes slower because it must materialize and commit to *d*-times larger leaves.

{76}------------------------------------------------
